Governor-General

His Excellency the Right Honourable Sir Zelman Cowen, a Member of Her Majesty's Most Honourable Privy Council, Knight of the Order of Australia, Knight Grand Cross of the Most Distinguished Order of St Michael and St George, Knight Grand Cross of the Royal Victorian Order, Knight of the Most Venerable Order of the Hospital of St John of Jerusalem, one of Her Majesty's Counsel learned in the law, Governor-General of the Commonwealth of Australia and Commander-in-Chief of the Defence Force.

THE ACTS OF THE SESSION
(FIRST SESSION: SECOND PERIOD)
Advisory Council for Inter-government Relations Amendment Act 1981 (Act No.7 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Advisory Councilfor Inter-government Relations Act1976.
Airlines Agreement Act 1981 (Act No. 75 of 1981 )An Act to approve the execution of an agreement relating to air transport, and for purposes connected therewith.
Airlines Equipment Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 77 of 1981 ) - An Act to amend the Airlines Equipment Act 1958.
Airlines Equipment (Loan Guarantee) Act 1981 (Act No. 38 of 1981). An Act relating to guarantees of certain borrowings by a domestic airline.
Antarctic Marine Living Resources Conservation Act 1981 (Act -No. 30 of 1981 ) An Act relating to the conservation of marine living resources of the Antarctic and its surrounding seas.
Apple and Pear Export Underwriting Act 1981 (Act No. 14of 1981 )An Act relating to the underwriting of returns from the export from Australia of apples and pears.
Apple and Pear Stabilization Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 15 of 1981)An Act to amend the Apple and Pear Stabilization Act 1971, to repeal certain related Acts, and for other purposes.
Appropriation Act (No.3) 1980-81 (Act No. 55 of 1981 )An Act to appropriate a sum out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund, additional to the sum appropriated by the Appropriation Act (No. I) 1980-81, for the service of the year ending on 30 June 1981.
Appropriation Act (No.4) 1980-81 (Act No. 56 of 1981)An Act to appropriate a sum out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund, additional to the sum appropriated by the Appropriation Act (No.2) 1980-81 ,for certain expenditure in respect of the year ending on 30 June 1961.
Australian Apple and Pear Corporation Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 16 of 1981 ) - An Act to amend the Australian Apple and Pear Corporation Act 1973.
Australian Capital Territory Gaming and Liquor Authority Act 1981 (Act No. 116of 1981)An Act to establish a Gaming and Liquor Authority for the Australian Capital Territory and for related purposes.
Australian Federal Police Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 22 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Australian Federal Police Act1979.
Australian National Airlines Repeal Act 1981 (Act No. 78 of 1981)An Act to repeal the Australian National Airlines Actl945 and for related purposes.
Australian National University Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 106 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian National University Act 1946.
Australian Tourist Commission Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 27 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Australian Tourist Commission Act 1967.
Beef Industry (Incentive Payments) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 49 of 1981 ) - An Act to amend the Beef Industry (Incentive Payments) Act 1977.
Bounty (Non-adjustable Wrenches) Act 1981 (Act No. 18 of 1981)An Act to provide for the payment of bounty on the production of certain wrenches.
Bounty (Polyester-Cotton Yarn) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 17 of 1981 )An Act to amend the Bounty (Polyester-Cotton Yarn) Act 1978, and for related purposes.
Bounty (Printed Fabrics) Act 1981 (Act No. 102of 1981)An Act to provide for the payment of bounty on the production of certain printed fabrics.
Bounty (Textile Yarns) Act 1981 (Act No. 103 of 1981)An Act to provide for the payment of bounty on the production of certain textile yarns.
Broadcasting and Television Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 113 of 1981)An Act to amend the Broadcasting and Television Act 1942, and for related purposes.
Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Amendment Act 198) (Act No. 114 of 1981 )An Act to amend the Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Act 1964 to extend it to certain licences.
Census and Statistics Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 48 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Census and Statistics Act 1905.
Christmas Island Agreement Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 107 of 1981)- An Act relating to the Christmas Island Agreement.
Coal Excise Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 19 of 1981)An Act to amend the Coal Excise Act 1949, and for other purposes.
Commonwealth Banks Act 1981 (Act No. 29of 1981).An Act to amend the Commonwealth Banks Act 1959, and for related purposes.
Commonwealth Employees (Redeployment and Retirement) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 26 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Commonwealth Employees (Redeployment and Retirement) Act 1979.

THE ACTS OF THE SESSION-continued

Commonwealth Functions (Statutes Review) Act 1981 (Act No. 74 ofl981 ) An Act to implement certain changes in Commonwealth functions.

Commonwealth Legal Aid Commission Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 62 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Commonwealth Legal Aid Commission Act1977.

Commonwealth Teaching Service Amendment Act 1981 (Act No.5 of 1981)An Act to amend the Commonwealth Teaching Service Act 1972, and for purposes connected therewith.

Companies Act 1981 (Act No. 89 of 1981 ) An Act to make provision for the government of the Australian Capital Territory in relation to the formation of companies, the regulation of companies formed in that Territory, the registration in that Territory of certain other bodies and certain other matters.
Companies (Acquisition of Shares) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No.2 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Companies (Acquisition of Shares) Act 1980.

Companies (Acquisition of Shares) Amendment Act (No.2) 1981 (Act No. 94 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Companies (Acquisition of Shares) Act 1980.

Companies (Acquisition of Shares-Fees) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 95 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Companies (Acquisition of Shares-Fees) Act 1980.

Companies and Securities (Interpretation and Miscellaneous Provisions) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 4 of 1981)An Act to amend the Companies and Securities (Interpretation and Miscellaneous Provisions) Act 1980.

Companies and Securities (Interpretation and Miscellaneous Provisions) Amendment Act (No. 2) of 1981 (Act No. 98ofl981)An Act to amend the Companies and Securit-ies (Interpretation and Miscellaneous Provisions) Act 1980.

Companies (Fees) Actl981 (Act No. 90of 1981 ) An Act relating to fees payable for the purposes of the Companies Actl98l.

Companies (Miscellaneous Amendments) Act 1981 (Act No. 92 of 1981 ) -

.

An Act to make certain amendments consequent upon the enactment of the Companies Act 1981 and for other

purposes.

Companies (Transitional Provisions) Act 1981 (Act No. 91 of 1981 )An Act to enact transitional provisions consequent upon the enactment of the Companies Act 1981.

Complaints (Australian Federal Police) Act 1981 (Act No. 21 of 1981)An Act relating to complaints made in respect of members of the Australian Federal Police, and for related purposes.

Conciliation and Arbitration Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 71 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Conciliation and Arbitration Act 1904.

Crown Debts (Priority) Act 1981 (Act No. 93 of 1981)- An Act relating to the priority of Crown debts.

Currency Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. II of 1981 ) - An Act to amend the Currency Act 1965.

Customs Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 64of 1981 ) - An Act to amend the Customs Act 1901.

Customs Amendment (Securities) Act 1981 (Act No. 67 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Customs Actl901 in relation to securities.

Customs Amendment (Tenders) Act 1981 (Act No. 45 of 1981 ) An Act to amend the Customs Act 1901 to provide for tenders for rights to import goods at concessional rates of duly, and for related purposes.

Customs Securities (Penalties) Act 1981 (A.:t No. 46 of 1981 )An Act to provide for penalties for refusal or failure to give certain securities with respect to the importation into Australia of dutiable goods.

Customs Tariff Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 68 of 1981 ) - An Act to amend the Customs Tariff Act1966.

Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 66of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Customs Tari.ff(Anti-Dumping) Act 1975.

Customs TarilfValidation Act 1981 (Act No. 87 of 1981 ) An Act to provide for the validation of certain collections of duty of Customs.

Customs Undertakings (Penalties) Act 1981 (Act No. 47 of 1981)An Act to provide for penalties for breaches of undertakings with respect to the importation into Australia of dutiable goods.

Dairying Research Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 12 of 1981 ) An Act to amend the Dairying Research Act 1972, and for related purposes.

Designs Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 42 of 1981 ) An Act to amend the Designs Act 1906, and for related purposes.

4 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 3099

Thursday, 4 June 1981
Mr SPEAKER CRt Hon. Sir Billy Snedden) took the chair at 10.30 a.m., and read prayers.
PETITIONS The Clerk-Petitions have been lodged for presentation as follows and copies will be referred to the appropriate Ministers:
Trans-Australia Airlines
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of unqersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
Many Australians depend almost exclusively upon Trans-Australia Airlines for the provision of essential air services.
The Federal Liberal Party PQlicy objective of selling Trans-Australia Airlines within 4 years is contrary to the national interest.
Sale of Trans-Australia Airlines would inevitably result in-
(i) the creation of a private monopoly of major airline services in Australia;
(ii) higher domestic air fares.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the House of Representatives will reject outright any proposal to sell Trans-Australia Airlines.
by Dr Cass, Dr Everingham, Dr Jenkins, Mr Les Johnson,. Mr Charles Jones, Mr Kent, Dr Klugman, Mr Morris, Mr Mountford and Mr Scott.
Petitions received.
Tertiary Education
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. This petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth that:
(i) The financial situation of students enrolled at Australian post-secondary Institutions is already extremely difficult (as indicated in the Commonwealth Department of Education's 1979 survey of student incomes and expenditure) and any substantial increase in the financial commitments of students would gravely worsen their financial difficulties.
(ii) The imposition of post-secondary tuition fees of comparable levels to those imposed prior to 1974 would force many currently enrolled students to discontinue their studies and deter many persons willing and able at present to undertake post-secondary study from doing so.
(iii) Those students and potential students from social groups currently under-represented and disadvantaged in the post-secondary education system would be most severely affected by the n,introduction of fees for post-secondary education.
Your petitioners therefore pray that:
(i) Post-secondary tuition fees not be reintroduced for any courses or for any category of students.
(ii) The imposition of visa charges upon private overseas students be ceased and desisted from immediately.
And your,petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.

by Mr Duffy, Mr Jacobi, Mr Kent, MI' Milton and Mr Mountford.
Petitions received.

Tertiary Education
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in the Parliament assembled. This petition of the undersignd citizens of Australia respectfully showeth that:
1. the proposal to introduce fees for higher and second degrees will mean that access to post-graduate and recurrent education will be limited to the rich; Australian research and ability to cope with technological change will suffer; this proposal must be seen as a step in the direction of the introduction of fees for all tertiary students.
2. the proposed introduction of a loans scheme for students will mean graduating with a heavy debt or being denied a tertiary education if loans are refused because a student is seen as a 'poor risk' (particularly affecting women and those doing non-vocational courses); only the rich will be able to get a tertiary education without course restrictions and the burden of indebtedness.
3. in regard to the proposal to restrict eligibility for TEAS and the foreshadowing of reviewing TEAS levels only in the light of the availability of loans, it should be noted that any further downgrading of TEAS, which is already inadequate, can only further limit access to education.
4. the proposed restriction of adult secondary assistance to those needing qualifications for employment will severely restrict access to a full bigh school education and necessary bridging education to tertiary education for those who left school eady.

3100 REPRESENTATIVES 4 June 1981

Petitions

5. the proposal not to fund 30 colleges of advanced education if they are not rationalised is an attack on teacher education. This attack will create teacher shortages in the mid 1980s, restrict access to education now and endanger the future quality of teaching available to Australian children.
Your petitioners therefore pray that:
(i) post-secondary tuition fees not be introduced for any courses or for any category of students;
(ii) that the TEAS scheme be extended so as to more fully meet the real financial needs of students.
(iii) that a comprehensive loans scheme not be introduced. (iv) that there be no restriction of adult secondary
assistance. (v) that the proposal not to fund 30 CAEs unless they are
rationalised be rescinded.
And your petitioners as in duty bourtd will ever humbly pray.
by Mrs Darling.
Petition received.
Excise on Beer
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
1. The hotel industry in Australia is long-established and a valuable part of the community of this nation, providing places were the people may partake of the three basic needs of life: a place to eat, a place to drink, a place to sleep.
2. The hotel industry is accepted throughout the nation as occupying an important place in the social and economic activities of Australia.
3. The hotel industry is almost entirely within the private sector and makes a large contribution to the government in taxes of various kinds.
4. The hotel industry has a need to sustain an appropriate level of turnover and growth, to make profit, and to sustain and improve the level of employment.
S. The hotel industry of Australia is gravely concerned at published reports that the Government intends to increase excise charges on beer in the forthcoming Budget.
6. Sales of beer constitute the major source of income of most of the industry's 5811 hotels in Australia.
7. J'he very steep rises in beer excise in 1975 and 1978 severely retarded the growth of the Australian hotel industry.
8. Any further increases in excise on beer in the 1981 Budget would further retard the industry at a time when growth is essential to maintaining economic viability and the ability to serve the constantly growing needs of the community.
9. Such is its significance as a community requirement, beer occupies a place on the Consumer Price Index, and any alteration to its cost to the community would have an inflationary effect.
10. Any increase in excise would result in and force the hotel industry to pass on such increases to the consumer by way of higher prices for beer.
11. Higher excise would be only the starting point for an upward spiral in beer prices because of the presence of other factors, such as State Licence Fee taxes, which are directly linked to excise rates.
12. The hotel industry of Australia already is gravely handicapped by heavy excise and other tax imposts, its growth is at a standstill; and inevitably must decline if beer excise is increased.

Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that Honourable Members of the House of Representatives:
1. Recognise the importance and place of the Australian Hotel industry in the social and economic life of the nation.
2. Accept that the viability of the hotel industry is essential to the maintenance of employment opportunities and stability of the industry is essential to enable it to continue to provide for the hotel needs of the nation in this period of growing population and increasing tourism.
And further, that Honourable Members of the House of Representatives resist and reject any move to increase excise on beer.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Braithwaite and Mr Lusher.
Petitions received.

To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The petition of undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:

of the Department of Education. (d) Scale down participation in international education
activities, particularly UNESCO and OECD. (e) Further tighten already restrictive eligibility guidelines
for TEAS. (f) Supplement TEAS assistance by means of a loan
scheme. (g) Phase in tuition fees for students undertaking degrees
and diplomas subsequent to an initial tertiary qualification. (h) Reduce grants to universities and colleges to take accourtt of fee income, (i) compeletely abolish 30 colleges of advanced education, and (j) Scale down various engineering schools in colleges of advanced education around Australia:
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the Government will rescind these regressive decisions and. desist from obvious attempts to pave the way for a complete reintroduction of tertiary fees.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Cross and Mrs Darling.

Rosemount Repatriation Hospital
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The petition of undersigned Australian citizens showeth:
Rosemount Repatriation Hospital has been of inestimable value to eligible repatriation beneficiaries for many years, and the closure or dispersal of the facilities available at the hospital would be a retrograde step.
The proposed decision to close Rosemount Repatriation Hospital through active discouragement by departmental administrative officers of any further referrals by medical officers of patients for medically prescribed occupational therapy is objectionable.
Closure of Rosemount Repatriation Hospital would severely disadvantage veterans in Brisbane's northern suburbs, as well as their relatives and friends who would be required to travel a great distance in order to visit hospitalised veterans at either of the lwo alternative repatriation hospitals.
Closure of Rosemount repatriation hospital and transfer of patients to Greenslopes would greatly add to the administrative burden of that repatriation and general hospital.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the Government will reverse its announced decision to close the Rosemount Repatriation Hospital.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mrs Darling.

Privacy and Personal Information
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth that:
Paragraphs 107 and 108 of the Privacy and Personal Information discussion paper number 14, brought down by the Law Reform Commission in June 1980, undermines the family unit and the authority of parents, which is the Christian basis of our society, by proposing that 'between 12 and 16 years, a child should be entitled to object to disclosure of information to a parent'.
It is your petitioners sincere belief that:
(a) an adolescent between the ages of 12 and 16 is not at a mature enough stage of life to decide whether or not the disclosure of information to a parent will be in the best interests of the adolescent.
(b) as a record keeper has no responsibility for the child, and as his moral standards may be different to those of a parent, he should not be permitted to come between child and parent.
(c) a parent has a unique position which may enable him/her to perceive a situation that the record keeper does not. The parent must therefore be aware of the problem if remedial action is to be taken, and has the right to guide the child in the direction he/she feels fit.
Your petitioners therefore pray:
That the Members of the House of Representatives will reject the recommendation of the Law Reform Commission and find some other means to protect children from the excessive .abuse of some parents.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
byMrDobie.
Petition received.

(i) that according to the latest ADS bulletin on child care, based on a June 1980 survey, it is estimated that nearly 50,000 Australian children do not have access to child care centres and may be receiving less than adequate attention in the homes of people other than their parents;

(ii) that the Federal Government has neglected to regularly review and update funding arrangements since the family day care schemes were introduced in 1973;
(iii) that due to rising annual costs and the shortfall in Government funding since 1973, family day care centres are stretched to the limit in even maintaining their usual amount of child care.
Your Petitioners therefore humbly pray that the Federal Government will review and increase in real terms, its funding of family day care schemes in the 1981-82 Budget to permit improved child care and to extend this facility to those 50,000 Australian children who do not now have access to child care centres.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Humphreys.

AVIATION POLICY Mr CARLTON-My question is addressed to the Prime Minister. What steps will the Government be taking to increase competition and improve consumer choice within domestic aviation?

Mr MALCOLM FRASER-I thank the honourable gentleman for his reaffirmation of the bipartisan policy in relation to apartheid in sport. I think it is terribly important that this Parliament maintain that approach for reasons that all honourable members will understand. I would also hope that everyone in this House, because of a long and an historic association of many different kinds, would not want Australia to get into the business of boycotting meetings with New Zealand, no matter what other countries might do. If we had a situation in which most countries were not going to that meeting I suppose that
might throw into question the basis of the meeting itself and whether that meeting ought to be held.

Mr MALCOLM FRASER-On the assumption that the legislation is finally passed-there are certain important aspects of that legislation
which the honourable gentleman will be aware of and which the Government believes must stand as they have been introduced into the Parliamentthe first additional step to which we are com-
mitted is an examination of the practicality and, if practical, the means whereby some or all of the shares of Trans-Australia Airlines could be passed into public hands. The move towards making TAA a public company will, of course, proceed. In the initial instance it has been said that the Government will own all the shares, but that does not constitute a decision to sell or not to sell. We

3106 REPRESENTATIVES 4 June 1981

Questions Without Notice

will immediately establish an independent examination of the practicality in the Australian environment of selling some or all of the shares and, at the same time, if practical, the best means of moving into private ownership.
A deregulation inquiry will be established. We expect that inquiry to report to government in the fifth year. There is a deliberate reason for that time scale. It will take two and a half to three years before all the new aircraft are operating in Australia. If the advice we have had is correct, those aircraft, because of the differences between the airlines, will break parallel sheduling and establish a much greater degree of competition on Australia's air routes. A deregulation inquiry to make a proper judgment of circumstances in Australia will need to be able to make judgments based on the experience of those new equipments operating. In any case, the present agreement will run for five years with the possibility thereafter of a three-year period of notice being given, whether in the fifth, sixth or any other year. The report of the deregulation inquiry will plainly need to be published and debated. The Government will plainly need to state its views in relation to it. It will do so.
I cannot prejudge whether what will come out of that examination will be for or against deregulation. All I can say is that the Government will be publishing that report. We will be making a statement about it and will have to justify to our own parties and to Parliament whatever stance we take at that time. Clearly, if events were moving in that direction and if the substance of examinations were moving in that direction, it would enable us to give notice in the fifth year if we wanted to and, at the same time, to indicate what would be taking place once notice had been given and what legislative changes and whatever would be required. These are all firm commitments of the Government. In addition, the present legislation establishes an air fares committee. On the deregulation inquiry and in the examination of matters relating to TAA as a public corporation we clearly need people of fair and independent mind who can make their own judgments about what will be in the best interests of Australia's travelling public.
TAX CONCESSIONS FOR THE AUSTRALIAN FILM INDUSTRY Mr HAYDEN-I refer to reports of the Treasurer's latest and embarrassing contortion in relation to tax concessions for the Australian film industry whereby apparently the Government will now' honour its initial undertaking to the industry. that the total cost of this undertaking is nearly $120m? That was the intent of the decision that I announced yesterday. Against that background, T stress that the complaints of some people in the film industry are in fact without substance. It is one of the matters that has cast doubts on the credibility of wealthier countries about their real intentions in these matters.
I think it is worth noting that because of financial stringency and even though, by and large, the real value of the aid dollar has been maintained over the past several years, as a proportion of GDP in 1974-75, when the level was 0.56 per cent, the figure has fallen to an estimated level of 0.42 per cent or 0.43 per cent for 1980-81. Whilst by and large the actual real value of the aid vote has been maintained, there has been a relative fall in the proportion of gross domestic product that Australia provides. This trend becomes more marked when our economy is growing strongly. We know the growth figures for the six months to December. We know the estimates of the Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development, which place Australia above all other OECD countries in terms of estimated growth throughout this year. There are additional reasons, perhaps, why Australia should seek to set some kind of example in this area.

OVERSEAS AID
Dr EDWARDS-l ask the Prime Minister: What is the Government's policy as to overseas aid? Does the Government have a particular percentage of gross domestic product as a target for overseas aid?
Mr MALCOLM FRASER-The Government has had, and Australia has had, a best endeavours policy of reaching 0.7 per cent of gross domestic

We need to understand that there are many reasons for aid being provided. There are moral and humanitarian reasons which for many people are sufficient in themselves. There is also, as 1 believe, a very substantial self-interest reason in helping to promote higher standards of living in many countries-self-interest in terms of promotion of peace and stability but also in terms of encouraging greater growth of economic markets. As a result of the removal of Australia's access to the European Economic Community, if it were not for the newly industrialising countries of Asia and the Pacific we would have been in great difficulty in terms of our own trade. It is those newly industrialising countries that have provided great opportunities for trade. As there is greater economic advancement in more of the developing countries, so those opportunities will be broadened. I believe that there are economic benefits for everyone in relation to that.
There was a report in one of the journals this morning which was, in fact, jumping far ahead of itself. The Minister for Foreign Affairs will have his aid submissions dealt with in the Budget context. I expect that any decisions being made about aid would be largely Budget decisions, although I

3108 REPRESENTATIVES 4 June 1981

Questions Without Notice

do not rule out the prospect of some aspects of decisions being made in relation to the Commonwealth Heads of Government meeting in October. Basically, these are Budget matters. They need to be looked at against the total overall context of Australia's requirements and Australia's interests. At the same time, I think we need to understand that this is a serious matter, that the North-South debate has been stalled, and that there is a very real need to promote greater opportunities amongst developing nations, and especially amongst the least developed nations. There is an obligation on all countries, especially relatively wealthy countries such as Australia, not just on a moral and humanitarian basis but also on a self interest basis. I hope that this might be another matter on which the Australian Labor Party will agree with what I have been saying.

DEPARTMENT OF THE CAPITAL TERRITORY
Mr SAINSBURY-Can the Minister for the Capital Territory inform the House who will be the next permanent head of the Department of the Capital Territory? Further, will the Minister and the new permanent head ensure that the interests of Queanbeyan are fully considered in all policy decisions affecting the Australian Capital Territory?
Mr HODGMAN-I thank the honourable member for Eden-Monaro for the question. It gives me very great pleasure to inform the House, the people of the Australian Capital Territory and, indeed, the people of Australia that the next permanent head of the Department of the Capital Territory will be Mr Tony Blunn. He will succeed that very great Australian and most distinguished Commonwealth public servant, Mr Lauric Daniels, C.B., OBE., who retires on 10 August next. I have every confidence and, I believe, this would be accepted on all sides of politics, that Mr Blunn will be a most worthy successor to Mr Daniels.
In response to the second part of the honourable member's question, I draw attention to the fact that I do recognise, as a result of strong representations by the honourable member, that the interests of Queanbeyan are very closely interrelated to the interests of the Australian Capital Territory. A very substantial number of people reside in Queanbeyan but work in the national capital. It is impossible to consider the future of the Australian Capital Territory without having regard to the interests of Queanbeyan. The interests are identical economically, socially and in many other ways. I am somewhat surprised that members of the Australian Labor Party do not regard this matter seriously. I assure them that I do. I assure the honourable member for Eden.. Monaro, the Mayor of Queanbeyan and others living in that area that while I have had the honour to be the Minister for the Capital Territory I have accepted the point that, economically, what is good for Canberra will be good for Queanbeyan. In every decision that I am called upon to make relating to the Australian Capital Territory I will respond to representations which are made by the honourable member for Eden-Monaro, who has been a most assiduous worker for the welfare of the citizens of the City of Queanbeyan.

MI' SPEAKER-The Minister will end his answer.
MI' HODGMAN-I only regret that the Labor Party does not regard Queanbeyan as importantly as do the honourable member and I.

Questions Without Notice

BEEF SALES TO THE REPUBLIC OF KOREA
Mr KATTER-Can the Minster for Trade and Resources inform the House of the latest developments in the Korean beef market and the extent to which Australian beef exporters have been successful in gaining re-entry to the Korean market?
Mr ANTHONY-I am very pleased to be able to say that the Republic of Korea has re-entered the Australian meat trade after being out of it for the last two years. Korea was a significant market in the 1976, 1977 and 1978 period but because of domestic meat marketing problems and a very tight balance of payments situation Korea cut out any importation of meat from Australia. In March this year it issued tenders for meat and an Australian company won the contract for 4,500 tonnes. Korea issued another tender in April and Australia won a contract for a further 4,000 tonnes. A tender is now out for another 4,000 tonnes and I am very hopeful that the Australian industry will be able to get the contract. The Government, of course, was working closely with the Korean Government during the period that we were not able to get access. It is now a very valuable market because the international market for meat is soft; the American market has been very slack, and the Japanese market also has been cutting back on the importation of chilled meat. This additional sale to Korea is very welcome.
EL SALVADOR Mr HOLDING-Does the Minister for Foreign Affairs still maintain the correctness of his Government's policy of unequivocal support for President Reagan's policy of providing armaments and military advisers to the present military junta in El Salvador? Is he not concerned that Australia is one of the few Western nations publicly to endorse all aspects of President Reagan's policy? Does he believe that Australia's policy should be modified at least to give support to the recommendations of the United States Senate Foreign Relations Committee and the United States House of Representatives Foreign Affairs Committee, which latter Committee voted 11 to one to cut off United States aid and to withdraw United States advisers from El Salvador unless President Duarte certifies in writing that his Government can control its armed forces, observes basic human rights and is making progress on political reform? If Australia's present policy in respect of United States intervention in El Salvador is not modified, is he not concerned that Australia's general standing on human rights issues will be seriously undermined both in the United Nations and in other world assemblies?

Mr STREET-The first point I want to make is that Australia has made known its opposition to violations of human rights in El Salvador to the United Nations and, in particular, the United Nations Commission for Human Rights. As long as the present situation exists we will continue to make our views known. The second point I want to make is that the honourable gentleman is not correct in referring to Australia's unequivocal support for the policies of the United States of America in El Salvador. What we have said is that we understand completely the view of the United States Administration that externally supplied military aid should be cut off; that it is a source of instability in Central America. We have said further that we believe the problems of El Salvador have to be attacked at two levels. One is to cut off supplies of foreign-supplied military aid, to which I have referred, and the other is to support social and economic reform. So long as a situation of terrorism and violence prevails, it is extremely difficult to get the social and economic reform that is needed.
It was made clear to me in my discussions with Vice-President Bush in Washington a few months ago that the United States Administration shares the view that the problem has to be tackled at two levels. In fact, quite recently the United States Administration made a gift of civil aid to El Salvador of about $USI26m. Until the acts of terrorism and violence are contained it will be very difficult to make the social and economic progress we would all wish to see in that violence-ravaged country. The United States Government by its recent gift of civil aid, clearly recognises that there is a dual problem.

Certainly it has been an impediment in the development of that market, particularly for the cheaper casket and pack wines. The increases in

4 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 3111

duty levels are of several hundred per cent, and that will make the sale of that wine very difficult. However, New Zealand does argue strongly that it has increased the import licences from $6m to $10m a year, which will mean that the Australian wine industry will be able to penetrate the market with the higher quality wines and should make more extensive sales in terms of value than had previously been the case. However, because of the irritation and because of the diffiqulties that have been created, I have arranged for the two wine industry bodies to meet and discuss the issue to see whether they cannot work out what might be a suitable accommodation of both industries' points of view.
RUSSIAN CRUISE VESSELS
Mr MOUNTFORD~I draw the Prime Minister's attention to the various bans and limitations that were placed on shipping, trade and personal exchange visits with the Soviet Union following that nation's invasion of Afghanistan some 18 months ago. One of the bans affected the operation of Russian cruise ships out of Australian ports, which until that time had been providing reasonably priced cruises through the CTC travel organisation for Australian travellers. In view of the fact that other shipping and trade bans imposed on the Soviet Union at that time have been lifted, will the Government now give approval for Soviet cruise ships to operate again from Australian ports?
Mr MALCOLM FRASER-The decisions the Government took in relation to this matter followed decisions taken in the United States and Canada which made it quite plain that the continuation of some aspects of Australia's earlier position were no longer tenable. Going beyond that, no further decisions remain; the position is as it was. It is worth reminding the House that the Soviet invasion and occupation of Afghanistan continues. There is still a very great degree of continued fighting, with the Soviet Union having great difficulty establishing its own order in that area. The decisions taken were taken as a consequence of decisions in Canada and the United States of America which would have made quite futile the maintenance of Australia's earlier position.
AUSTRALIAN WOOL TESTING AUTHORITY
Mr BUNGEY~I direct my question to the Acting Minister for Primary Industry. What additional costs are likely to result firstly to the Government, secondly to the Australian Wool Corporation, thirdly to wool growers and fourthly

to wool users because of the disposal of the Australian Wool Testing Authority? In particular, will the Minister comment on the recent statement by Mr David Asimus, the Chairman of the Australian Wool Corporation, that all alternatives would result in an increase in costs to the industry with no discernible benefit to it?
Mr SINCLAIR-My advice, and certainly I have had only telephone discussions with the Chairman of the Australian Wool Corporation, is that the basis of sale of the Australian Wool Testing Authority and its intended operation will result in very little net increase, if any, in costs to growers. The terms and conditions of the sale and the arrangements by which the body to continue the responsibilities of the Australian Wool Testing Authority are to be constituted are still to be concluded. Indeed, the Government's objective, as a result of discussions that have been held between my colleague the Minister for Primary Industry and, I believe, the Prime Minister with representatives of the Australian Wool Council, resulted in an agreement that there would be consultations with the industry throughout the period to determine just what alternative body would be constituted.
The Government's objective is to ensure that what is essentially a body operating in a market place should do just that, that funds presently used for that purpose through the Australian Wool Corporation should, if possible, be freed for growers' benefit within the Corporation and that the private enterprise operation which in many respects is the responsibility of the Australian Wool Testing Authority be reconstituted in such a way that without net additional cost the growers can receive the same service and the body itself can be constituted independently altogether from the Australian Wool Corporation but with an association with it.
I believe the discussions have not yet reached the point where the final structure of that body can be defined, but I have little doubt that the net benefit will be that the body itself will certainly retain the stature, reputation and service now maintained by the Australian Wool Testing Authority but with little additional cost, if any, to the Australian wool growing community.

DEPARTMENT OF PARLIAMENTARY LIBRARY
M1' SPEAKER-For the information of honourable members. I present the third annual report of the Department of the Parliamentary Library for the calendar year 1980. The report makes it clear that this Parliament has library and research services which serve it in an excellent manner and which rank amongst the best of their kind to be found in any parliament. Much attention is given by the Library to forward planning,

taking into account the need of modern democratic institutions for reliable, accurate and prompt information services, free of bias or partisan views and accessible beyond the limits of this or the proposed new building or even this city. Recently, other parliamentary libraries have started to explore with our Library ways in which they could share resources through computerised net .. work systems. Such an arrangement would benefit all participants and greatly enhance the amount of relevant information available. I am sure that all honourable members would wish me to thank the Library Committee for its services and progressive approach and to congratulate the Parliamentary Library stair on their achievements during the past year.
COMMONWEALTH EDUCATION POLICY AND FINANCIAL GUIDELINES
TO THE COMMONWEALTH EDUCATION COMMISSIONS
Ministerial Statement
Mr FIFE (Farrer-Minister for Education)- by leave-The purpose of this statement is to announce the Government's decisions on guidelines for its major education programs in 1982 and, as appropriate for the triennium 1982-84, against the background of the recent triennial reports of the Tertiary Education Commission and the School Commission and of the Government's decisions on the Review of Commonwealth Functions. In that context, we have given further consideration to the report of the National Inquiry into Teacher Education and have undertaken a review of education and training opportunities for youth.
The overall financial outcome for 1982 for Commonwealth programs administered by the education commissions is that total spending will be maintained at the 198 t level in real terms with the cost of election initiatives provided as an addition. There will, however, be some reallocation of resources between areas reflecting the Commonwealth's approach to priorities. The total sums to be made available for 1982, in outturn prices, will be $1,867.6m for tertiary programs and $1,003.6m for schools programs-an overall total of $2,871.2m and an increase over 1981 of 1.5 per cent in real terms. The present practice under which programs are supplemented retrospectively for cost increases will be changed so that a prospective allowance is built into the figures in advance to take account of cost increases.
Commonwealth's Role in Education
The States have the primary responsibility for the administration and delivery of educational

3114 REPRESENTATIVES 4 June 1981

Commonwealth Education Policy Guidelines

services, particularly at the schools level. The Commonwealth provides full funding for universities and colleges of advanced education and supplementary funds for quality improvement, for general development and for priority areas in schools and colleges of technical and further education. The Commonwealth believes it has a particular role in identifying and bringing resources to bear on educational issues of national importance. We have been reviewing our priorities across the range of tertiary and schools programs and have taken some decisions on the reallocation of resources. As set out in our 1980 election policy, we will continue our extensive support for education with increased emphasis on vocational and technical training. We will give greater priority to the school to work transition program, to the education of migrants, and to the handicapped.
Review of Commonwealth Functions
In the application of its policy of maximum expenditure restraint, the Government has already announced a number of decisions on education arising from the Review of Commonwealth FUnctions, and action to put these into effect is proceeding. Tuition fees will not be reintroduced for the majority of students-those taking first qualifications or recognised combined degrees and diplomas-in universities and colleges of advanced education. Fees will apply for second and higher degree students; this is in practice a very limited basis and reflects the Government's view that the principle of free tertiary education should be restricted to a basic qualification. Beyond that, those who wish to further their tertiary studies should pay fees, which in any event will represent only a minor proportion of the full costs of that tertiary study. The arrangements will be phased in, so that students already on course will be exempt. The Government plans a new initiative to assist tertiary students; it proposes to introduce, from the beginning of 1982, a loans scheme which will be in addition to the Tertiary Education Assistance Scheme and not in substitution for it. Details of both these decisions will be announced as soon as possible.
The Curriculum Development Centre is to be abolished unless State governments agree to meet 50 per cent of the cost of the Centre's operations. I have sought the views of State education Ministers, and several have requested further clarification of what is proposed. I have proposed a meeting of officers to discuss funding, management and other implications if the CDC were to continue as a joint responsibility of the Commonwealth, the States and the Northern Territory. Whatever the

final decision on the future of the CDC, I want to reiterate that the Commonwealth will continue to be prepared to share in the costs of curriculum projects sponsored by the Australian Education Council and in any projects it assesses as being of national significance that are not so regarded by the AEC. Whilst the Government has decided to abolish the Education Research and Development Committee and its special research program, some funds for education research will be available under co-ordinated arrangements across the education portfolio. Projects currently supported under ERDC grants will be maintained until the end of 1981 and further funding will be available to meet contractual obligations during a phasing-out period.
The Government also announced in the context of its Review of Commonwealth Functions that it was concerned at the proliferation of separate tertiary institutions, and it has taken prompt action to provide for more efficient use of resources. Both this and the Government's decision to reduce the involvement of the Commonwealth Schools Commission in program administration will be discussed later in this statement. Consistent with the Government's approach to seek greater identification of its very considerable support for education, we have decided to include the word 'Commonwealth' in the titles of the Schools Commission and the Tertiary Education Commission. This change is provided for in the Statute Law Revision Bill 1981 recently introduced.
Review of Opportunities fol' Youth ami Transition Program
The review of education and training opportunities for young peoplc, announced on 16 January 1981 by the then Minister for Employment and Youth Affairs and myself, has been considered by the Government in the context of deciding on guidelines for the education commissions for 1982 programs. The review was undertaken because of concern to find alternative approaches for those young people who leave school ill prepared for work and with inadequate educational standards. The Schools Commission in its recent report 'Schooling for 15 and 16 Year 0lds' concluded that 'in their general orientation, most schools lag in their adjustment to the needs of the full range of students'. The report goes on to comment that secondary schools seem preoccupied with preparation for tertiary study.
Against this background, the Government wishes to accelerate the development of appropriate transition courses in secondary schools, with emphasis on basic changes in curricula relevant particularly to the needs of low achievers. We will

Commonwealth Education Policy Guidelines

4 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 3115

be pursuing this through the Australian Education Council. With a view to encouraging more early school leavers to continue their education, we will also promote in TAFE colleges the development of courses to meet a wider range of vocational needs. In addition to the trades area, it is particularly important to expand vocational edlJcation relevant to occupations which require skill but are not apprenticeable trades. These two initiatives together represent the first step in improving the prospects of young people 'at risk', including both those in school who are tempted to leave early and those who have already tried unsuccessfully to obtain work.
The review of education and training opportunities was undertaken against the background of the announcement by the Commonwealth in November 1979 of the introduction of the school to work transition program for young people aged 15 to 19 who were likely to be 'at risk' in the tran-
sition process. At that time the Commonwealth and States had agreed that concerted action should be taken from both quarters. The Commonwealth undertook to provide a total of $150m in real terms over five years from 1980 for the program and asked the States to match this expenditure dollar for dollar for government schools and TAFE colleges from 1982 onwards. This was designed to provide a total additional effort costing $250m over the five-year period. The program commenced in 1980, and the Commonwealth continued it in 1981 without a State contribution for that year. Although the program is still being developed, all State Ministers have reported an excellent response to it. A large number of transition courses and activities have been initiated or expanded in government and non-government secondary schools and in TAFE
colleges:
It is expected that approximately 5,900 young people will undertake full time TAFE courses of up to one year's duration under the program in 1981. At least 8,000 students in secondary schools are undertaking alternative courses receiving significant financial support under the program. In addition, many thousands of young people are being assisted through improvement and expansion of such activities as vocational guidance and counselling services, work experience schemes, link courses and alternative course curriculum development. However, to date the States have not been prepared to indicate their readiness to make additional contributions to match the Commonwealth's expenditure, which was $15.7m in 1980 and is estimated at $25m in 1981 for govern-
ment schools and TAFE. This has meant that the transition program has not helped as many young

people as the Government envisaged when it was introduced.
Accordingly, we feel compelled to take special action to increase significantly the resources devoted to transition activities. Up to $75.4m wil1 now be allocated in 1982. Specifically, we have decided to earmark up to $27m within Commonwealth general recurrent grants to the States for government schools in 1982 for expenditure on the transition program in secondary schools. The Commonwealth's direct expenditure of $27m on the transition program in 1982 will then be directed to TAFE. If, however, the States increase their own expenditure on transition, this will be offset against the sum to be earmarked. Whatever the States decide, the total recurrent expenditure on transition in the government sector in the States and the Northern Territory in 1982 will be brought up to $54m, the level originally envisaged in the Commonwealth's November 1979 statement. Non-government schools will continue to receive grants from the Commonwealth under the program as originally intended. These will amount to $2.4m in 1982.
Within the total of $27m to be earmarked from the Commonwealth Schools Commission general recurrent program for expenditure on transition activities, a component of about $9m will be set aside for cash grants to government secondary schools boards or committees at the rate of $10 per pupil-$500 minimum per school. These funds will be able to be spent on a wide range of activities in the transition area subject to guidelines to be developed in consultation with the States. The Government's purpose here is to ensure that all government secondary schools have an opportunity to participate in the transition program and to encourage school communities to take an active interest in the program. Similar pr-)visions for the Australian Capital Territory will be considered in the Budget context.
As a further indication of the Commonwealth's commitment to this priority area, we propose to earmark 15 per cent, about $19m, of the government schools capital program for equipment and facilities for transition education in secondary schools in 1982. We shall also ask the Commonwealth Schools Commission to consult with nongovernment schools and to consider ways, within current allocations and commitments, of ensuring that the activities of these schools in the transition area are given high priority. The net effect of these measures will be to more than double the resources to be devoted to the transition program in 1982 as compared with 1981. The capacity of TAFE and of secondary schools to admit students to transition courses will be very greatly enlarged

3116 REPRESENTATIVES 4 June 1981

Commonwealth Education Policy Guidelines

as was foreshadowed in the 16 January announcement of the review of education and training opportunities.
Through these changes the Commonwealth hopes to see more young people who may be at risk in the transition process staying on to complete their secondary education and/or pursuing further study in TAFE. In all of this we are endeavouring to assist the education system to do a better job in serving the needs of all children and in maintaining their interest in formal education. The objective is to improve the basic skills and employment prospects of young people as they leave the education system, particularly in the face of a decline in the number of unskilled jobs in the community. A second step in alleviating the problems faced by young people is to ensure that there is an adequate system of financial support for those undertaking education and training.
The Government is also very concerned about young people who have already left school and are having difficulty in finding work. Improved training subsidies were introduced following the announcement of the Government's commitment to youth policy in October 1980. These have made an immediate impact in meeting the needs of the longer term young unemployed. The Government will continue to pursue'the program and promote it vigorously so that as many young people as possible can be assisted. The Commonwealth has recently introduced a new transition allowance-at the level of unemployment benefit plus $6 per we~k-to encourage young people who have been,unemployed for some time to take up transition or other TAFE courses. This allowance has been paid from the beginning of the current academic year.
Among th~ young unemployed there are those who are severely disadvantaged and for whom f\,lrther education or work experience WQuid be insufficient. These young people may need other forms of assistance. The reasons for their disadvantage are numerous, but generally include a range of physical, mental or social handicaps that combine to deny them the ability to compete on the open labour market. My colleagues, the Minister for Social Security (Senator Chaney) and the Minister for Employment and Youth Affairs (Mr N. A. Brown) will be giving further consideration to this issue. The Government is particularly concerned to ensure the adequacy of allowances for low income families and other disadvantaged groups. Young people should not be forced into unemployment benefit, or prematurely into the work force, because their families are unable to support them. We will be giving further consideration to the range of allowances for young people

in the Budget context. The Government also has under review the payment of unemployment benefit to 16 and 17-year-olds. The Government restates its position that no adverse changes to unemployment benefit will be made unless better alternatives are available to the young people affected and the Government is satisfied that the interests of low income families and other disadvantaged groups would be protected.
The Government will be giving further cohsideration to the development of more comprehensive arrangements for young people as they make the transition from school to work or further education and training. Our objective is to bring about a substantial shift of emphasis within schools. We will co-operate with the States, through the Australian Education Council, in efforts in this direction and will invite the Commonwealth Schools Commission and the Commonwealth Tertiary Education Commission to share in this endeavour. The Government has noted and will give careful consideration to the report which has just been handed down by the Senate Standing Committee on Education and the Arts entitled 'Preparation for the Work Force'.
National Inquiry into Teacher Education
As part of its review of policy directions the Government has consulted the States on the report of the' national inquiry into teacher education. The Australian Education Council has accepted that, since the quality of school education is in turn dependent on the quality of teachers, teacher development should be a continuous process of pre-service education, induction and inservice activities. Implementation of most of the report's recommendations will be the responsibility of employing authorities and tertiary institutions. The Commonwealth has examined its direct finimcial contributions in these areas as part of its consideration of the forward programs for the education commissions. Specifically, the Commonwealth will continue to provide for professional development programs for teachers in 1982, including grants to education centres within the Schools Commission's program, at the level of $17.5m in 1982. The Commonwealth does, however, see in-service education as the responsibility of the employing authorities.
Programs Administered by tbe Education Commissions
The guidelines set out the total funding levels to be made available for the programs administered by the Commonwealth education commissions, which will report back in the forthcoming Budget sittings of Parliament on the detailed allocation of

Cost Adjustment Procedures

commissions. From the beginning of 1982 a prospective allowance will be made to take account of forecast cost increases in substitution for the previous arrangement whereby costs have been adjusted on a retrospective basis. In adopting this new arrangement, the Government has taken a more rigorous approach towards cost adjustment which in the past has provided additional funds automatically and has had a tendency to insulate educational institutions from the effects of inflation and from the need to contain excessive wage claims. It is designed also to encourage greater efficiency in institutions. The new arrangements will reduce the need for frequent amendments to legislation which were necessary under the previous retrospective cost adjustment arrangements. The following amounts, which include a prospective allowance for cost adjustment, will be provided in 1982, in comparison with 1981. Leave granted.

The Government has noted the Commission's recommendation that additional funds be provided in 1983 and 1984 to higher education institutions for superannuation purposes. This matter requires special consideration and is being examined separately by the Government. The Government has confirmed its commitment to the triennial principle which it reintroduced at the beginning of the present triennium. Recurrent programs for universities and colleges of advanced education will be funded on a fixed triennial basis for the 1982-84 triennium so that base recurrent programs will be maintained at the 1982 level. Of this amount, $21.4m represents the final instalment under the Government's original supplementary '$50m' grant over the period 1979 to 1982. Although the total allocation is slightly less than in 1981 it will enable expenditure of about $37m on new projects in 1982. The cost of the election commitments in the tertiary area in 1982 is being provided as an addition to the overall 1981 level of expenditure in real terms. However, there has been a reassessment of priorities within the program. Additional funds are to be made available in 1982 for general and special purpose recurrent grants and for equipment in the TAFE sector and also for equipment and certain non-government teachers colleges and the Australian Maritime College in the higher education sector. This has required a reduction in capital allocations for both sectors and in the basic recurrent grants for universities and colleges of advanced education.

In the Review of Commonwealth Functions, we decided that both government and nongovernment school building projects being supported with Commonwealth funds should not exceed certain standards developed in a cooperative Commonwealth-State school building standards study. The Commonwealth Schools Commission is being asked to give effect to this decision from the beginning of 1982. In the funding of government schools, the Commonwealth will continue to determine its contribution in the Budget context each year and will not be tying its grants to a fixed standard of services, as proposed by the Commission. It has maintained the real level of these grants for 1982 notwithstanding the expected further decline in enrolments. 3120 REPRESENTATIVES 4 June 1981

Commonwealth Education Policy Guidelines

grants and average government schools standard costs and to compress the existing six subsidy levels to three. The latter will be achieved by combining the existing levels 1 to 3 into a new group 1, levels 4 and 5 into a new group 2, and level 6 will become the new group 3. The 1982 per capita rates for the new group 1, 2 and 3 schools will be 20 per cent, 30 per cent, and 38 per cent respectively of average government schools standard costs. Per capita subsidies for group 3 schools will be raised to 40 per cent of average government schools costs in 1983. The estimated additional cost of these provisions will be $25.9m in 1982, subject to actual enrolments. The Government has accepted a number of the Commonwealth Schools Commission's recommendations for changes in the per capita grants scheme for nongovernment schools to the extent that these do not conflict with the Government's basic policies. These are detailed in the attached guidelines.
In recognition of the fact that some nonsystemic non-government schools would be reclassified to a lower subsidy level as a result of the approved changes, the Minister, on the advice of the Schools Commission and its State planning and finance committees, will permit those schools to remain at a higher subsidy level for up to two years until they adapt their resources to the new arrangements. The Government will review any cases where this is not achievable within two years. The Commission, through its State planning and finance committees, is to give consideration to school income as well as expenditure in the assessment of subsidy levels but the assessment will continue to be based primarily on recurrent expenditure. Principles to be used for this change will be approved by the Minister following consultation by the Commission with nongovernment school authorities. Any subsequent changes in schools' subsidy levels will continue to be subject to ministerial approval following advice from the Commission.
The present provisions for new non-systemic schools will be varied so that they receive supplementary establishment grants over and above the general recurrent subsidy for the first four years at the rates of 10 per cent, 7.5 per cent,S per cent and 2.5 per cent respectively of government schools' standard costs. The Government has not accepted the proposal to base its grants on a fixed standard based on the Commission's proposed 'basket of services '. This would represent a fundamental departure from the Government's long standing policy of linking its grants to nongovernment schools to movements in average government school running costs. Similarly the proposal to assess non-government school systems

for funding purposes by aggregating individual schools' entitlements rather than by averaging the system as a whole, is not accepted.
The Government is also anxious to ensure that non-government schools continue to adapt their facilities to the special transition needs of students who are in secondary schools. The Commission is asked to give particular consideration to transition needs in developing the capital program for non-government schools. In addition, the Government expects that non-government schools will continue to give special attention to the allocation of their recurrent resources to help meet the needs of transition students. The Commission is asked to monitor closely through its State planning and finance committees progress in this direction. The Government has accepted the Commission's recommendation that basic per capita grants at the rate of 20 per cent of government primary schools' standard costs be provided for nongovernment special schools. This arrangement will mean that special schools in both the government and non-government sectors will receive basic per capita Commonwealth funds in addition to grants from the special education programs.
The Government's allocation to the nongovernment capital program includes its election commitment to prC'vide an additional $25m over the three years 1981,1982 and 1983 to assist nongovernment schools with the provision of new places; $8m will be allocated for this purpose in 1982. In addition to the reallocation of resources within the general purpose programs, the Government has decided to redirect its effort among and within a number of special purpose programs in the light of Commission advice and the Government's own priorities. An additional $6.6m has been allocated for the migrant education program. As proposed by the Commission, the program will be retitled 'English as a Second Language'. It will also incorporate an element from the former contingency program for refugee children which has been transferred from the Department of Education. This latter element will be redesignated as being for new arrivals and will be widened to provide intensive English language instruction for non-refugees. In view of the significant restructuring of these programs, the Commission should review arrangements, in consultation, with State and non-government school authorities, at the first opportunity.
The Commonwealth's programs for the handicapped will also be restructured in 1982. The joint program for integration of handicapped children into regular schools will be more closely related to the special education programs for government and non-government schools by incorporating the

Commonwealth Education Policy Guidelines

4 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 3121

former as an element of these programs. Again, the Commission should enter into early negotiations with State and non-government school authorities to ensure that the restructuring can be effected without disruption. As proposed by the Commission, the former disadvantaged country areas program will be increased by $1.5m in 1982 and retitled as the country areas program to reflect the approved new criteria for allocation of funds in terms of relative isolation and size of settlements. In the context of the policies underlying the Review of Commonwealth Functions, the Government is not prepared at this time to consider commencing any further programs. Whilst the proposed school improvement program will not be implemented, the joint program for professional development will be retained at its existing level of funding.
An amount of $IAm, equivalent to the 1981 grant, will be allocated to the special projects program in 1982 for projects of national significance. The Commission is asked to advise on the proposed distribution of these funds between particular projects. The Commission is asked to consider its proposals for new initiatives in respect of gifted children and students and the community within the context of the total level of funds available. The Government has accepted the Commission's advice that schools in the Australian Capital Territory be eligible to participate in an extended range of programs for disadvantaged schools, special education, and services and development, and will consider the extent of funding in the 1981-82 Budget.
Priorities in Education
The Government believes that Commonwealth grants for the accelerated advancement of education in the past decade and a half have made possible a very great development of facilities and opportunities. We are now going through a process of consolidation in education which, in substantial part, is a reflection of the adjustment to changing demographic patterns being experienced in Australia as a whole. We are now at a stage when it is necessary to take stock of the real gains that have been made and to assess future needs. I have convened a meeting of State and Northern Territory Education Ministers next week to discuss the decisions announced recently as a result of the Review of Commonwealth Functions and those I am announcing today.
The Government is mindful of the stabilisation of enrolments that has taken place in universities and colleges of advanced education after a decade of steady growth and the reduced level of enrolments which is being experienced in government

schools. These elements indicate the changing needs that have to be taken into account in determining the future shape of our education commitment. On the basis of current assessments, the Commonwealth will continue to give priority to the areas of technical and further education, vocational education, and transition education. The Commonwealth will maintain its overall commitment to education as a priority area of direct Commonwealth endeavour. I seek leave to have incorporated in Hansard the attachments to which I have referred.
Leave granted.

TERTIARY EDUCATION COMMISSION GUlDELINES 1982-84
The Government endorses the Commission's view that the 1982-84 triennium will be a period of growth and diversification for TAFE and of adjustment and consolidation for higher education.
For 1982 a total of $1 ,867.6m has been allocated for tertiary education. All 1982 program amounts in this statement are on an oullum basis, while for the purposes of comparison, those for 1981 are expressed in the same price terms. The allocations include provision for the Australian National University, the Canberra College of Advanced Education, the Australian Maritime College and the Darwin Community College. The Tertiary Education Commission is asked to prepare recommendations on the distribution of these funds in accordance with these guidelines.
Universities and CAEs
When it announced its decisions on the Review of Commonwealth Functions (RCF), the Government stated that it had been concerned at the proliferation of separate institutions and proposed immediate action to reverse this trend and to provide for more efficient use of resources. The Government announced that, arising from the recommendations of the Commission, it would promote a major rationalisation and reallocation of resources in higher education. This will involve:
consolidation into larger units of 30 CAEs for which teacher education is the main activity by their incorporation into mUlti-purpose or multi-campus colleges with a single governing body or by integration with neighbouring universities;
redirection of effort in advanced education from teacher education to the technologies and business studies and reallocation of resources accordingly; a similar reallocation of resources to occur in and between universities, as necessary;
the provision of additional resources for CAEs which are able to expand their effort in the technologies and business studies. Having regard to the recommendations of the Commission, an amount of $5 million has been earmarked within the general recurrent grants for advanced education in the years 1982 and 1983 for this purpose;
phasing out of the engineering schools at Deakin University, Bendigo CAE and Preston Institute of Technology, with no new students admitted after 1981; the future of the engineering school at Caulfield Institute of Technology to be examined;
the assessment of grants for the University of Western Australia and Murdoch University to be based on a greater

3122 REPRESENTATIVES 4 June 1981

Commonwealth Education Policy Guidelines

sharing of resources and collaboration between these' two institutions:
no provision of funds to establish a University in Northern Territory. In connection with the consolidation of colleges of advanced education, the Government announced its intention to reach agreement with each State concerning rationalisation measures by the end of 1981. If this cannot be done, the Com-
monwealth will not be prepared to continue recurrent funding
for the colleges concerned af~er the end of 1981, and if the State Governments wish these colleges to continue as separate entities they wiJI have to make their own separate financial provision. :rhe list of the 30colleges concerned is attached.
The Government intends that the consolidation of institutions should result in genuine amalgamations with one governing body and a real saving of resources. In determining the level of recurrent funds for 1982-84, the Government has assumed ,such savings in respect of institutions which have been or wiJI be consolidated during the triennium. The Government will expect the Commission to continue to promote the most efficient use of resources available for tertiary education and requests it to consider the opportunities for greater efficiency and savings by reducing unnecessary duplication of effort among faculties and schools. The Government will continue to look to the Commission to provide ongoing advice on the relatiol1llhip of manpower supply to demand in areas which are critical for education planning.
When the Government announced its Review of Commonwealth Functions decisions, it noted that some action had already been taken in the States on consolidation. Since that announcement, further steps have been taken by some States. In view of the progress which has been made towards consolidation and the need for the changes to be taken into account in legislation during the 1981 Budget Sittings, the Commission is asked to provide me with a progress report on consolidation by3pJune.
In accordance with the policies outlined above, and the Government's commitment to maximum expenditure restraint, a total of $I,558.4m has been allocated for recurrent programs for universities and colleges of advanced education in 1982.
Specific Provisions The Government has reviewed the existing scheme of assistance under which teacher edt;cation courses in certain non-government teachers colleges receive up to 50 per cent of the recurrent costs of teacher education courses in CABs and has decided that from 1982 these wiJI be fully supported. Provision has been made in the total allocation for this purpose. The Government also considers that non-government teachers colleges should be eligible for consideration for capital grants within the total funds available. The Commission is asked to advise on the implementation of this decision after consultation with State authorities and the institutions concerned. Funds have been provided for the Government's 1980 election commitments for the establishment of research centres of excellence in universities: the development of community language courses in universities and/or CABs; and, the provision of 350 additional basic nurse training places in CABs, including the establishment of new pilot courses in Tasmania and Queensland. An additional $50,000 will be provided for evaluation programs with the special objective of expanding the evaluation ofbasic nurse training. Special provision has been made for the Australian Maritime College which is still in its developmental stage. The Government asks that within the level of funds available, the Commission give consideration to the establishment

of a national school of fisheries. TheComm~ion is requested in particular to advise on the desirabil.ity of locating the school in Tasmania.
The Government has previously announced its intention to introduce tuition fees from 1982 in universities and CABs for students newly enrolled in degrees and diplomas subsequent to an initial tertiary qualification. The scale of fees will be an- . nounced in the Budget context and universities and colleges will be advised. on the mannet: in which adjustments will be made to theJevels ofrecurrent grants.
For equipment expenditure, the allocations for 1982 include an additional $4.2m to -assist institutions in replacing outdated equipment and in responding to .nCOlls arising from technological change. While the total grants for capital programs wiJI decline by $5.6/11, there will be some provision for high priQrity projects to commence in 1982 as a result of a lower expenditure commitment to on-going projects.
