tity. I believe that in the interim the allocation of priorities cannot be evaluated properly now. They

additional-I mean net additional-irrigation were also acting with a defective body of infor-

licences should be made with great caution and mation on soils, water, vegetation and, most im-

that the Commonwealth Government should get portantly, how natural systems operate. We have

3142 REPRESENTATIVES 4 June 1981

Institute ofFreshwater Studies Bill

learned a great deal in the last century about management of agricultural lands; unfortunately, the problems seems more to be our inability to implement what we have learned.
Two features of the natural environment in particular have caused substantial problems for irrigators. Firstly, the hydrological systems which evolved, whether surface or subsurface, under natural conditions largely did so in an arid environment; that is, they evolved to cope with relatively low inputs of water. Irrigation has resulted in massive increases in the amounts of water these drainage systems have to handle. One of th~ results is that in areas where subsurface flow characteristics have not been able to adjust, watertables have been forced to rise. When the watertable reaches the root zone waterlogging occurs and yields decrease. Often the watertable rises through soil containing high concentrates of salt. When this occurs the salt is mobilised in solution and carried to the root zone where it harms vegetation. Eventually this salt may enter the river, adding to salinity levels in the mainstream itself. The second feature is that much of the underlying sediment and groundwater, particularly along the lower reaches, is naturally very salty. This is because for millions of years the sea extended as far east as Swan Hill. This natural saltiness has been mobilised by land clearing, river structures and irrigation drainage. The resulting high levels of salinity in the river itself reduces crop yields and limits the choice of crops which can be grown by irrigation relying on river water.
When discussing water quality and drainage problems in relation to agricultural production along the Murray System it is helpful. to perceive the area in terms of two distinct zones with the dividing line running approximately through Swan Hill. To the. east is what is generally known as the Riverina Plains zone and to the west the Mal1ee zone. These zones can be differentiated not only in relation to geographical factors but also in that farmers in each face different salinity problems. The Riverina Plains zone produces mainly livestock, although other things are grown also.. Irrigation is used to grow pastures and supportmg fodder crops. Horticulture is important around Shepparton whilst rice is an important crop in the Deniliquin region. The production l~~els of those areas are very much threatened by nsmg watertables and associated movement of salt into the root zone. If the Shepparton region has a very wet winter, the problem with pear trees becomes crucial. Downstream in the Sunraysia and Riverland regions of the Mallee zone the predominant land use, horticulture, suffers from high salinity levels in the river water used for irrigation.

The important thing to realise is that the two problems are related with salinity levels in the Murray being affected by mobilisation of salt by rising watertables. The fact that the effects of irrigation in the Riverina Plains are felt by those many miles downstream has been the cause of long bitter arguments between the States. The effects of rising watertables and increasing salinity are not always physically noticeable to the passer by or even farmers themselves. Degradation occurs over a period and marginal decreases in yield often occur long before vegetation dies or salt appears at the surface. Further, salinity levels in irrigation water fluctuate markedly with changes in the volume of flow. Drought conditions can lead to much higher concentrations. Therefore, the situation is considerably worse than may be indicated by the extent of land that has become completely unproductive. In addition, production on some salt affected farms may have been maintained, but only by costly increases in labour and capital.
On a regional basis the seriousness of the problem is highly variable, dependent on land use and location. For instance only 1 per cent of the Deniliquin region is at present affected by shallow watertables and the underlying sediments appear to have a relatively low salt content. Yet, on the opposite banks of the river, in the Kerang region, many farms have been put out of production and on others farmers are struggling to make a living on properties badly affected by salt. Two-thirds of this region is underlain by a highly saline watertable within 2 metres of the surface. Barr Creek, which drains the region, adds about 150,000 tonnes of salt a year to the Murray. Obviously for the 3,500 people who live in the region a very real crisis exists.
Other regions have also had their social and economic well-being seriously dislocated. The prime fruit producing areas around Shepparton have symptoms of salinity in more than half of the region's famous pear trees. Unless something is done here the area of land affected by saline watertables is expected to increase threefold by the year 2000. If this is allowed to happen it could spell the end for this rich, intensively irrigated district presently supporting 70,000 people. The Wakool region has also been seriously affected, although here the problem is less widespread. However, the situation is expected to worsen in the next few years.
Downstream in the horticultural areas of Sunraysia and the South Australian Riverland irrigation farmers have to battle against lost markets, inadequate farm size and insufficient resources as well as salty irrigation water. The

Institute ofFreshwater Studies Bill

4 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 3143

threat of a high concentration of salt is greatest in years of reduced flow. Therefore during most years salinity levels are not high enough to affect crops directly. However, evidence suggests that when damage does occur it may remain evident for two to three years; further, if internal drainage is not good salty soils will develop. The towns and irrigation settlements along the arid lower reaches of the system are almost totally dependent on irrigated agriculture for their existence; there is very little else they can turn to.
Obviously the twin problems of rising watertables and salinisation pose serious threats to the continued socio-economic viability of irrigation dependent areas along the Murray system. Further, they pose a serious environmental threat and impose considerable costs, including health costs, on domestic users. A quick examination of relevant available material shows that there has , been no shortage of research carried out into the problem. Nor has this situation suddenly appeared. Rather, the seriousness of the problem has been realised by governments for at least the past 12 years. Indeed, water quality has been the source of infighting between the four governments directly concerned for just as long. If bitter words and so-called protection of sovereign rights could improve salinity levels the rivers waters would be crystal clear by now. I have already given the reasons why there has been inaction.
Managing a complex resource system like the Murray for each of the many differing and often conflicting possible uses is a daunting enough task, but when the management policies must satisfy four separate governments it becomes impossible. The fragmented approach that has existed to date has proven to be woefully inadequate. Bitter infighting has been going on between the States over the Murray since before Federation. Implementation of the River Murray Water Agreement in 1915 solved many of the problems regarding water quantity. This provided for the appointment of the River Murray Commission to give effect to the agreement and authorised the construction of works and the apportionment of water between the States. However, the Commission was given no responsibility for water quality. As a result, any attempts to deal with problems of salinity have been on a State by State basis. This has proven to be both costly and unsuccessful.
Steps were first undertaken to have water quality brought under the auspices of the River Murray Commission early in 1973. Amendments to the River Murray Water Agreement were finally decided on in 1975. As of yet the necessary legislation has not been formally implemented.

The worst of all this is not that it has taken eight years to decide on and implement amendments concerning water quality, but that the amendments will make little real difference to the situation. They will enable the River Murray Commission to monitor salinity levels, co-ordinate research, recommend standards, and advise the States on control measures with relation to water quality. In other words, all they'do is make the Commission an advisory body to the States. It still will not have effective powers directly to control salinity and its associated problems along the river. Further, and most importantly, the agreed upon changes to the agreement will only allow the River Murray Commission to become involved with quality in the main stream; they do not include tributaries. It is absolute nonsense that we do not involve tributaries. Obviously the success of these changes depends almost entirely on the extent to which the States are prepared to co-operate.
Almost every independent body that has had anything to do with the water quality problems of the Murray System has pointed out how formal and informal institutional constraints have effectively limited mitigation of the effects of shallow watertables and salinisation. Similarly, there is a general consensus that a comprehensive and coordinated approach is required to deal with the problems both in the short and long term. As pointed out by Maunsell and Partners in their Murray Valley salinity and drainage report:
It is often not physically possible or economically desirable to deal with these problems on a State by State basis.
The answer lies in control being empowered with a single body which is the sole source of administrative and regulatory power. Inarguably the Murray River system is a resource of national importance. As such it is up to the Federal Government to become more involved before it is too late and this important part of our national heritage is lost not only to the present generation but also to all future generations of Australians.
The second fundamental management issue that I see as being of central importance to the survival of the Murray is that to date there has been a failure to recognise the Murray system as a single renewable natural resource governed essentially by laws of nature. It is a geographic and ecological unit; therefore changes in any part of it will be felt throughout. Thus, having one body to manage soil conservation, another irrigation, another recreation, yet another conservation and then duplicating the whole arrangement every time the river croses State boundaries reflects a thorough disregard or ignorance of the interdependence and

3144 REPRESENTATIVES 4 June 1981

Institute ofFreshwater Studies Bill

complexity of the Murray as a renewable resource.
The present situation of decreasing yields and loss of tracts of agricultural land is basically due to a failure to understand the ecological implications of wide-spread clearing and disruption of natural drainage systems. Surely we have learned enough from our mistakes to realise the importance of ecological guidelines to land use management. Our overall goal for the management of the Murray should be to utilise it in such a way that in the long term it remains a viable renewable resource capable of sustained output. Yet it is well recognised that the Murray wiII be able to provide sustained agricultural yields provided sound conservation practices are adopted.
A positive relationship exists between maintaining as much as possible, natural processes and sustained rural incomes, because once land and water start to degrade it becomes more and more costly to maintain production, and eventually it must decline. Many of the measures likely to result in a significant long term improvement in water quality are on farm and as such require farmer acceptance rather than large scale capital works. Here I could point to the impact of laser grading in helping farmers. Some examples include improved methods of water application, improved farm layout, changes in crop types and in some cases perhaps even land retirement.
Further, planners when attempting to formulate strategies for dealing with drainage and salinity should realise that these are only some of the problems facing farmers in the Murray Valley. Farmers particularly in the horticultural industries are simultaneously trying to cope with other major social and economic problems. Solutions to salinity and drainage problems need to be integrated where appropriate with other government policies and programs relating to agriculture. The situation for thousands of farmers and their families living along the Murray and for towns along the Murray is grave. This area, once commonly known as Australia's 'food basket', is steadily losing ground to the advance of rising watertables of salt. Effective action needs to be taken now if the viability of this national resource is to be maintained for future generations. In my few brief remarks I have tried to stress the agricultural aspects of the problems being faced by the

Murray and the whole Murray River system. I have stressed the fact that it is a living natural resource. What we need is some means whereby the States and the Commonwealth can get together with objective information and do something about the greatest natural resource that Australians possess. I fully back the BiII introduced by the honourable member for Hawker which calls for the establishment of an Institute of Fresh Water Studies. Once again 1 congratulate him. I am sure that most members of this House and people in the Riverland and irrigation areas of this country would thank the honourable member for the initiative he has taken.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Millar)-Is leave granted to proceed with the debate forthwith? There being no objection, leave is granted.

PUBLIC DUTY AND PRIVATE INTEREST
Discussion of MaUer of Public Importance Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER-Mr Speaker has received a letter from the Deputy Leader of the Opposition (Mr Lionel Bowen) proposing that a definite matter of public importance be submitted to the House for discussion, namely:
The failure of the Government to adopt a code of conduct for Ministers and for Members of Parliament despite the lapse of over three years since the establishment of the Committee of Inquiry into Public Duty and Private Interest.
I call upon those members who approve of the proposed discussion to rise in their places.
More than the number ofmembers required by the Standing Orders having risen in their places-
Mr LIONEL BOWEN (Kingsford-Smith) (3.27)-For some years now this Parliament has been waiting for a code of conduct that will regulate the propriety of conduct of Ministers and members of this Parliament. It beggars belief-I have to recite this fact-that this matter has been under discussion for some six years, since the inception of the Gordan committee. In 1977 the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) announced in a Press statement that he proposed to set up a committee. This committee became known as the Bowen Committee of Inquiry into Public Duty and Private Interest. I emphasise that it was in December 1977 that that announcement was made. It is significant that at the time the Prime Minister was having a number of difficulties with one of his Ministers because of allegations about profits being made from land speculation because the Minister held a position in public office. On that occasion the Prime Minister said that it was important that there be a whole new approach to a solution of the problem. He further said that he intended to appoint a commission to establish procedures whereby any breach of the high standards demanded of those in public office could be dealt with.
Next in this chronological table, in February 1978, the right honourable gentleman was saying: 'I have now announced the creation of a commission which will comprise Sir Nigel Bowen, Sir Cecil Looker and Sir Edward Cain. I have given them terms of reference.' Those terms of reference seemed to be rather weak in that reference was made to a statement of principles to be drawn upon concerning the question of private interest, whether pecuniary or otherwise. As 1 said, a report was commissioned in February 1978. It must startle the people of the nation to learn that the report was handed to the Government in July

Public Duty and Private Interest

4 June [981 REPRESENTATIVES 3149

1979, some two years ago. That adds weight to what I have said in support of this matter of public importance. There has been failure hy the Prime Minister, by the Cabinet and by the Government to do anything about adopting a code of conduct for members and Ministers of this Parliament and for a number of other people.
Let us confine discussion to the area in which we can certainly make our own solutions, namely, this Parliament. As the House knows, of late and in fact throughout the whole period since that report was presented, a number of allegations have been made as to whether certain conduct by members of parliament has been proper. Not the least of these allegations have been those concerning the Prime Minister in what became known as the Facom dispute. The question was raised as to whether there had been an undue influence by a tenderer to contact the Prime Minist~r to suggest that some patronage may be given. The House will recall the intensity of the debate and the fact that tender arrangements were, in fact, cancelled. Only in recent days has it been questioned whether it was proper for two members of the Parliament to accept trips overseas sponsored by a person who might be interested in satellite communications or related matters. I say without any fear of contradiction that the whole of Australia would agree that members of parliament should not accept gifts or patronage because to do so could affect their judgment. Is it not clearly indicated that in accepting gifts it would appear that they were beholden to some sector of the community or some person who expects to gain an advantage from a Government decision? Once the matter was raised in the Parliament, the two honourable gentlemen quite wisely decided not to avail themselves of the trip.
There are other matters from which patronage can be inferred. For example, it is known that amendments to the Broadcasting and Television Act, which will be debated shortly were circulated to a number of select people in the community in recent weeks. That seems to indicate that an unfair advantage was given to certain people in the community. If one is interested in the share market or the stock market it could well be to one's advantage to know the intentions of the Government. Details of the proposed new two airline agreement also were made known to a certain select few. Obviously those with pecuniary interests would be interested in the profits or otherwise that would result from certain Government decisions. Such decisions may affect the share market and the value of the shares that people hold. All those things create the political climate I am discussing today.

Why is it that we have waited for so many years to get a code of conduct which is very simple to define? The interesting thing about the Prime Minister and his style is that when he is asked questions about this matter in the Parliament he seems to attribute the blame for not doing anything to somebody else, namely, the Parliament. This is a curious way of avoiding the fundamental issue. This is tantamount to what happened at the Federal Council of the Liberal Party last week. Whenever a motion on any contentious matter was moved by the Prime Minister's enemy, Sir Charles Court, the Prime Minister promptly seconded the motion. I can think of no better way to anaesthetise the whole matter than to have the Prime Minister second anything. Nothing will happen. It is a ploy that is adopted to avoid any criticism. I have no doubt that every time Sir Charles Court sneezes the Prime Minister will apologise for him. This seems to be a curious public stance. Applying that sort of posture to the problems we now face let us have a look at what happened when the right honourable gentleman was asked by the Leader of the Opposition (Mr Hayden) what he was going to do about codes of conduct for members. The matter was raised in the Parliament on 26 May last. The Prime Minister said:
Quite plainly members' travel is a matter that members themselves must decide. I think that this instance and the publicity that has related to it have made it quite plain that the sooner this Parliament discusses in detail the Bowen report and applies it to the members of this Parliament the better it will be. That is under discussion at the moment with members of the Parliament. I draw attention to the fact that the Bowen report has been applied in relation to Ministers and the Commonwealth Public Service. Of course, commercially sponsored trips by Ministers would be outlawed under the Bowen report. But I emphasise again that members of Parliament are in a somewhat different position. I believe that they need to make their own jUdgments about these matters for themselves against the background of experience in the Parliament generally-
Within a matter of hours of making that statement, and again when he had been pressed by my leader about what he was going to do about the Bowen report, he said that we should get ahead and process the Bowen report in relation to members of this Parliament because only then would we have some adequate form of control. If we must get ahead with processing the report, who is stopping us? It is the Prime Minister who has done nothing about it. We have raised this matter in the Parliament time and time again. For example, I raised it on 22 April 1980. Statements have been made in the Parliament by Ministers that there will be discussions about the matter, that it is of considerable importance. Revelations have been made about the shareholdings of Ministers and their families and about the sponsorship

3150 REPRESENTATIVES 4 June 1981

Public Duty and Private Interest

of trips. We want to have the matter finalised and dealt with.
That is the background, but I come now to the present situation. Effectively, nothing has been done, and the Opposition suggests today that some action be taken immediately. We think it could be taken by September this year, when the Parliament will be assembled in the Budget session. Before adverting to that, might I address my remarks to the background of a matter which caused some dissention and discussion, namely, what was known as the Webster case. Honourable members will recall that Senator Webster was deemed under the Constitution to have a pecuniar~ interest because he was a member of ll: propnetary company that had a beneficial interest in the supply of equipment to the Postal and Telecommunications Department, I think, and the matter was raised in the Parliament. The Constitution states that anybody who has a pecuniary interest can forfeit his right to be a member of the Parliament. The question arises: What is a pecuniary interest? It is significant that Sir Garfield Barwick decided that no pecuniary interest was involved in the case of Webster because, on the basis of an old English law, there was no suggestion of anybody beinginhibited by what Webster had done.
The real issue, the real intention, the real background of that law was that members were not to be intimidated by contractors who had contracts with the Crown. It was rather convoluted reasoning, I thought, because any of us who have had experience in either State parliaments or local government know that if a person dares to take part in a decision in that parliament or council on a matter in which he or his family has any interest he is ousted from office, fined, and dealt with on the basis that he has failed to declare his interest. Standing Order 196 says very much the same thing. It is incredible that the Chief Justice of Australia said that our real worry was that we might be inhibited in our discussions in the Parliament because of a contract in which we might have an interest. Surely the modern concept is that people outside would say that we were a bunch of crooks if we were members of this Parliament and did not disclose the fact that we had an interest in the benefit that would flow to us from a contract that was being entered into.
I took the trouble to look at the ancient law, and it is certainly ancient from the point of view of the basis on which the Chief Justice sought to establish his premise. The relevant questions raised by the Chief Justice were in respect of an ancient law that goes back to the eighteenth century or even earlier. If we look at what it was

about, despite the fact that it is in old English, it states quite clearly that any person who wishes to be a member of Parliament and is participating in a contract with the public-the Crown at that time-shall be incapable of being elected. That is the first premise to which I do not think the Chief Justice adverted. The second, and I think it is very clear, is that anybody who gets a benefit from such a contract shall be ousted from office. I fail to see how we could arrive at the reasoning that there was some suggestion in those cases that they were talking only about the freedom of the parliamentarian and not about his benefit.
For all those reasons, we are now in a stupid situation in Australia. We are the most backward of all the Western world countries in the regulation of pecuniary interests. We should have a register of the interests of members, and of'their spouses and their dependent children so far as it is within their knowledge. It can be done; it should be done. It is a pity that the Bowen committee did not recommend it because it would overcome a great deal of the problems. The register would disclose matters such as sponsored travel and the question of acting in a professional capacity and being a Minister at the same time. There would also need to be a number of prohibitions, all of which would be dealt with in what would be known as a code of conduct. We have a .twopronged attack-a register of our interests, disclosed annually, and a code of conduct stating that a member shall not accept remuneration from anybody, either in cash or in kind, other than from government sources. We have a duty of trust to the whole Australian nation.
It is a very simple matter which could well have been dealt with in one month from the point of view of the introduction of these sorts of actions in this Parliament. There are ilmple precedents for such action, whether in the United States, Canada, the United Kingdom, or anywhere else in the world. All those countries have this sort of concept, and it operates also in Victoria, New South Wales, and other areas. I make the point that we should have such a register, available to the public for inspection and containing updated information on what members are able to disclose about their assets and incomes and those of their spouses and dependent children. Because I would like a guarantee that something will be done, I therefore move:
That so much of the Standing Orders be suspended as would prevent the Deputy Leader'of the Opposition from moving: That this House calls upon the Government to introduce into the House before 24 September 1981, a code of conduct governing the conduct of members of parliament and Ministers of the Crown.

Mr HYDE-The honourable member says that liabilities should be included. To achieve the ideal effect, maybe they should. The difficulty occurs in defining liabilities. Some of them are known and some are not. Liabilities tend to come upon one unexpectedly. One cannot account for the unex-
pected, except byway of a code of conduct so that a member might have some understanding of how he should react if the event occurs. Certainly liabilities cannot be recorded in a register. The type of debts that would be recorded in a register are not the ones that we would be worried about. I
go further than that. What about contingent debts-a guarantee given to someone-or contin-
gent assets? What about those things that one might reasonably expect to inherit from an ageing parent? Are they to be included in a register? The Bowen Committee, in my judgment, quite rightly
rejected the concept of a register. It did so because it believed it would not help the situation. What is important is that members of parliament make adequate decisions when they are faced with a situation that has in all probability come upon them unexpectedly, hence the value of a code of conduct. It is not that the code of conduct will be valuable for punishing members who have done something that is not of high moral order; rather the code of conduct is there to forewarn them so that they do not do something that they may later regret. States (Tax Sharing and Health Grants) Bill

4 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 3153

members will be aware that a claim is, in fact, currently under consideration by the Aboriginal Land Commissioner, Mr Justice Toohey, in relation to lands in the Alligator Rivers Stage II Region, which incorporates the Mudginberri and Munmarlary leases. The Commonwealth offered in early 1978 to acquire the leases but it was not possible to reach agreement on the amount of compensation. The leases were compulsorily acquired on 23 June 1978. However, the companies holding the leases have contested the validity of that acquisition and further attempts to reach a voluntary settlement have so far failed. So long as these two leases are held, or appear to be held, by the companies, the Government is inhibited in giving effect to policies which relate to future activities in the Alligator Rivers Region.
The former lessees have instituted proceedings in the supreme court in the Northern Territory to challenge the validity of the Commonwealth's acquisition of the leases. The Bill now before honourable members would give the Commonwealth title and right to immediate occupation of the leases, but at the same time ensure that the rights of the former lessees to continue to contest the validity of the original acquisition is in no way affected. The Bill also provides for any compensation to persons who had interests in the acquired land to be paid on just terms. Honourable members will appreciate that the determination of validity of the original acquisition as well as negotiations on compensation could be very protracted issues. It is the view of the Government that these matters should not prejudice or delay the determination of the Aboriginal land claim in the Alligator Rivers Region which is required in order that Government policies relating to establishment of the Kakadu National Park and mineral development can be implemented. The Government has therefore decided that it is appropriate that legislation be passed to ensure the Commonwealth's right to title and immediate possession of the Mudginberri and Munmarlary pastoral leases. I commend the Bill to honourable members.

States (Tax Sharing and Health Grants) Bill

will be funded from a special health grant for the Community Health Program and School Dental Program. A further factor which stems from this legislation is that the States will be forced to implement some very considerable increases indeed in the charges which they impose in public hospitals, both for in-patient services and for outpatient services.
That is what the Bills do in a formal sense. Practically, however, the legislation gives effect to measures ,which have created an unprecedented storm of protest from the States, both in respect of the effect that these measures will have on the States and in, respect of the method of their implementation. It is no exaggeration to say that by ~hese Bills the Government is asking the Parliament to give legislative approval to the most shameful episode in intergovernmental relations in our history in which the Commonwealth has indulged in deceptive, dictatorial and grossly offensive methods to implement its policy of di~inishing the financial viability of the States. The, Opposition is disgusted by both the Governmenfs policies as outlined in the main Bill and by the appalling process by which the Government has sought to have them implemented. Accordingly, "we completely oppose the States (Tax Sharing and Health Grants) Bill. We do not oppose: the Local Government (Personal Income Tax Sharing) Amendment Bill as this does no more than retain the present system of determining the allocation of tax funds to local government.
I will be confining my remarks to tax sharing arrangements. The honourable member for Bonython (Or Blewett), the Opposition spokesman on health, who will be the next Opposition member to follow me in this debate, will deal in more detail with health matters. Before examining more closely what is in the legislation, the circumstances of its determination and the effect it will have, I wish to remind the House of the background to the events which led to the drafting of this legislation now before the House. Tax sharing was brought in by the Fraser Government as a result of so-called new federalism which was proposed by the now Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) when he was in opposition in 1975. The key element was the right of the States to a share of personal income tax receipts of the Federal Government.
I make it clear that, of course, there has always been tax sharing in a sense. This arrangement was formally to allocate general revenue funds to the States as a percentage of personal income tax receipts. This was to replace the formula previously employed by the Labor Government, which make

general revenue grants to the States which they could spend on anything. The Labor Government's formula involved movements in average weekly earnings, movements in population and'a betterment factor of 3 per cent. Another element of the tax sharing proposal was that a guarantee would be given that for' four years the States would receive no less under the tax sharing formula than they would have received under the Labor Government's formula.
Those were the main elements of the so-called new federalism. Of course, great claims were made by the Prime Minister-as the Leader of the Opposition, as he was in 1975, and subsequently as Prime Minister-about the importance of this so-called new federalism. In 1975 the present Prime Minister said:
It is the greatest advance in Federal-State relations since Federation.
That is not a minor claim. In 1976 the Treasurer (Mr Howard) waxed very enthusiastiC about new federalism in a speech in this House on 28 April, when he said:
The truth is that the revenue sharing arrangements, the new federalism arrangements, unveiled by the Opposition, as it then was towards the end of last year, are certainly the most dramatic and the most significant development in government relations since Federation.
He was echoing what the Prime Minister had said. He went on:
When implemented these proposals will end for all time the begging bowl of the Premiers Conference.
In the light of recent events, of course, that rings rather hollow. He stated further:
They will end for all time the wrangling that has gone on between Federal and State governments over the years.
Those were the claims made by the Treasurer. I am sure he rather regrets having made them in the light of subsequent events.
The developments in tax sharing after it was implemented were along these lines: Over the first four years the States were forced to rely on the guarantee that they would getno less, by way of general revenue grants, than they would have under the Labor Government's formula. So without that guarantee they would have got less, and this shows what a great reform it was. It' was, in fact, the Labor Government's ,policy that was being implemented rather th,an the Fraser Government's policy. A further development was that other forms of payment. did not increase as fast as general revenue payments. Indeed, some categories fell markedly in real terms. I ask the House to take note of the following figures. Between 1975-76 and 1980-81 in real terms general revenue grants increased by 19.4 per cent,specific purpose recurrent grants increased by 12+ per

States (Tax Sharing and Health Grants) Bill

4 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 3155

cent, general purpose capital payments declined by 38.6 per cent, and specific purpose capital payments declined by 54 per cent. So on the capital side there was an enormous fall in real terms in payments to the States. Overall there was a 6 per cent fall in real terms in total payments to the States. In the five full financial years of the Fraser Government the States have received 6 per cent less in real terms than they received in the last year of the Labor Government.
When one adds to that the fact that there has been a 6 per cent rise in the population we see that the payment by the Commonwealth Government to the States in 1980-81 is, in fact, 12 per cent less in real terms per head of population than it was in the last year of the Labor Government. This is a very substantial fall indeed especially as Commonwealth payments to the States account for 70 per cent of total funds available to the States. A 12 per cent fall in 70 per cent of total funds is a very substantial fall indeed.
In 1980-81-the current financial year-the Fraser Government ended the four-year guarantee that the States would get no less in the way of general revenue grants than they would have obtained under the Labor Government's formula and gave a guarantee for that one year, which of course was an election year. This guarantee, which was given in June last year, was to the effect that the States would get no less in real terms in 1980-81 than they had received in the previous year. Of course, this was much less than the Labor formula would have given the States.
For 1981-82 the States were concerned at what was likely to happen. They were concerned because of the substantial overall decline in real funds over the previous five years. It was not clear to them what, if any, guarantee would apply in 1981-82, particularly if the Commonwealth and the Treasurer were talking of the possibility of introducing a broad-based indirect tax and making some offsetting cuts in personal income tax. This greatly concerned the States as their tax sharing formula was based on personal income tax. Any significant decline in personal income tax as a result of the introduction of a broad-based indirect tax would have had obvious implications for the payments to them under the current formula; and they were very concerned about that.
In February this year the States made a combined written submission to the Federal Government seeking various reforms of the tax sharing arrangements. The key elements of that submission by the States were that they were very concerned about the unsatisfactory current situation. It had been satisfactory in the past because

of the Labor Government's formula guarantee but that had expired after four years. They were concerned about the base for determination of the States tax sharing grant and said that that should be either personal income tax or total tax depending on what happened to Federal Government tax policy in relation to the broad-based indirect tax. They said that a guarantee should exist which was the same as the Labor Government's formula but with a betterment factor of only 1.8 per cent, which of course had been the betterment factor earlier in the Labor Government's period of office. The States said also that there should be much more consultation between the Federal and State governments in regard to all these matters. As I said, the States made that submission in written form to the Commonwealth in February this year. Clearly, they were very unhappy but were trying to be constructive.
The next development was the Premiers Conference which was held exactly one month ago on 4 May this year. At that time the State Pr.emiers met the Prime Minister and the Treasur1:r in a Conference which was undoubtedly the greatest setback to the federal system in our history. The Federal Government did various things at that Conference. Firstly, it imposed its proposals on the Premiers, with some minor alterations, in a way which made a mockery of its obligation to consult the Premiers. The Treasurer told the Premiers that the Government had decided to change the base for calculation of tax sharing grants to a total tax-that is, from personal income tax to total tax-and to give only an 8 per cent rise in tax sharing grants in 1981-82. This involved breaking away from the fixed percentage formula of personal income tax of 39.87 per cent of the previous year's revenue as laid down in the States (Personal Income Tax Sharing) Act.
Later at that Premiers Conference on 4 May the Commonwealth Government agreed to raise the 8 per cent increase in the grant for 1981-82 to 9 per cent. The States were still very angry that there had been no real consultation especially in regard to the tax base. I will just quote the words of the Premier of New South Wales, Mr Wran, in respect of the discussion that day, he said:
It was characterised by arrogance and domination in the sense that everything was on a take it or leave it basis. And I don't know frankly, of any Premier there who accepted what was offered or what was finally thrust upon them. For my part I rejected the concept of total tax sharing and the percentage which was offered and it's quite laughable for Mr Fraser or for Mr Howard to talk about negotiating.

So there was a lack of real consultation which greatly annoyed the States because of the legal requirement of the Commonwealth to consult, particularly in relation to the base for the establishment of the tax sharing formula. Section 13, sub-section (1) of the States (Personal Income Tax Sharing) Act states:

The Government of the Commonwealth shall, before' 30

There is a clear requiremerit to consult with the States. As I read the dictionary, the word 'consult' means to take counselor to seek advice. That being the meaning of the word 'consult', it is highly questionable whether the Commonwealth Government, in fact, abided by the Act by properly consulting the States. Even though technically it may have done so by holding the Conference in the first place and simply studying the States' submission, it certainly did not discuss the matter with the States before determining its view as to whether any change in tax sharing provisions was desirable. So much then for co-operative federalism, which was what new federalism was supposed to be all about. The Commonwealth clearly did not consult with the States in any normal meaning of that term before determining its view, and it sought to impose that view on the States.

The second thing that happened at the Premiers Conference was that the Commonwealth deceived the States. The Commonwealth told the States, at first, that in all the economic circumstances, it could not give more than an 8 per cent rise in the general revenue grant for 1981-82. After much protesting from the States it raised that figure to 9 per cent, again saying that it could give no more. Then, in a quite extraordinary episode which I am sure is well in the minds of all honourable members of parliament and most of the public the Prime Minister and tho Treasurer deliberately briefed selected journalists to the effect that they would have been prepared to give another one per cent in the general revenue grant to the States for 1981-82, which would have been about another $70m. The States were therefore clearly deceived by the Federal Government, which misrepresented its real position to the States. Clearly the Treasurer and the Prime Minister had Cabinet approval to go to a 10 per cent rise, but they did not do so. ! quote from an ~nter view with the Premier of Queensland, Mr BJelkePetersen, on AM on 7 May in regard to this matter. He said:

But I do believe that this is mosf disgusting and despicable. That the Federal Treasurer and Prime Minister have in effect, deceived the Premiers, they informed us that they'd given us all that they could possibly give us. We had a whole day and half a night arg1;lmen.t about, it and t~ be ~~ld. this, to h~ve this admission at thiS pomt of time, I thmk It s Just unbelievable almost.
The admission he was talking about was the admission by the Prime Minister and the Treasurer that there was another one per cent to be had. Clearly, Mr Bjelke-Petersen felt as though he had been deceived. I am sure he was speaking on behalf of every Premier. They all felt they had been totally deceived by what the Commonwealth had said to them. It will be argued by the Government that it had a range of increases that it could give under its Cabinet approval and that it is appropriate in the negotiating situation for the Commonwealth to be flexible. That presumes that a Premiers Conference ought to be about negotiating with a view to giving the States as little as possible, which is clearly the way the Commonwealth entered this Conference, rather than in the spirit of co-operative federalism, trying to assess the real needs of the States and to balance those needs against the Commonwealth's own constraints. There is no evidence that the Commonwealth concerned itself at all with the States' needs; it just sought to give them as little as possible. It went in there to give them as little as it possibly could and if it could get away with less than 10 per cent certainly it was happy to do so regardless of the impact on the States. In so doing it. told the States things that were not true-that It could not responsibly give more than it finally gave; that is, 9 per cent. That was not its real position at all. The States were undoubtedly deceived and instead of co-operative federalism we now have highly distrustful and resentful federalism.
The third thing that happened at that Premiers Conference was that the Prime Minister and the Treasurer humiliated and grossly offended the Premiers. They did this by leaking to the journalists the one per cent, which I have already mentioned. This humiliated the Premiers because they were made to look like fools who did not get as much out, of the Commonwealth as they could have had they been more competent. To compound the felony, after a couple of .days ~f prevarication as to whether or not the Pnme Mmlster and the Treasurer actually leaked this, they finally admitted that they had dorie so, but they chose to defend that decision in this Parliament rather than apologise for their actions. Thus, in Parliament on 8 May, the Prime Minister said:
It would have been grossly offensive to the Premiers not to go into a conference with a negotiating position.

States (Tax Sharing and Health Grants) Bill

4 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 3157

I have dealt with this nonsense of negotiations being what Premiers Conferences are all about, but the point is that nothing could have been more offensive to the Premiers than what the Prime Minister and the Treasurer did. They publicly humiliated the Premiers and made them look fools. The Prime Minister also said there was nothing wrong with making Cabinet decisions public, as though this was some normal announcement of a Cabinet decision which had to be made public. It was nothing of the kind. It was not a public announcement; it was leaked to journalists. It was not a decision that had to be made public; indeed, it was a decision that, in all the circumstances, should never have been made public. Why did they do it? The Treasurer said that they did it so they would not appear to have been backing down on their own rhetoric about the need to cut government expenditure by giving the States an extra one per cent. This could have been avoided simply by being honest with the States in the first place, by telling the States how much they were really prepared to provide them with. They were not prepared to do that. Instead, they totally misrepresented their position and deceived the States, which therefore led to this appalling situation. I note also that the Prime Minister and the Treasurer apparently made some apology within their own party room. They have made no apology to the Parliament or to the public at large and I believe this debate is a convenient time for the Treasurer and the Prime Minister to make that public apology.
The overall effect of what has been done has been to deal a grievous blow to the federal system in this country by making a complete mockery of new federalism. How hollow-or laughable, now-is the Prime Minister's 1975 claim that it represented the greatest advance in Federal-State relations since Federation. The situation now is that the State Premiers are intensely bitter at what happened and, more important than their wounded pride, is their strong distrust and lack of confidence in this Government. 1 draw attention in thrlt respect particularly to a statement in a letter by Sir Charles Court to the Prime Minister on 8 May, in which he said:
I must make it clear that if the Commonwealth were to give effect to your proposals, contrary to your earlier assurances and despite the protests of the States, we could no longer feel that we could conclude any agreements with your government with confidence that they would be honoured.
That is a very tough statement indeed. It is very important in this debate, because the proposals about which Sir Charles was talking are the proposals now before this Parliament. The proposals in this legislation are those which Sir Charles said, if implemented by this Government, would cause

him to have no confidence or trust in the Fraser Government. We are being asked to vote on that legislation and honourable members on the other side of the House should bear that fact in mind when casting their votes following this debate. The fact is that the Western Australian Government,. a Liberal government, now clearly has no confidence or trust in this Government. That is an incredible situation. Sir Charles made another pertinent comment when he said that we had reached the absolute nadir of federation. I think that properly describes the situation that has been reached. Clearly, federalism is a chaos. The Commonwealth is riding completely roughshod over the States. The Commonwealth's behaviour has been arrogant, deceptive, dictatorial and grossly offensive to the States. The States are bitterly resentful at their maltreatment. 'Maltreatment' is a word which has particular poignance in the present situation. Without confidence or trust in the Federal Government our new federalism is completely exposed as a hoax and a fraud.
I turn to the financial impact of the Premiers Conference decisions being imposed on the States. Firstly, there is a substantial cutback for 1981-82 in the real level of general revenue funds to the States. As I have mentioned, there is a 9 per cent rise. That is clearly less than the rate of inflation. The consumer price index rose during the last 12 months by 9.5 per cent. However, a more relevant inflation rate is the implicit deflator of government consumption expenditure because most of this money is spent on recurrent expenditures, consumption expenditures, by the States and the inflation rate, as published by the Commonwealth Government in the National Accounts for government consumption expenditure, is 13.3 per cent over the last 12 months. That is by far the most relevant figure.
Mr Howard-That includes defence.
Mr WILLIS-The Treasurer says that includes defence. That is a point he made in a previous debate. It is totally irrelevant. He said there had been a 7 per cent increase in real growth of defence expenditures and that had something to do with this figure. It has nothing whatever to do with it. We are talking about the Government's own calculation of the price index which applies to government expenditures. It has nothing to do with real increases; it is the Government's own calculation of the inflation rate which is properly applied to government consumption expenditures and which is published in the National Accounts. So, on top of the 13.3 per cent increase, which I
say is the appropriate figure, there has been a 1+
per cent increase in population, so the States needed 14+ per cent to stay at the same real level

3158 REPRESENTATIVES 4 June 1981

States (Tax Sharing and Health Grants) Bill

per head of population as last year. In fact, the States got 9 per cent, or $375m less than was required. As a percentage of income tax, which is the previous way it was calculated, the 1981-82 grant will fall from 39.87 per cent of personal income tax in the previous year to 38.32 per cent, which is a loss of some $260m. Again, that is what honourable members are being asked to vote for today.
The second element which comes from the Premiers Conference is that this cutback will continue into future years. It is not just a cutback for 1981-82, but it is built into all future years for as long as the new formula lasts. This is because for 1982-83 we take the 1981-82 grant asa percentage of the previous year's total tax then apply that to the 1981-82 total tax receipts. This percentage, which will be about 20 per cent, will then be applied in future years. Thus, the 1981-82 cutback is reflected in reduced funding for all future years while this legislation remains on the books.
The House should note also that the use of 1981-82 as a transition year was just a device to reduce payments. There is no real need for a transition year. If we want to move from an income tax base to a total tax base we can simply take the 1980-81 grant as a percentage of the previous year's total tax and apply that percentage to the 1980-81 total tax receipts to obtain the grant for 1981-82. But the Government has not done that. It has introduced this transition year as a means of reducing the amount not only for that year but also for all future years.
Thirdly, there has been an abolition of any meaningful guarantee. There is to be no future guarantee along the lines of the Labor Government's formula, nor even any guarantee that the real level of the grant will not decline. The only guarantee is that there will be no fall in the money amount of grants in any year below the level in the previous year. At a time of substantial inflation that, of course, is a totally useless guarantee.
Fourthly, the Government will be able to reduce grants to the States in future below the level given by the formula by declaring an increase in any Commonwealth tax to be a special surcharge and to be excluded from the total tax sharing base. This leaves the States in a totally precarious position because the Treasurer can increase some tax and so say: '1 declare this to be some special surcharge'. As a result it is not included in the total tax base and therefore he does not have to share one cent of it with the States. The States having no guarantee now, apart from the useless one about no reduction in the money amount, are therefore left in a precarious position. From now

on the Commonwealth can quite clearly manipulate the system to give the States what it likes. If it so wishes it can force the States to share in any tax reductions and deny them any benefit from any tax increases.
A further point I wish to make relates to the falsity of the Government's arguments for the cutback. The Government has sought to justify its harsh treatment of the States by the measures incorporated in this legislation by stating, as the Treasurer did in his opening address to the Premiers Conference, that there had been a 3 per cent real per capita increase in the States' recurrent expenditure on goods and services between 1975-76 and 1980-8l. The Treasurer said that there had been a rapid improvement in the levels of service provided by the States in recent years. It is true that there has been an increase in the real level of recurrent expenditure per head of some 3 per cent by the States over that period. But that is because the States' recurrent funding has been increased mainly because of the application of the Labor Government formula as a guarantee underlying general revenue payments to the States over most of that period. But that is not total spending by States on services. State services are also affected by capital expenditures and, as I mentioned previously, they have fallen disastrously. They have fallen by some 5.7 per cent in real terms per head over that period. This aITects services in such important areas as education, public housing, roads, transport, aged persons, accommodation and so on. Many State services are badly affected by cutbacks in capital works or capital expenditures.
Total State expenditure has increased by only one per cent per annum in real terms per capita over 1975-76 to 1980-8l. One per cent per annum has been the increase in total State expenditure, and that one per cent is exactly the same as the rate of growth in the Commonwealth Government's expenditures. All attempts by the Government to portray itself as the collector of revenue and the States as the big spenders is absolute nonsense. The fact is that the States' expenditure has increased at the same rate in real terms per head over the last five years as the Commonwealth's expenditure. It is absurd to talk in terms of the States being the big spenders as against the Commonwealth and the need for the States to be cut back to bear their share of the burden. They have borne their share. They have increased their expenditure no faster than has the Commonwealth over this period. There is no doubt that the Premiers Conference was disastrous for Australia. in the context of federation.

Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Armitage)- Before I call the honourable member for Curtin I remind the House that this is the honourable member's maiden speech in this House. I ask honourable members to extend to him the usual courtesies.
Mr ROCHER (Curtin) (4.30)-As is the custom on such occasions, I seek the indulgence of the House to make some brief acknowledgements. I am acutely aware of the stature and achievement of the man who gave his name to my electorate. They speak for themselves, along with the ac'- colades of historians who have recorded them far more eloquently than I could ever hope to, It may be sufficient to say that John Curtin was a respected leader of his party and Government. While Prime Minister he was charged with conducting the affairs of his country in the most dire period of its history. He did so with skill and determination and, towards the end especially with courage. John Curtin was a truly great Australian.
The first member for Curtin, elected in 1949 is also a great Australian. However, probably because he is still alive his contributions have yet to be fully recorded. Sir Paul Hasluck was a journalist who lamented in his first autobiography called Knocking About the passing of an age where the journalist's primary task was reporting events factually without interpretation. After distinguished public service, he was elected to this Parliament where'his abilities and experience were soon put to good use in the Menzies Ministry. His retirement from Parliament was followed by service to his country as Governor-General of Australia where he further distinguished himself in that high office. I understand he is maintaining his writing and scholarly pursuits in retirement and I sincerely hope he will continue to enjoy the fruits of his public service for many years to come.
Sir Paul's successor and my immediate predecessor was the Honourable Vic Garland. His achievements in representing the electorate are fresh in the minds of most honourable members. It was his good fortune to be elected as a young man, to serve with distinction in both opposition and government, to become a respected Minister and eventually to leave this place still a comparatively young man. Whilst his experience and undoubted ability will be missed by honourable members on this side of the House, they will be invaluable in the task ahead of him. I wish him and his 'wife and family well, both for his term as High Commissioner in London and thereafter.
It remains for me to thank, however inadequately, the members of the Curtin division of the Liberal Party for their expressions of confidence

in me, which took many forms both before and since my election. The same may be said generally about Liberals throughout Western Australia and all those with whom I was privileged to associat~ during my term as a senator for Westen Australia. I acknowledge also the support of voters in the by-election of 21 February who accorded me the great privilege of representing them in this Federal Parliament. To you,Mr Deputy Speaker, to your fellow deputies and in particular to Mr Speaker, I extend my thanks f<?r the courtesies and considerations that have already been extended to me. I hope that they continue and that I may earn them. The same may be said for the staff of the House of Representatives.
The following comments are relevant to the States (Tax Sharing and Health Grants) Bill which is being discussed in this cognate debate. As the economic strength of Australia becomes even, more dependent on activity in Western Australia and Queensland, the benefits will be more and more apparent in those areas of Australia where there are heavy concentrations of manufacturing industry. In particular, the States of Victoria and New South Wales will derive economic benefits from increased production of manufactured goods following rising orders generated from expanding resource industries. Because of its unique contribution to the development of the Australian nation, as well as geographical factors and at times a fierce confidence in the individual and collective abilities of Western Australians, my State is well equipped to continue to playa significant role in advancing the interests arid well-being of all Australians.
In the context of this debate on the legislation before us, it is important to remind the House of some facts and figures. All States except Queensland have enjoyed benefits superior to those bestowed on Western Australia arising out of Commonwealth Government assistance to industry. Of those, the States with the greatest concentration of population have enjoyed the greatest advantage. Assistance to Victorian industry in 1977-78 amounted to $1,871m, $15m more than was involved in the entire consolidated revenue fund for Western Australia in 1980-81. By comparison, Western Australian industry in 1977-78 was subsidised by $284m. If, as has been arg,ued elsewhere as being equitable for tax sharing purposes, all subsidy of industry had been distribuied on a per capita basis, the Western Australian economy would have benefited by a furth~r $ 133. 3m. Victoria would have received $561.2tl less.

3160 REPRESENTATIVES 4 June 1981

States (Tax Sharing and Health Grants) Bill

The figures I have cited and the ones I will proceed to cite are taken from a study based on the material compiled by the Industries Assistance Commission, it being the principal adviser to the Commonwealth on policies affecting industry. It 910uld be noted that the greater part of subsidisition of industry took the form of tariff protection. Estimates of the totals for each State in 1977-78 are: Victoria $1,871m, as I have already mentioned; New South Wales, $1,759m; South Australia, $444m; Queensland, $400m; Western Australia, $284m and Tasmania, $113m. Expressing those figures on a per capita basis: Victoria received $492 per head of population; New South Wales, $353; South Australia, $346; Tasmania, $274; Western Australia, $235, and Queensland, .$186. One of the effects of legislation on tariffs and quotas is a deficit in trade between the States and Western Australia oUl,400m.
If the more populous States have benefited to the extent I have just outlined, it is equally evident that the more sparsely populated States have been disadvantaged. The system of economic subsidy has been enduringly to the benefit of the more populous States of Victoria and New South Wales, as I have already mentioned. It has made inevitable the concentration of popUlation and industry in those States. It has given them a stronger base for Commonwealth, State and local government taxes and charges. It has afforded economies of scale in the provision of services, uncomplicated by the need to service a small population scattered over a huge area. It has made it difficult for manufacturers to operate close to small domestic markets, in competition with subsidised industries in those States with big mark,ets in their own back yards.
If ever we must adopt a per capita basis to determine the competing claims of the States for shares of tax. revenues-there is some good argument for doing so-account must be taken of other factors such as those just canvassed, if economic justice is to be seen to be done. It cannot be left to the Commonwealth Grants Commission to provide the remedy for the ills of protectionism and its effects on State finances and economies. Clearly! if tax sharing and financial arrangements with the States cannot allow for the insidious, invisible impositions of the tariff, steps should be taken to offset the costs of tariff protection. The obvious answer, and one which I personally advocat~-is to cut and phase out the cause of the problem,which is to be found in tariff policy and legislaton itself.
[t is simply not good business to disregard the p£st and potential contribution of Western Australia to the wealth creating process. However

these remarks may seem to smack of parochialism, real wealth enables the provision of help for those demonstrably in need throughout the country. The State which produced $3,854m of export income in 1979-80, 2004 per cent of the national total incidentally, cannot be doing too much that is wrong. What is more, its achievements and initiatives do not benefit Western Australians alone. For example, about one half of Australians engaged in work on the North West Shelf gas project are employed in other States. That number is currently 2,000 out of an estimated 4,000.
The purchasing power of a population of 1.5 million people is important to Australian manufacturing industry. The development of vast resources is also important. Resources development itself, often undertaken in remote areas, requires the provision of services and the creation of assets which have come, rightly or wrongly, to be expected of State governments. Services of which I speak involve schooling, domestic power, transport, health care and other basic needs. Assets include roads, ports, industrial power stations, aerodromes and the like. Traditionally, these are financed out of State general revenues, tax sharing entitlements- -such as those we are discussing today-Loan Council funds and, latterly, special borrowing rights. All these sources of funds are substantially or entirely directly controlled or influenced by the Commonwealth Government. Given the potential returns to all the people of Australia in the form of employment, company and personal taxation and royalties, it would be short sighted not to invest now in public services and assets essential to expanding exploitation of natural resources. The lesson of the past decade, when our primary industries enabled Australia's economy to weather the international recession, should not be forgotten.
It is in the light of these realities that I address my remarks today. The reduction of all Commonwealth expenditures remains a priority of the Fraser Government, and rightly so. The containment of inflation, management of the money supply, elimination of Budget deficits and reduction of taxation, all depend on the ability of government to tackle its expenditure. It is to be acknowledged, of course, that one of the most difficult tasks is to establish spending priorities within overall economic strategy. If these tax sharing arrangements, in combination with Loan Council deliberations and Grants Commission findings, lead to a situation whereby clearly beneficial developments cannot proceed for want of public services and assets, there should be the flexibility to undertake a review whenever those
