Dr BLEWETT-I have no doubt that the Treasurer did. I would like to know what the Treasurer, or whoever he got the words from, means by an out-patient occasion of service. Does it mean that a single visit to a hospital out-patient department whatever the number of services would attract a charge of $15? Or does it mean that if on a single visit one has, say, three services delivered a $15 charge will be made for each service? I think it is important to clarify that point. There is no clear evidence provided by the Commonwealth as to the basis of the calculations for the additional revenue raising capacity of the States in the health sector. Certainly they provide figures for increased hospital charges and the return from an anticipated insurance cover and for charges for out-patient and compensable charges. But no evidence has been presented either to this House or to the States as to the basis of those calculations-what kinds of assumptions and expectations underlie the various calculations which relate to the additional hospital revenue that the Commonwealth is claiming the States can raise.
On the other hand, for the States of Tasmania and South Australia, which were wise enough under Labor governments to sign a 10-year cost sharing agreement with the Commonwealth, hospital funding and a health grant covering community health, women's refuges and the school dental program will at least remain distinct. That is, hospital funding will continue under the co.st sharing arrangement and a single health grant WIll cover community health, women's refuges and the school dental program. At least in those two States there will be a distinction. Of course the Minister for Health made it quite clear that despite the hospital cost sharing agreements hospital funds for Tasmania and South Australia will be squeezed in exactly the same way as funds for other States..
Overall the States and the Northern Territory will receive some $253m less. That figure is correct as long as I am up with all the latest changes. We have had the Queensland change, an adjustment for Victoria and there may be others in the pipeline at the moment. As far as I can calculate, some $253m less will be received' by the States than they would have received had the existing arrangements continued. That represents an overall cut in Commonwealth funding of about 16 per cent. Of course,the States are expected to recoup the money by the increased hospital in-patient and out-patient charges and by the application of

a rigorous means test to all their public health services.
I think, first of all, it is ir;.jortant to recognise the centrality of this measure to the whole reactionary health package of the fifth Fraser health scheme. While the Government is abdicating all responsibility for public hospitals in this country and is washing its hands of the very real problems in the hospital sector, it aims by these provisions to compel the States to raise charges for in-patient and out-patient services in their public hospitals, to the levels I have already specified, and to impose a strict means test so as to widen the scope of those compelled to meet the charges. Of coursethis relates to the whole package-the purpose of these changes is to compel Australians back into fiat rate contributions to private health insurance funds.
I say just in passing-I will deal with this matter at much greater length at another stage-the only significant difference between a near" compulsory private health insurance system and an income related health levy is that the fiat rate contributions of the private insurance system are inequitable and regressive and advantage the better off as against the less well off. On the other hand, an income related health levy is fairer an~ more equitable in that it places the heavieI: burdens on the broader shoulders. For all practical purposes both are taxes, bu~ in terms ?fe9~ity and fairness and, as well, effiCIency and sImpltcIty, the health levy is clearlypreferable. But no doubt we will argue that proposition for the next two years. There is no doubt about the purposes of these changes. This is the chief weapon in this Government's armoury to force Australians into the private tax system of health insurance.
How does the Treasurer defend this abdication of Federal responsibility for. the hospitals of this country? Given the unfortunate compl).ny which the Treasurer keeps, I have frequently admired the fact that, unlike the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser), he does participate in debates. Unlike so many of his colleagues, including the Minister for Health, he does respond to the arguments of his opponents, usually in logical and lucid ways. However, I fear that when the Treasurer gets into the health area he takes on some of the characteristics of his colleague, the Minister for Health, that is, double-talk, evasions and vacuous cliches. Look at the major arguments he advanced for the health grant proposals. He stated:
The new health grants arrangements reflect the Govern-. ment's consideration of the Jamison Commission of Inquiry into the Efficiency and Administration of Hospitals, -discussions with State Health Ministers and the outcome of the

States (Tax Sharing and Health Grants) Bill

4 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 3163

Review of Commonwealth Functions. This Bill, in short, refiects detailed consideration of the wide range of views that has been expressed on these matters.
The only possibly perfectly correct statement there is that it may represent the view of the razor gang. I would not put anything beyond that lot. It may reflect their view. It is certainly not the result of discussions with State Health Ministers. I quote from the joint statement of the State Health Ministers on 8 May. They stated:
We observe that we have never been consulted in the real sense of the word and at no time prior to its recent decision has the Commonwealth indicated any clear intention to the States.
There is no evidence of real discussions with State Ministers. The Jamison Commission opposed the proposals embodied in this Bill. It recommended against the proposal to absorb hospital funds into general revenue funds. The Commission was supported by nearly every major health care organisation in this country. Even the Australian Medical Association, before it was bought off with the goodies in the fifth Fraser health scheme, opposed absorption of hospital funds into the general revenue grants to the States. The health care organisations all have very good reasons for opposing absorption.
The Jamison Commission believes, and most of those health organisations believe, there should remain a distinct national provision, a participation by national governments in setting national priorities for hospitals across this country. The Jamison report specifically argued, despite what the Treasure~ implies and despite what the Minister for Health has constantly implied, that it was necessary that the Commonwealth retain this contribution to hospital priorities in order to maintain a rough equality of hospital services across this country. Again, to divide the system of health funding from any responsibi\ities for hospitals is a recipe for inefficiency. If the Federal Government just passes the whole responsibility of public hospitals back to the States but continues to control the national insurance system then that division of responsibility is a recipe for inefficiency. I fear, as do many others, that there would be a decline in the resources allocated to health as a long run result of these proposals because of many other powerful pressures on State Budgets. For all these reasons, the groups that I have talked about, including the Jamison Commission, opposed what is now being done in this Bill.
Let me take a second major argument advanced by the Treasurer. He stated:
The Government sees this change as a move towards ending divided control over the provision of health care services in ! the 'St~tes and the Northern Territory and thus eliminating

potential costs and inefficiencies that can be involved in such duplication.
That is mostly, I think, gobbledegook. At the very same time that the Government was giving away a role in relation to public hospitals it was increasing its support for private hospitals and it was accepting responsibility for total funding of nurs~ ing home benefits. As a result of these shifts and changes a whole host of problems of duplication, overlap, and lack of integration either remain or are made worse. For instance, there will be an increasing problem in relation to the proper provision of nursing home beds. There are two chief sources of new nursing home beds. One is a conversion of surplus acute-patient beds in public hospitals and the other is the provision of new private nursing home beds. Nursing homes remain very much in the Commonwealth sphere; the public hospital sphere has been given back to the, States. Indeed, I can see active efforts by the States as they try to evade some of the financial difficulties they are now encountering by converting excess acute beds to beds for nursing home type patients. Instead of that kind of competition what we want in this matter is effective cooperation. These health decisions have made that effective co-operation between States and Federal Government more difficult. Again, every report has talked about the need to rationalise acute bed provision across the whole hospital sectorpublic and private. This has been rendered more difficult by taking the Commonwealth out of the area of determining priorities in the public hospital field while adding to its funding responsibilities in relation to the private sector.
Then there are the Department of Veterans' Affairs hospitals which are hospitals run by the Commonwealth Government but which are increasingly playing a bigger role in general community health provision. Co-operation between Veterans' Affairs hospitals and State public hospitals will be made more difficult by the Commonwealth's abdication of any responsibility for public hospitals. Again the possibility of building ~ national policy of support services for the fraIl aged has been rendered more difficult by giving community health care back to the States but by retaining home care services mainly as a federal responsibility.
These decisions are no answer to most of the problems we have in this field. They create new forms of duplication, new forms of overlap, new forms of competition. This system does not end divided control; it simply complicates it.
Despite the repeated utterances of the Health Minister, now echoed by the Treasurer in the quote from his speech I have previously given, the

3164 REPRESENTATIVES 4 June 1981

States (Tax Sharing and Health Grants) Bill

divided responsibility between the Commonwealth and the States is not the major factor in any inefficiencies in the public hospital sector. There is not very much hard evidence about specific inefficiencies in that sector. The evidence we have suggests that the problems lie in the impact on the costs of hospitals of fee for service procedures, the lack of positive incentives for efficiency, the lack of cost control and utilisation reviews and the inadequacy of budget reviews in hospitals. Certainly there is a need for better budgeting procedures. But none of these issues are tackled by the proposals outlined in the Minister's statement. They have been put into the too-hard basket and shoved back to the States. The Commonwealth Government virtually wipes its hands of all these problems. This is a totally negative contribution to the problems of hospital efficiency.
The only other contribution the Bill provides is the cutback in State funds. Again that is pretty negative. The cutbacks do not relate to efficiency. No effort has been made to penalise the more expensive or less efficient State hospital systems. There has been no effort to base funding on any per capita basis. The per capita health grants to the States range from $68 in Queensland to $168 in the Northern Territory and $118 in Western Australia. I am not saying that we will ever get perfect per capita allocation of grants, or that such would be desirable. But no effort was made in these arrangements to tackle that issue. One and only one criterion was behind the calculation and that was, the more developed the public sector in the public hospital the greater the cutback. Thus, the three States which received the largest cuts in their allocations-Queensland, Tasmania and the Northern Territory-were the three that had most fully developed the public sectors in their public hospitals. All three had 80 per cent or over of hospital or public patients in their public hospitals. Such a cutback plus the increased charges and means tests may reduce the health burden to the Commonwealth Government but it would not reduce the health burden to the community as a whole and it would be counterproductive in terms of efficiency. The last comment on this illogic is the kind of Bjelke-Petersen provident fund giving Queensland a $16m a cushion-to use the Treasurer's words. It is quite ironical that this money has been provided to Queensland to help it defeat the very objective which lies behind this set of health grant proposals. Finally, I quote again from the Treasurer-another major part of his argument, I suppose. He said that it was intended:

. . . to restrain the growth of public sector expenditure so as to make room for productive growth in the private sector without giving rise to inflationary pressures that could only damage the economic prospects for Australia.
In the short run that is quite untrue, at least in relation to the cost to the Commonwealth Government of the total health package. I refer to the whole health package because the Treasurer tells us that 'these proposals must be considered in the context of the overall revision of health care arrangements'. In 1981-82 expenditure in the Commonwealth health sector will be greater by about $400m as a result of the total impact of these changes on the Commonwealth public sector. That is because the $600m rebate and the $120m assumption of nursing home benefit by the Commonwealth funding far outweigh the gains on the hospital front. There are a few minor balancing items but the above three items are the major factors.
In the long run, although the expenditure figures will be hidden by absorption in general revenue grants to the States, the total effect of this package of health care changes will lead to an escalation in health care expenditure in the public sector; that is, taking the Commonwealth and States together. I think there is nothing in this package to prevent the whole community's total health expenditure passing $IO,OOOm in 1982-83. In essence the source of the problem is that the underwriting of private fee for service medicine and the private hospital system by a massive expansion of the private health insurance system is a recipe which will lead to an acceleration of health costs. It will certainly give rise to inflationary pressures, despite what the Treasurer has said. Health insurance contributions must rise this year as a result of increased bed charges for inpatients in public hospitals, as a result of increased payments for expensive medical procedures formerly performed in public hospitals by salaried staff, as a result of the rise in benefit refunds from 75 per cent to 85 per cent of the scheduled fee, as a result of a $25m reduction in the Commonwealth's subsidy to the reinsurance pool, and as a result of the rise in medical fees expected at the end of the year. All of those will push up the contribution rates for health insurance. There will be an offsetting factor in the assumption by the Commonwealth Government of all nursing home benefits. However, the General Manager of the Hospital Benefits Association has already suggested a possible rise of 47 per cent in the combined medical and hospital basic contribution rate. His expectations are that the rise will be around 47 per cent. The impact on the consumer price index of an increase of this order, or even one of a somewhat lesser magnitude, would be

States (Tax Sharing and Health Grants) Bill

4 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 3165

around one per cent. Clearly, we cannot accept the Treasurer's anticipation that these proposals will not give rise to inflationary pressures.
Finally, let me simply congratulate the Treasurer that in all his arguments he never advanced the cliche that somehow these proposals will improve health care in this country. At least he had the honesty never to mention that any of these proposals was likely to improve the health care delivered to ordinary Australians in this
country.
Mr TAMBLING (Northern Territory) (5.7)-1 wish to speak to the States (Tax Sharing and Health Grants) Bill now before the House, particularly as it relates to the implications for my electorate, the Northern Territory. As a member of the majority party in the Northern Territory Legislative Assembly which began the negotiation of the financial agreement between the Commonwealth and the Northern Territory governments in 1977,1 have a personal interest in and knowledge of the events leading up to the special provisions now incorporated in this financial Bill, specifically in respect of the Northern Territory. Initially, 24 local and State government type functions were transferred from the Commonwealth to the Northern Territory on 1 January 1977. The next important step was the 1977 Brisbane meeting between the Commonwealth and Territory Ministers. As I remember it, the Commonwealth party comprised Ministers Adermann, Carrick, Ellicott, Robinson and Viner. The Territory was represented by the then majority l(;ader, Dr Goff
Letts, and myself as the Deputy Leader with responsibilities for finance.
At that initial meeting it was established that one of the fundamental views and principles so far as the Commonwealth was concerned was that the special circumstances and the particular difficulties of the Northern Territory would be fully recognised and provided for in the funding arrangements and in establishing a sound financial basis for self-government in the Northern Territory. After many months of detailed negotiations and ministerial meetings, a Memorandum of Underst~nding became the formal document which was signed by Prime Minister Fraser and Northern Territory Chief Minister Everingham in June 1978. The agreement came into effect on Self-Government Day, 1 July 1978, and, inter alia, established a fiscus in the Northern Territory. Honourable members will recognise the importance of the agreement. It was fundamental to the Territory Government's agreeing to accept the Commonwealth's offer to move to self government. At the time of the early negotiations
in 1977 many Territorians were understandably

apprehensive about the likely cost to them in runnink a self-governing territory, given its smalll'evenue base and the prospect that at any time the Commonwealth may withdraw its financial support or fail to adhere to its agreement. Thus, the Memorandum of Understanding and the prospects of a sound financial future which we believe were set down in the Memorandum were critical to the whole success of the Northern Territory move into self-government.
Some of the key provisions in this important document, the Memorandum of Understanding, so far as it concerns this Bill, are set out in fine detail in paragraphs 1, 7, 8, 15, 19, 22 and 23. Honourable members will note in those details that particular reference is made to a formula for the calculation of the Territory's general revenue assistance grant. This formula sets out the Northern Territory's entitlement each year as the percentage increase in personal income tax collections multiplied by the percentage increase in the Northern Territory population. Under the new State tax reimbursement arrangements the words 'total tax collections' now apply in lieu of the previous reference to 'personal income tax col1ections'. I understand that the Territory has agreed to amend the corresponding reference in the Memorandum of Understanding so that 'total tax collections' is now to be read in place of 'net personal income tax collections'.
This amendment would leave unchanged the reference in the formula and in the other parts of the Memorandum to the population of the Northern Territory as an additional factor in the entitlement calculations. It will be noted that the reasons for this population factor in the funding arrangements which led to self-government were, 1 believe, in recognition of the tremendous backlog in the provision of adequate services in the Territory and the near impossibility facing the Territory Government in providing a reasonable standard of services to a rapidly growing population in the absence of the depth of infrastructure support that exists in the now-established States of our Commonwealth. In particular, given the very low revenue capacity of the Northern Territory and the very high percentage of its inhabitants who have little capacity to contribute towards State-type taxes, there was little prospect for the Territory being able within the foreseeable future to cope with a growing population and the realities of neglect by successive Federal governments. For that reason, the popUlation factor under the Memorandum of Understanding Was a fundamental provision which formed the basis for self-government of the Territory.

3166 REPRESENTATIVES 4 June 1981

States (Tax Sharing and Health Grants) Bill

The proposal now before the House recognises and maintains this principle and ensures the integrity of the arrangements. The specific clauses relating to the Northern Territory continue the recognition of this Government's commitment to the development of the Territory and its special disabilities of isolation. As part of the Government's wise tax sharing and economic management policies, the Territory, like the States, has had to accept a share of the Australia-wide financial constraints. Perhaps I should suggest that the Territory should have the same revenue-raising powers as the States to cushion the effect of the restraints. It may be time that the Territory Government be given the power to levy normal mining royalties on uranium.
I turn now to the provisions of the Bill dealing with health grants. I support the general intent of the legislation before us because it will give the States and Territories greater influence over the operation of their health services. It will reduce the spiralling costs of health services throughout Australia, as well as requiring the users of the services to contribute. It will enable more attention to be given to those essential health services which lie outside hospitals. However, there are areas of Australia where this global approach has not acknowledged significant difficulties, and of course I refer to the Northern Territory. The Northern Territory has been assessed by the Commonwealth to have the same proportion of disadvantaged hospital users as are found elsewhere in Australia. With some 50 per cent of Northern Territory hospital beds occupied by Aboriginal people, that is clearly not the case. The Northern Territory welcomed the Commonwealth's announcement that its assessment of the States' revenue-raising capacity would be reviewed later in the J981-82 financial year. This will provide the opportunity to have the Northern Territory's particular disability recognised and quantified for general revenue purposes. Similarly, clause 19 of the Bill will ensure a review of relativities by the Grants Commission.
Whilst we all recognise the need for major changes in the operation of health services throughout the nation, I do not believe it was the intention of the architects of these changes to disadvantage Aboriginal people; nor do I believe it was the intention to force the States to restrict services to Aboriginal people. The recent statement by the Minister for Health (Mr MacKellar) is certainly consistent with affording special recognition to Aboriginal health needs. The Northern Territory has a specific and quantifiable disability in this regard and, whilst supporting the overall intent of the new legislation, I point out to

honourable members that it is vital that the Northern Territory have this disability acknowledged. The Northern Territory has a case for topping-up of the funding level proposed under the new arrangement. This case does not dispute the decision to introduce charges, not does it dispute the proposed level of the charges. What is questioned is the statistics upon which the total potential revenue attributed to the Territory by the Commonwealth is based. The overriding factor affecting revenue raising for both inpatient and outpatient services in the Northern Territory is the high number of disadvantaged persons as a percentage of patient bed days and occasions of services.
Over the past J 5 years the Aboriginal population has represented a decreasing proportion of the total population of the Northern Territoryfrom 36 per cent in 1965 to 22 per cent 'in 1980. However, the number of Aboriginal in-patients has gradually increased as a proportion of total inpatients over the past four years, as is evideri<:ed by the following statistics: In 1976-77 the figure was 28.8 per cent; in 1977-78, 29.5 per cent; in 1978-79, 31.8 per cent and in 1979-80, 34.7 per cent. The trend in the first six months of 1980-81 shows that the percentage for this year will be approximately that of 1979-80. Whilst representing only 22 per cent of the total population and 35 per cent of total in-patients, the significant factor in relation to potential revenue calculations is the percentage of total bed days taken up by Aboriginal people. The average length of stay in hospital by Aboriginal patients is 10.1 days as compared with 5.4 days for the remainder of patients.
The proportion of occupied bed days relating to Aboriginal patients has increased steadily O,ver the past four years. Again I will give statistics: In 1976-77 the figure was 40.3 per cent; in 1977-78, 46.1 per cent; in 1978-79, 48.2 per cent and in 1979-80, 48.5 per cent. The trend in the first six months of \980-81 indicates that the percentage for this year will be approximately 49 per cent-a further increase over previous years. It is anticipated that the percentage for 1981-82 will be in the vicinity of 50 per cent. From these figures it follows that 50 per cent of potential revenue for in-patients applies to Aboriginal people and, therefore, cannot be collected.
No statistics are available on the percentages of bed days occupied by pensioners and other disadvantaged persons. However, factors to be considered are the high transient populationparticularly during the dry season-the majority of whom fall into the disadvantaged or bad debtor category, and the fact that a disproportionate number of patients resulting from accidents,

States (Tax Sharing and Health Grants) Bill

4 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 3167

brawls and a lack of hygiene and prevtmtative health measures come from the lower socio~ economic groups, the majority of whose members are disadvantaged. It is estimated that pensioners and disadvantaged persons lltiiisc 20 per cent of in-patient bed days in the Northern Territory. From the figures which I have just cited it follows that 70 per cent of in-patient bed days are not chargeable, which in turn means that any revenue calculation must be based on only 30 per cent of total bed days.
Within the suggested Commonwealth funding figure of $28m, it has been proposed that the Northern Territory should be able, by the introduction of charges on 50 per cent of services-the Commonwealth estimate--to raise $11 m. It is proposed to increase in-patient fees from $80 to $110 a day. That would raise $8m. It is also proposed to raise the charges for out-patients to $15. Based on charging for 50 per cent of total occasions of service, that would account for $3m, making a total of $llm. Compare these figures with the Northern Territory's own estimate for the same proposals; firstly, on the Commone wealth's assumption of 50 per cent of patients being chargeable and, secondly, on the Territory's estimate based on 30 per cent being chargeable. On the Northern Territory's estimate this would raise a total of only $5m. That would constitute a shortfall of $6m between the two estimates.
It is the policy of the Northern Territory Department of Health to discourage patients from using out-patient services and to channel them to private medical facilities. The results of this policy has been a marked increase in the number of private medical practitioners in the Territory over the past few years and a gradual decline, despite the population growth, in the number of outpatient occasions of service. As medically insured patients tend to use private medical practitioners, it follows that the percentage of treatments of disadvantaged patients as a proportion of the existing number of total occasions of service would be at least that which applies to in-patient services, if not greater. Therefore, the chargeable proportion of out-patient services is less than 30 per cent.
The estimates regarding cost sharing arrangements for 1981-82 are calculated on the following basis: Hospital cost-sharing arrangements, $27.13m; community health services, $588,000 and school dental services, $563,000. That makes a total of$28.281m. This compares with the Commonwealth estimate for the same activities for 1980-81 of $25m, plus a 10 per cent allowance for inflation of $3m, giving a total of $28m. On the basis of those calculations the Northern Territory

would have a shortfall of $281,000 on its requirements. From these figures it is evident that, on the basis of the Commonwealth's assumption, the $11m represents a $2.lm overestimation of the potential revenue, whilst on the basis of Northern Territory statistics the overestimation is
in the order of $6.3m. r am pleased to note that
the provisions as announced by the Minister will apply and that a review will take place later this year. As I have outlined, that is most important for the Northern Territory. In all other respects I believe the legislation which is before the House has recognised the requirements and the needs of the Northern Territory. I commend the Bills.
Mr UREN (Reid) (S.22)-The States (Tax Sharing and Health Grants) Bill and the Local Government (Personal Income Tax Sharing) Bill are being debated cognately. My colleague the honourable member for Bonython (Dr Blewett), as the Labor spokesman on health, dealt with the hea Ith grants legislation. As the Labor spokesman on urban affairs and local government matters I intend to concentrate on the local government personal income tax sharing legislation. At the outset I wish to say that Labor supports an active and adequately funded role for local government as an equal partner in the three-tier system of government. It was a Federal Labor government between 1972 and 1975 which originally was responsible for the breakthrough in giving direct finance to local government, which it had been seeking for many years. This Bill continues that principle and provides the machinery to allow local government a 2 per cent share of net personal income tax collections through general revetHle assistance. That is a provision that is supported on this side of the House as a necessary step towards encouraging a more active role for local government. In our view the amount of money which is being made available to local government is not sufficient. I believe national government has to go further.
Let me set out Labor's policy in relation to local government where it differs from that of the Government. Firstly, we would guarantee that the real level of funds made available-which provide the purchasing power of local government-would not fall below that of the previous year. In other words, we would guarantee that the 2 per cent share for the previous year would be paid in subsequent years. Secondly, Labor believes that 30 per cent of general revenue assistance should be distributed on a per capita basis and 70 per cent on the basis of need as assessed by the Commonwealth Grants Commission. Such a method of distributing payments to local government would take a proper account of the

3168 REPRESENTATIVES 4 June 1981

States (Tax Sharing and Health Grants) Bill

principle of equalisation with due regard to the differences in the cost disability of regions as a result of population structure, area, geography and topography. Of course, in our regions and our country they vary greatly. It is a great task which faces local government. I speak from personal experience of the years between 1972 and 1975 when I had direct contact with local government.
A Labor government will provide an additional program of special block grants to areas of need. Some regions which are associated with resources development are experiencing specific needs in relation to the problems of coping with expanding industrial activity and population growth. One of the great failings of this Government is that it has not been prepared to look at that situation. As a central government, it has put all its eggs into one basket-resource development. That has been the major thrust of its economic policy. It has thrown enormous responsibilities on to the States and local government.
Some areas affected by this policy are the Gladstone area, the Hunter Valley, the Lithgow area, the Latrobe Valley and the Portland area. There has been accelerated growth in these areas. On Tuesday I spoke about the housing problems that exist in Gladstone. At least one-quarter of the people in that area are are living in caravans. One can see the other social problems resulting from a lack of infrastructure. The Federal Government has not taken on the role of ensuring that the resource development companies pay their fair share in assisting local government. It has a pass the buck mentality.

problems. They can be solved only by the Australian Government, State governments and local government working together. These problems often do not have a local origin. They are problems that flow from the broad decisions made by the corporate sector relating to the location, nature and scale of their investment. They also flow from decisions made by the Australian Government in relation to economic management. Consequently, these problems require the co-operation of and intervention by governments at all levels with an active participation of regional communities.
The thrust of this Government's approach to inter-governmental relations, and especially relations between the Federal, and local levels of government, merely relates to a flow of money. The former Department of Urban and Regional Development had built up a strong relationship between the Australian Government, State governments and local government. We worked in a spirit of co-operation to solve many of the problems. This Government has washed its hands. It has withdrawn from the arena. It has handed over responsibility to the States and local government by saying: 'Here is the money. You can solve the problems'. Many of these problems have been caused by decisions made here in the national capital or in other countries by overseas corporate bodies. But it is the people in the local communities who have to take the brunt of those decisions.

Other regions are also facing problems associated with the contraction of manufacturing activities because the Government has placed all its eggs into the resource development basket. The enormous increases in exports as a result of the mineral resource development have affected manufacturing industries in many regional areas. Ballarat, Bendigo and Geelong certainly feel the squeeze by this Government. The closure of rail services, post offices, banks and public sector activities are other problems associated with this contraction.
In order for the Government to retain its viability it should assess the needs and the potential of those regions and intervene to establish viable and durable employment-generating activities. While these locally felt problems require local responses they also require national responses. I say to the Treasurer (Mr Howard), who is sitting at the table, that the sad situation is that the Government has withdrawn from trying to solve these

The reduction in specific purpose payments has meant a reduction in the amount of contact between the bureaucrats at different levels of government. I have stressed that often. The national government has cut itself off from essential contact with the people by not assisting in the design and implementation of programs to meet the real needs of the people. To give honourable members some idea of the Government's withdrawal, particularly in relation to specific purpose grants, I seek leave to incorporate in Hansard a table headed 'Commonwealth Payments to the States for Local Government 1975-76 to 1979-80', which has been taken from the Budget Papers.
Leave granted.

Mr UREN-I thank the House. Because of the
slowness in information coming through from the Government I have only given the figures up to 1979-80. The figures for 1980-81 are not available at this stage. If one looks at the table one will note that in 1975-76 the amount of money allocated to
the States for local government by the Labor Government including the amount allocated for roads was 4.5 per cent of total income tax revenue. Excluding the percentage allocated for roads, the figure was 3.5 per cent. Excluding the
percentage allocated for roads and the percentage allocated for the Regional Employment Development scheme the figure was 2.2 per cent. The percentage to be allocated this year will be, at best, 2 per cent, plus a small percentage for specific purpose grants. That figure is minute. So, as a percentage of income tax collectons in the
previous year, it is still below the percentage that the Labor Government made available to local

government in 1975-76. Because the national Government has withdrawn from its responsibilities, the onus is being placed more and more on the States and local government and the burden on local government is becoming greater.
With the withdrawal of the Federal Government from its responsibilities and expenditures on social services and the provision of social amenities, local government authorities are forced into a position of either allowing the rundown of those services or increasing rates in order to continue the services. Under the Federal Government the social needs in the majority of regions have grown larger and become more neglected. Local government bodies are called on to meet emergency housing needs, to provide children's services, to care for aged persons, to provide community information services and so on. At the same time, in a number of regions, local government authorities

3170 REPRESENTATIVES 4 June 1981

States (Tax Sharing and Health Grants) Bill

are having to pay a higher proportion of their outlays for the provision of infrastructure associated with resource projects or expanded commercial activity. So their funds are squeezed at both ends. In recent years we have seen a large increase in the borrowing programs of the Federal Government and the semi-government authorities. These borrowings on the domestic capital market have been in competition with larger authorities and corporations seeking funds.
The high levels of foreign capital inflow in recent times in the context of a restrictive monetary policy on the part of this Government have created conditions in the domestic capital market which have disadvantaged the smaller borrowers, whether they be home buyers, small businessmen, small farmers or small local government authorities. They have all experienced less security in gaining access to finance and they have all had to pay higher interest rates. These are the realities facing local government authorities throughout our nation. But they are realities that this Government either cannot see or chooses to neglect. The situation is that semi-government authorities are paying interest rates of 13.4 per cent to 13.9 per cent for their money. There is every indication that that rate will rise still further. That enormous problem is being thrown on to locai'gov~rnment. In the long term people have to pay for it in the form of increased rates.
The other sad thing about which I am concerned is that this Government-a government of market forces--allows the market to determine :vhat :vill develop without any planning. That in Itself IS of great concern to me. In a recent statement by the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs (Mr Macphee) on popUlation trends we find a great challenge to local government and to the States. Population growth is controlled basically by the national Government and not by those people at a lower level. If we look at that statement we see that 35 per cent of the 14.6 million people living in this country are under the age of 20 years. Imagine the profound implications for future planning. In addition, by the turn of the century there will be a 66 per cent-or two thirds-increase in the aged popUlation and the net annual intake of immigrants will be between 80,000 and 90,000. Just imagine the social mix and the great problems that will create at the grass roots level of local government. That is another problem which this Government is transferring to local government. By the turn of the century the population will be between 18.4 million and 19.6 million.
The previous time we looked at this situation was in the mid-1970s when we dealt with the

Borrie report. It was then said that Australia would have a population of between 16.5 million and 17.3 million by the year 2000. The projection has increased to between 18.4 million and 19.6 million. That will have immense implications for the infrastructure needed in the States and particularly by local government. At the very least Australia is faced with the need to duplicate the provision of the urban infrastructure of the past 10 years into the next decade. We will have to face also the problems of a changing popUlation mix on the basis of age, sex and country of origin. We also have to consider the growing problem of dislocation caused by the changing distribution of population. There are structural changes in population in this country, particularly, as I have said, in those new resource development areas. How can Australia cope with these emerging problems when the Government is dismantling the machinery for handling these problems, when the Government is restricting the provision of information and is cutting back ruthlessly on essential social expenditures? So I say clearly that even though we support the 2 per cent increase to local government in this Bill it is not enough. There has to be greater interrelationship between State and local governments and between the private sector and community groups if we are to overcome the difficult, social problems that are growing at an alarming rate at the grass roots level of local government. I criticise this Government's inactivity in that area. We want to see more interrelationship.
Dr JENKINS (Scullin) (5.39)-1 enter this debate briefly. I understand we have an agreement to finish the debate in about eight or nine minutes' time. I want to add some remarks on local government. There is a degree of bipartisanship to these grants to local government. There is a recognition on both sides of the House of the nature of local government; that it is grass roots; that it is sensitive to basic community needs; and that the people who have to carry out the tasks of local government are subject to strong electoral pressures as those pressures are very close to home. Yet what happens in local government is so sensitive to what happens at State and Federal government levels. The percentage of grants going to local government has risen from 1.25 per cent in 1976 to 1.75 per cent in 1979 and to 2 per cent in 1980. Local government has conducted a very active campaign to get this level of grants.
However, having reached this stage we have to ask the question: What is the basis for the increase of 2 per cent? What recent assessments of needs and tasks imposed on local government have been

States (Tax Sharing and Health Grants) Bill

4 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 3171

made to assess the appropriate level? Has the 2 per cent been looked at relevant to the abolition of other special purpose programs which were utilised by local government? After all, this is a cognate debate which includes the matter of health grants to States. These changes will affect some of the tasks that are now carried out by local government. Many of these tasks are of the welfare type. Little accountability is built into any of these measures; so we are not sure how effective they are in the cost sense. However, I have a subjective view that most of these programs in local government areas would be cost effective. People at the local grass roots level have a feel for what is going on, which reduces reaction time bureaucracy and administrative costs.
When I talked to those people involved in local government in my area the general feeling was that there has been a real decrease in funding to local government which has put it in an insecure situation. There was a feeling of resentment that cutbacks in programs and the whole question of the level of funding had been made without any consultation with local government representatives. Obviously the Federal Government takes the attitude that local government is a State function. In fairness, in many ways it is. As local government bodies play such an important part in the nature of national development and the quality of life for ordinary people, surely some degree of consultation is possible between representatives of Federal government, State government and local government. Local government delivers services to people. In the present circumstances some councils will have to increase rates substantially to provide those services. Others may seek to maintain increases in municipal rates with the increases in inflation rates. This would be possible only through an internal manipulation of finance and services.
One of the problems that bedevils local government-at least in Victoria-is, naturally, the periodic valuations of property by the State Valuer-General which must lead to marked rate increases. Different municipalities have different needs. Some are stable, with maintenance posing the main problem. Others are still growing at a rate which requires a greater demand for services. I know that the various State Grants Commissions, in making their allocations to municipalities, have taken all these sorts of criteria into consideration. Those who are slicing up the cake have no say in the size of the cake that is baked.
I remind honourable members of the vast backlog in the provision of facilities such as sewerage, roads, recreation facilities and so on that had accumulated by the early 1970s. Surely there is a

national responsibility to see that that backlog never reaches the same sort of dimensions again. Even a simple matter like waste disposal can produce a marked drain on the finances of local government bodies. Yet rationalisation of these services is more than just a matter for a particular municipality. Land fills are the most common method. A suitable disused quarry hole can cost from $500,000 to $lm. There is some cost recovery in the long term from ratepayers and other municipalities using it but in the short term the financial effect on a local government body can be severe. Federal and State governments have pushed more and more welfare functions on to local government. I illustrate this by citing some figures that refer to the City of Preston. Since the financial year 1976-77 the welfare budget of that municipality has increased by 310 per cent. The actual expenditure figures are as follows: 1976-77, $310,000; 1977-78, $393,000; 1978-79, $577,000; 1979-80, $676,000; and now, in 1980, $838,000.
Just as there has been an increase in expenditure of 310 per cent, the portion of expenditure obtained from State and Federal government sources has pecreased, the expenditure for which the council itself is responsible having increased. The result is that quite apart from rate increases due to inflation another quantum of rate increases has had to be applied to maintain these services. Many of the services are highly labour intensive. For example, 20 per cent of the wages for maternal and child services have to be met by the local government authorities. Changes in the classification of roads, which this Government has implemented, have meant a 7.9 per cent drop in Commonwealth funding for local roads. This has slowed their development. One of the other municipalities covered by my electorate, the shire of Whittlesea, has a southern developing residential area and a substantial northern rural area. Population growth means demand for welfare services and rural areas mean expenditure is constantly required for roads and so on. All this income tax share means to those municipalities is that they have an amount to cover increased expenditure due to inflation, so keeping rate expenditure increases at a level of about 11 per cent to 12 per cent. A councillor for the Shire of Diamond Valley suggested to me that a program under threat because of these changes, as well as the changes in health funding to the States, was the dental scheme for pre-school children and other children. The Treasurer (Mr Howard), in his second reading speech, said:
Firstly, the States should assume their full constitutional responsibility for the provision of health services . . . .

3172 REPRESENTATIVES. 4 June 1981

States (Tax Sharing and Health Grants) Bill

However section 51 (xxiiiA.) of the Constitution reads:
" The provision of . . .' medical and dentalservices.
So, who has responsibility for these things? This Government also h3$ a responsibility. I have to finish my remarks. One could say much more about the needs of local government. I want to emphasise the lack of consultation with local government at Federal and State level. There shoul!! be co-operation to look at the tasles imposed on local government to see on what the figure of 2 per cent is based. This Government should recognise sufficiently the needs of that level of government iil the carrying out of so many functions that affect the ordinary individual.

Indeed, one of the principal' reasons for the decisions taken by the Goyernment wa,s the very rapid real increases in general revenue payments. that have accrued to the States since 1976. They include real increases of 7.8 per cent, 7.5 per cent, 3 per cent, 3.6 per cent and 0.3 per cent in the five years that the Fraser Government has been in qffice. One has to look at that trend and at the decisions affecting the overall payments to the States in the light of the relatively high base of paymentS to the States, which the present Government inherited; the revel1ue raising caPllcity of the States themselves, as evidenced by the taxation reductions that many of them have been able to provide, and also the very significant increases in levels of expenditure which Were acknowledged by the honourable member for Gellibrand.

It was a decision of the States, and of the States alone, that they would meet separately from the Commonwealth and together put a submission on what they thought the new arrangements should be. At the time that decision was taken-of course, it was their perfect right to

take it-the Commonwealth indicated, and repeated that indication on several occasions, that it would be better if the States and the Territory did not meet separately but rather involved the Commonwealth in all meetings on the subject. In the course of the remarks of the honourable member for Gellibrand he quoted some comments attributed to the New South Wales Premier following the Premiers Conference on 4 May. One of those quotes referred to a statement by Mr Wran in which he purportedly rejected the concept of total tax sharing. That rejection by Mr Wran-+-I recall the remark very well-sits rather oddly with the States' written submission to the Commonwealth, to which Mr Wran was the first signatory. In part, that submission read:
. . . there are arguments to be made out for the adoption of total Commonwealth taxation collections as the revenue base. One of the advantages of such a scheme would be that the States would be 'insulated' from the effects of shifts in emphasis between personal income tax and other Commonwealth taxes.
Whilst I am not denying for a moment that the amount to be received by the States would have been more had the previous arrangement been continued, equally one has to bear in mind that the States themselves had argued on many occasions for the need to have total taxation as the revenue base, and had in fact criticised the vulnerability of reliance on personal income tax.

COMMONWEALTH FUNCTIONS (STATUTES REVIEW) BILL 198.1
Second Reading Debate resumed from 27 May, on motion by MrViner:
The the Bill be now read a'second time.
Mr HOWARD (Bennelong-Treasurer)-Mr Deputy Speaker, may I have your indulgence to suggest that the House have a general debate covering this Bill and the Income Tax (Assessment and Rates) Amendment Bill as they are associated measures. The separate questions will, of course, by put on each of the Bills at the col)- elusion of the debate.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Millar)-Is it the wish of the House to have a general debate covering these two measures? There being no objection, I will allow that course to be followed.
Mr WILLIS (Gellibrand) (8.0)-The Bills now before the House give effect to those decisions of the so-called razor gang which require legislative action and some associated measures.

3174 REPRESENTATIVES 4 June 1981 Commonwealth Functions (Statutes Review) Bill

They also give effect to some contradictory measures. The razor gang's decisions represent part of the Government parties desperate attempt to portray themselves as small government parties. They are highly embarrassed that despite all their small government rhetoric the public sector is bigger now than it ever was when the Australian Labor Party was in office. Associated with the Government parties' desire to be seen as small government parties is their desire to be seen as low tax parties. Taxes, as a proportion of gross domestic product, are higher now than they have ever been before in our history. Thus, with regard to both expenditure and taxation the Government is having great trouble in getting its performance in line with its rhetoric. An important measure in the Bills now before the House will ensure that the tax level will increase even further in future years.
The details of the Bills are complex. I refer firstly to the Income Tax (Assessment and Rates) Amendment Bill, which can be broken up into three categories. Firstly, it relates to cutbacks in tax concessions for industry, the main ones of which are in the investment allowance and the special depreciation rates. Both reductions are of a fairly small order. The second category relates to the introduction of a health insurance rebate, which is not opposed by the Opposition. Neither are the reductions in various tax concessions for industry opposed by the Opposition. The Treasurer (Mr Howard) has said that the health insurance rebate will be of great benefit to families in Australia. But this will depend on the rise of health insurance rates following the large increase in public hospital charges. That rise could be very high. The House will recall that prior to the suspension of the sitting the Opposition spokesman on health, the honourable member for Bonythan (Dr Blewett), informed the House that one senior official of a major health insurance organisation in Australia had estimated that its rates would Increase by 47 per cent as a result of the decisions of this Government to change the form of health funding. Thus, the health insurance rebate must be seen in the light of such possible increases. We should also consider why a tax concession has been given rather than a subsidy. The Government is abolishing or reducing subsidies in the hospital and medical area. It is reducing the subsidy for medical services over $20 and the subsidy for hospital care, thus making health costs and, therefore, health insurance more expensive. That in turn will put up the inflation rate. It is ironic that the rationale for such action is to reduce inflation, that is to reduce the Government's deficit. The Government is cutting back on health funding to reduce the deficit in various ways

which it says will be anti-inflationary. In fact, that very process increases health insurance costs and feeds into the consumer price index. Therefore, it increases inflation and has the opposite effect to that which is intended. It is another of many examples that there have been during the Fraser Government's period of office of a policy of fighting inflation by increasing prices.
By making health insurance more expensive before tax it is more likely that lower income earners will take a punt and not take out health insurance even though an subsidies for medical services are being removed and free public hospital treatment in shared wards is also being ended. Such people would be taking an enormous risk. They could face mammoth bills if they became really ill, but they may still take the risk because there are so many pressures on small incomes and so many other expenses that must be met. Many members of parliament, living on relatively high incomes, have probably forgotton what a struggle people on relatively low incomes have to make ends meet. When there arc such pressures, some of which cannot be avoided and others which can be put off in the hope that the expenditure will not be necessary, it is the second type of expenditure that is likely to be posponed. In that situation it is quite likely that people on low incomes will take a risk, in particular because of the high cost of health insurance. That risk could be terribly damaging for them if they became ill. This is so despite the fact that very low income earners will be able to receive free hospital and medical care.
There is, of course, a provision in the change in the health funding system for those on low incomes to get free care in the future.The income limits for a single person are $96 a week and for a married couple $160 a week with $20 for each additional child. A couple with two children could have a family income of $200 a week and receive free cover. But with an income of just over $200 people face very large costs in taking out health insurance and, therefore, are likely to be frightened otT from taking it out. One must ask the Government: Why do it in this way? Why provide tax concessions to people for health insurance which come after they have made the initial expenditure rather than a subsidy which would reduce the initial cost and, therefore, make it far less likely that the lower income people would take a risk and not take out health insurance? Clearly, the Government's intention is that most people, should take out health insurance, but the very way in which it has gone about encouraging this will ensure that quite a few people do not. The reason it has gone about it in this way is that it

The third sector of the Income Tax (Assess-
ment and Rates) Amendment Bill deals with tax indexation. This is a very important aspect. The Government proposes to abandon tax indexation after the next financial year. That, of course, is completely opposite to the Government parties' rhetoric on being low tax parties. There are various points to make in this regard. Firstly, the Government has attempted to sneak in the abolition of tax indexation along with the change in health funding and the introduction of the tax rebate for health insurance, At the same time as the

indexation there is automatic protection against that. Thus to remove tax indexation is to remove the protection to taxpayers from such automatic tax rises. This is so even if, as has been the case, it were not fully implemented. Of course, we have never had full tax indexation. In 1976-77 we had 92 per cent tax indexation; in the next year it was 79 per cent; then 35 per cent; then nothing in
1979-80; and 39 per cent in the current financial year. The average indexation over that period is 49 per cent or about half. Although for two of those years it was supposedly full tax indexation.

The further point to make in this regard is that the impact of only 49 per cent application of tax indexation in the past five years has been substantial. For example, four taxpayers out of five taxpayers-that is, the bottom 80 per cent-are now paying a higher percentage of their income in

up. As I said, tax indexation gives automatic proing less of their income in income tax because of a

3176 REPRESENTATIVES 4 June 1981 Commonwealth Functions (Statutes Review) Bill

taxpayers on lower incomes. The bottom 80 per cent of taxpayers are paying more of their income in tax now than they did in 1975. With total abolition of tax indexation in the future and with only 38 per cent tax indexation to 1981-82, that proportion of income paid in tax will rise even further.
The Government will say, of course, that it will be able to make discretionary changes at Budget time. Why abandon tax indexation in the first place if it intends to make fully compensating discretionary changes? It simply does not add up. The abolition of tax indexation also has implications for wages policy. If there is no tax indexation the real take-home pay of wage and salary earners is reduced by the bigger automatic tax bite. That can fuel wage claims to compensate for that reduction in real take-home pay. That in turn has obvious implications for inflation. That is a very important argument for tax indexation and one which the Minister for Foreign Affairs (Mr Street) recognised in 1975 when, as Opposition spokesman on industrial relations, he said that tax indexation was essential as a support for wage indexation. That statement was made in the days when the Liberal and National Country parties pretended they supported wage indexation as well as tax indexation. Of course, commitment to wage indexation went by the board many years ago, almost as soon as it started, and tax indexation has hung on much longer before going the way of most of this Government's promises.
In regard to the abolition of tax indexation, I note no real reasons have been given by the Treasurer for such an abolition. We are getting used to having no reasons given for government action as promised by the Treasurer at the second reading stage of the debate. It is unusual to get such reasons. One would think when a very important decision like this is made-a fundamental promise of the Fraser Government in past years was to adopt a system which it claimed was terribly important to the people of Australia-that reasons to abandon such a system would be given. No reason was given in the second reading speech. The subject took up four paragraphs. If indexation were so important in the past why has it been

abandoned now? In a speech on 29 April when the Treasurer announced the decision that tax indexation would be abandoned he mentioned that tax indexation could be said to place an inhibition on government Budget flexibility. This is clearly nonsense. There is nothing to stop the Government from making discretionary tax changes when a system of tax indexation operates. That is quite possible to do. It simply means that in addition to that discretionary change there would have to be tax indexation changes to the Schedule as well, certainly if the full amount of flexibility in regard to discretionary changes remains. It is absurd to say it does not.
In total, I say that the abolition of tax indexation has been a shabby exercise indeed. The implementation of half indexation for 1981-82 is also proposed in the legislation. Of course, this will be the final occasion since the indexation will be removed from the legislation at the same time as it is being implemented for this last year. What is supposedly half indexation becomes 38 per cent indexation for this year by a process of obtaining a 9.9 per cent increase in the consumer price index for the 12 months to March 1981 against the 12 months to March 1980. By taking away 2.3 per cent for government-induced price increasesthat is, from increased oil prices and health insurance costs, which means the Government is responsible for about a quarter of the inflation rate directly, on its own admission-and then halving the 7.6 per cent which remains as part of the process of what is supposedly half tax indexation we are left with a figure of 3.8 per cent. The effect of less than half tax indexation is to bring about a situation in which the tax paid by wage and salary earners and other income earners will increase in 1981-82. We are not seeing a situation of tax cuts but a situation in which the proportion of the income paid in tax will increase. I seek leave to incorporate in Hansard two tables which show the impact on taxpayers of a lack of full indexation in 1981-82.

Mr WILLIS-I thank the House. Because of time constraints I will not go into the details of the tables. They show clearly that at all income levels there is an increase in the proportion of income paid in tax and an increase in the amount of money tax which far exceeds the assumed rate of increase of income which for the purposes of the table is 12 per cent. It is clear that only partial tax indexation is not full protection against the secret tax of inflation, but at least it is some protection. When it is abolished entirely automatic rises in tax will far exceed increases in income. When the Treasurer in his second reading speech announced the partial implementation of tax indexation for 1981-82 he released some tables showing the impact on taxpayers and showed tax cuts at various levels. Of course, these tax cuts were only the money tax cuts which will occur from 1 July. They disguise the real impact of what has occurred which, because there is not a sufficient money tax cut from 1 July, means that in 1981-82 there will be an increase in the proportion of income paid in tax by all taxpayers in that year.
The second Bill which is before us is the Commonwealth Functions (Statutes Review) Bill

1981. This Bill is a very unusual Bill which incorporates a wide range of legislative changes, most of those being changes announced by the Prime Minister in presenting the report of the Review of Commonwealth Functions setting the matters which require legislative action and which are not tax measures. The Review of Commonwealth Functions was carried out by the Cabinet committee known as the razor gang. The review announced 350 decisions regarding government expenditure cutbacks, some of which are farsically minor and some of which are very important indeed.
It is not possible in the time available to deal with these measures in any detail, so one can only pick out a few which are of note and look at the motives of the Government in making those cutbacks in expenditure. There are various categories of decision-the transfers to the private sector, such as the sale of the Belconnen Mall here in Canberra or the sale of the Housing Loans Insurance Corporation; deregulation of various spheres of activity by such action as the winding up of the Prices Justification Tribunal or reducing the activities of the Trade Practices Commission;

3178 REPRESENTATIVES 4 June 1981 Commonwealth Functions (Statutes ReView) Bill

reduction of assistance to industry by cutbacks in the Export Expansion Grants Scheme; and transferrence of various functions to the States-this is, in fact, a euphemism for doing much less about various programs and leaving the States to take up those programs if they so wish-such as the abolition of the Urban Public Transport Assistance Program. The original report by the Prime Minister also contained a long list of internal cutbacks in the operations of the Commonwealth but those do not require legislative action; nor, of course, do any other decisions announced in the report as these can be implemented by Executive direction.
The attitude of the Opposition to this Bill is that we oppose it, although we are not implacably opposed to some of its individual provisions; for instance, the abolition of the receiver of rabbit scalps in the Australian Capital Territory is not one which disturbs us greatly particularly as we understand that no such rabbit scalps have been received for many years. However, there are many provisions of much greater importance than that. We oppose the Bill because we believe it is based on a political and economic philosophy which is ill-conceived, ill-fated and which will cause increased hardship and divisiveness in our society as the role of government is diminished. Let me make it absolutely clear that we are not opposed to measures designed to improve the efficiency of the public sector. Indeed, we positively want it to be as efficient as possible and to abolish waste in government departments and instrumentalities.
As I have mentioned on a previous occasion the Labor Party, in government and in opposition, has made quite clear its devotion to increasing the efficiency of the public sector. But we cannot support the Bill before us now because, with minor exceptions, this Bill is not about improving the efficiency of the public sector so much as it is about diminishing and dismemberingthe public sector. Such action is based on several completely false assumptions. One such assumption is that the freedom of the individual is increased by the diminution of government. Thus, the Prime Minister in particular is fond of uttering such inanities. To hear him speak, to read his speeches, one would think that freedom is maximised in a country like Chile, for instance, because it had implemented the monetarist dogma of Milton Friedman and slashed its level of government expenditure. The fact that the country is run by a military dictatorship apparently does not matter because the important thing is to cut back on government expenditure, which maximises the freedom of the individual.

It is clearly a totally simplistic and absurd proposition to argue that freedom is a product of reduced government activity because there is less regulation of our daily lives and we have more of our own economy to spend. In a society increasingly marked by monopoly and oligopoly, as all Western economies are, we need government interference in the market-place in order to produce freedom for those without economic power. The credo of non-interference is the credo of the rich and the powerful. Those people are quite happy to say: 'Don't interfere; everything is great for us'. If we are considering the freedom of people to spend their own money, what of the freedom of those without much money, those who need welfare services or other public services in order to be able to live a decent life? Insofar as freedom to live a decent life depends on having an adequate income, we must consider the freedom not only of those with money and paying taxes but also those without it. Clearly the latter will not be free if the government does not intervene to ensure that they have an adequate income both in cash and in kind through various public services.
So we totally repudiate the nonsense line that diminution of government in Australia is necessary to make us more free. That would have the possibility of truth only if we had an extremely large public sector. But we simply do not have such a large public sector; in fact, we have one of the smallest public sectors in the whole of the Western world-far below those of the advanced European countries, even well below that of the United States of America. The only advanced country with a smaller public sector than ours is Japan. But there is a tradition of lifetime employment by many large enterprises in that country with workers in sickness and old age being provided for by the company and with many services normally provided by governments in other countries being provided by the company during the working lives of the Japanese people. So Japan is a clear exception. If anyone were to compare us with any other advanced country he would see that om public sector is quite small. To argue that our public sector must be reduced to increase freedom in Australia simply defies credibility.
A second point to make in this regard is the fact that our public sector is small is also relevant to the argument that big government in Australia is crowding out private enterprise and not giving it room to grow. It is clear that our public sector has far more room to grow than that of many other Western countries because we have a much smaller public sector. Yet the Government argues that the public sector is crowding out the private sector both physically and financially. When the

Commonwealth Functions (Statutes Review) Bill 4 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 3179

Government says the public sector is physically crowding out the private sector it means that it is taking over the physical resources-labour and capital-which are not then available to private enterprise to utilise. But it is difficult to believe that that can be so when there is' still quite a high level of unemployment. There is much unused capacity in Australia. We have had a very large increase in private investment in the past year. Why is there such a large increase in private investment in Australia if the investors are scared that there are not the physical resources available with which to utilise that investment money?
Quite clearly, it just does not make sense to argue that there has been physical crowding out by having a large government sector in Australia in the past, nor can the argument be sensibly made out in respect of financial crowding out by which is meant that the Government by having a large public sector borrowing requirement forces the interest rates up by competing with private investors for the available capital. The fact is, however, that the public sector borrowing requirement has fallen substantially as a percentage of gross domestic product over the past couple of years. It peaked in 1978-79 at 5.6 per cent; it is now down to 3.5 per cent. That means that there has been a significant reduction and that the argument about financial crowding out has far less relevance now than it might have had a couple of years ago. The government need to borrow has fallen relative to total money value of output of the nation. The financial crowding out argument, therefore, is clearlydiminished in weight. There are other reasons for the interest rate rises which are occurring at the present time, and they have a great deal to do with this Government's monetary policy rather than with the size of the Government's public sector borrowing requirement.
A further assumption underlying this legislation is that a reduction in the public sector is necessary in order to provide room for real tax cuts. This again is simply not valid. The Government can, as an alternative, increase the tax burden on those who have a clear capacity to pay, such as resource companies which should have applied to them a resource rent tax which this Government continually refrains from introducing despite enormous profits in some areas. We could take much stronger action against tax avoidance. We could cut out unnecessary tax concessions for business.
!VIr Howard-Come on. Mr WILLIS-Well, the Minister has not hit that one on the head. I will deal with that one tomorrow.

Mr HowardI am waiting with bated breath.
Mr WILLIS --1 bet the Minister is.
Mr Howard-I am. I am really waiting with bated breath,
Mr WILLIS-That is terrific. The Minister will be enlightened tomorrow. We certainly argue that the Government should cut out unnecessary tax concessions to business. The Government can also create more'room for tax cuts if it can generate more economic growth. In that regard, until recently growth in Australia has been abysmally low under this Government. What has happened is that the Government has increased taxes to provide the revenue that extremely low growth rates by comparison with other Western countries have denied the Government. Faster growth, if maintained, will allow tax cuts to occur without the slashing of important government expenditures. In any case, how ironic is it that one of the Bills before the House, the Commonwealth Functions (Statutes Review) Bill is justified at least partly on the ground that it makes room for tax cuts, whilst at the same time the other Bill, byabolishing tax indexation, increases taxes.
It is also assumed that the private sector by its very nature is more efficient than the public sector. No proof is provided for that. The sell-off of such bodies as the Housing Loans Insurance Corporation is based on that kind of assumption. There is absolutely no reason to believe that that Corporation will be run more efficiently by private enterprise than it has been by the Government. No one in the Government has attempted to show that that would be the case. I look forward with interest to someone proving in this debate that the Corporation will be run more efficiently by private enterprise. There are other points to be made, but time is against me. I foreshadow an amendment to the motion for the second reading of the Income Tax (Assessment and Rates) Amendment Bill in the following terms:
That all words after 'That' be omitted with a view to substituting the following words:
'whilst not opposing the second reading of this Bill, the House notes that:
(a) because a tax indexation factor of only 3.8% has been applied, all taxpayers whose incomes in 1981-82 will be more than 3.8% above their 1980-81 incomes and whose circumstances do not otherwise alter, will be paying a higher proportion of that income in income tax in 1981-82 than in 1980-81;
(b) such an increase in the burden of income tax continues the pattern of an increasing income tax burden during the Fraser Government's term of office; and
(c) such developments are in stark contrast to the Government's continual attempts to portray itself as a low tax party,
and accordingly deplores the Government's action in abolishing tax indexation altogether for future years, which indicates

3180 REPRESENTATIVES 4 June 1981 Commonwealth Functions (Statutes Review) Bill

its clear intention to make full use of the secret tax of inflation'.
