Clauses I to 89-by leave-taken together.
Mr SCHOLES (Corio) (8.40)-1 think that it is a condemnation of the Parliament that these clauses are before the Committee tonight. It is a condemnation of the lack of a system of government and the lack of consideration of the democratic rights and obligations of the people of the Australian Capital Territory that these clauses include matters, none of which are Commonwealth functions anywhere else in Australia except in the Territory in which this Parliament House stands, This is a Territory where no democratic rights at all exist for citizens, those which did exist having been taken away over the last three years.
Mr Donald Cameron-That is not true. They chose not to have self-government, and you know it.
Mr SCHOLES-If the honourable member's electorate had its democratic rights taken away and if he were not here it would be a big advantage. In this case the people of the Territory have chosen very good representatives. Unfortunately, they are not allowed to govern their own Territory, nor are they allowed to have a say in matters which would normally be dealt with by State governments and local government bodies. At the time of the retirement in frustration of the former Minister for Post and Telecommunications, Mr Staley, proposals were in hand to give a measure of self-government and responsibility to the citizens of the Australian Capital Territory. If I may say so, for the information of honourable members opposite, democracy is not something which one chooses or does not choose. An obligation rests on all of us not only to support democracy but also to be a part of it. I do not believe that in a society which claims to be democratic the citizens should accept dictatorship be-. cause they think it might be a little cheaper. I doubt whether it is cheaper. I think that they have found out now just how hollow the se1fgovernment referendum was.
Let us look at what the citizens of the Australian Capital Territory are losing. If this Bill were to apply to any other area in Australia the Government would have to go through the processes of consultation with local government

Commonwealth Functions (Statutes Review) Bill 4 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 3181

and State governments and have State governments approve these clauses of the Bill. It is a tyrannical Bill, a Bill which the locally elected representatives who have no authority in government in this Territory have not even been allowed to express a firm opinion upon. This is a Territory where ordinances are made at the whim of the Government, where the financial arrangements for the allocation of funds within the Territory are denied. Even details of the raising of funds are denied to the elected members of the House of Assembly. J have a document here, which J will mention later in the evening, in which the Assembly refers to what it thinks are the funds raised from taxes collected in this Territory and the available funds. Members of the Assembly are not privy to the information because this Government has decided to impose on this Territory a completely totalitarian regime. The elected representatives of the Australian Capital Territory House of Assembly have no democratic rights. They have no right of a say in the distribution of the taxation which is collected in this area and in the manner in which matters affecting their day to day lives are decided.
Mr Cadman-That was their decision.
Mr SCHOLES-it was not the decision of the people at all, and the honourable member knows it. They were threatened with massive tax increases if they denied the Government's wish that they forgo self-government. Greed is not an excuse for the denial of democracy, nor is convenience, which is the excuse the Government chooses to use.
In dealing with major legislation, the national Parliament is discussing the waters rates in Canberra. Are the water rates of other electorates discussed in this Parliament? Of course not! Locally elected bodies and State governments deal with those functions elsewhere. Agents and architects controls-State functions and functions which are properly the responsibility of the people in the area-are dealt with in this Bill.
The disposal of the Canberra Commercial Development Authority in itself is worthy of a full scale debate by those people who are affected by it. But what we have here? The elected representatives in this Parliament, if the Government has its way, will not even be allowed to speak in a debate on a measure which proposes that a major commercial development be sold off by the Government. A capital gain of something like $20m will be pocketed by the Treasurer (Mr Howard) on behalf of his friends. It will not be distributed back to the taxpayers of this Territory, from where it was derived. The big donors to the

Liberal Party will get access to that commercial development. They will be allowed to run it and the tenants in that area will be pushed out by ever increasing rents imposed on a what-you-can-get basis. Honourable members opposite may yell but they do not believe in democracy. One Western Australian member opposite has been talking about democracy. His colleagues have just wiped out the right of the people of Western Australia to elect their own government. A couple of years ago they selectively tried to deny people in an electorate the right to enrol. Honourable members opposite should not talk about democracy. They do not know what the word means. They believe that Hitler was a communist left winger. The Canberra Commercial Development Authority runs a highly successful operation.
Mr Bourchier-What about what the Public Accounts Committee said?

It wants to race through this change in an Act of Parliament.
So what we see tonight is the government of Canberra being run by honourable members from Western Australia, Tasmania, South Australia and New South Wales but not by the people of Canberra. Mr GROOM (Braddon) (9.3)--1 think I can dispose of the question asked by the honourable member for Canberra (Mrs Kelly). She asked why the Parliament is debating these issues when they relate to the Australian Capital Territory. There are two major reasons for that. Firstly, there was a referendum, and the result of that referendum clearly indicated that the people of the Australian Capital Territory wanted this Parliament and the people we represent in this Parliament to remain responsible for the overall conduct of the Australian Capital Territory. Secondly-this is the obvious historical pointthe Australian Capital Territory happens to be the national capital. It was established initially as the seat of government of this country. Therefore, all of the people we represent, including the people who happen to live on the north-west coast of Tasmania, have an interest in the Australian Capital Territory. I hope that they will continue to have an interest in the Australian Capital Territory. The reason the people who live in the Australian Capital Territory want the whole of Australia to remain responsible is so that those people who live in places such as the electorate which I represent will pay taxes to help support the Australian Capital Territory. The people who live in Canberra cannot have it both ways. They cannot receive the benefits that come from this Parliament being responsible for Canberra and have the total independence that some people, such as the honourable member for Canberra, want to have.
I want to speak generally to this piece of legislation. I relate my comments to the title and the purpose of the Commonwealth Functions (Statutes Review) Bill. This Bill seeks to implement certain changes in Commonwealth functions. I indicate briefly that I support the spirit and the purpose of this important legislation. That is not to
say that I agree with everything that is proposed in this Bill. Obviously there would be some parts

of the Bill with which I might disagree. I might have done things in a slightly different way had I
been responsible for preparing the legislation and making these decisions. Overall, I believe it is a
sensible package and that is why I support it.

It represents a genuine attempt by the Govern-
ment to reduce the size of federal bureaucracy. I believe the majority of people in Australia realise
at this point in our history that government is too
big, that bureaucracy is too big-especially the bureaucracy attached to the Federal Government
and to this Parliament-and that it is costly. As a result people are paying too much tax, and that is
taking away incentive. So we have this general
feeling that people in the community want to get the size of government down and that they want to get their taxes down. They want to pay less tax
and have a little more in their pockets. Individuals can usually spend money more wisely than
governments can spend it.

Of course, this Bill involves a very fundamental

difference between the people who sit on this side

of this Parliament and those who sit on the other

side. If we look at the history of the Liberal Party,

in 1944 Robert Menzies, as he then was, was

concerned-as was the community-about the

threat of centralised government in Canberra.

The then Labor Government proposed a refer-

endum to give the government in Canberra a

whole range of additional powers. The Liberal

Party wants to distribute, the power of govern-

ment around Australia fairly among the Com-

monwealth and the States. That is a fundamental

purpose of the Liberal Party. On the other hand,

the Labor Party believes in a very strong central

government in Canberra. It wants to reduce the

power and the influence of the State governments.

Therefore, I can understand why people on tp.e

opposite side of the House tend to get upset when

we are debating this kind of issue.

.

The Bill results from the examination of Com-
monwealth functions undertaken by a committee of the Cabinet under the Minister for Industry and Commerce (Sir Phillip Lynch), which is
sometimes known as the razor gang. I believe that
exercise had two major purposes: Firstly, to transfer functions from the government sector to the
private sector and, secondly, to transfer fupctions
from the Commonwealth to the States. If I have time to do so, I will comment briefly on the second
purpose of that exercise. I want to dismiss one point that was made by the honourable member
for Gellibrand (Mr Willis) earlier when he said
that this legislation showed concern only for those people who have money. He said that there was a
need to protect the less fortunate people in the
community with a larger public sector.

Commonwealth Functions (Statutes Review) Bill 4 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 3185

I want to make it clear that I do not support the notion that we should reduce the size of everyone of our programs and cut cloth for the sake of cutting cloth. We must maintain the important programs we have as a Commonwealth Government, especially those programs that assist the people in genuine need in the community. Of course, we recognise that there are people in genuine need who must be supported by the overall population, and that is why we have the programs we do. We recognise that people such as the handicapped, especially in the International Year of Disabled Persons-I refer to orphans, Aboriginal communities and the aged-must be protected.
Honourable members interjecting-Mr GROOM-I ask honourable members opposite to wait for just a moment. If we can do away with the wasteful areas of Government expenditure and the useless programs we will have more money available for those people in genuine need. One of the reasons I support the measures contained in this Bill is that they will give the Commonwealth Government a greater capacity to help those people in the community in genuine need. I believe we will see as an end result greater assistance provided for those sections of the community that need help from the government. Also, as a result of these decisions we will find that the Government will have a greater capacity to reduce the tax burden borne by taxpayers around Australia. This legislation gives the Government the chance to do both of those things. I certainly hope that the Government very soon will consider family allowances or increasing the rebate for dependent wives. Also I hope it will consider reducing the size of the income tax bill paid by the taxpayers of Australia. I ask the Minister for the Capital Territory (Mr Hodgman)- who is at the table and who I know has great understanding of these matters-the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) and his colleagues to look very carefully at the issues of family allowances and personal income tax. I believe there will be, as. a result of this legislation, an opportunity to do something positive in those fields. I hope that we do not wait for two or three years to do those things; I hope we do them very soon. The DEPUTY CHAIRMAN (Dr Jenkins)- Order! I remind the honourable member that he is straying from the content of clauses 1 to 89. I invite him to get back to them. Mr GROOM-As I said, Mr Chairman, I was directing my comments to the title and purpose of

the Bill, which is contained in clause 1. It is concerned with functions. I believe it is very relevant to consider the end result of the legislation. It gives me an opportunity to talk generally. At the moment Commonwealth-State relations are a problem in this country. I believe it is important for the Commonwealth to get out of those areas in which it should not be involved and to let the States conduct the business that they should conduct under our constitutional system of government. Since Federation in 1901 the size of the Commonwealth Government has continued to grow. We are now seeing, probably for the first time, a change in direction which gives us the chance to reduce the size of the Commonwealth Government. It is important that we get out of the areas of government we are involved in but should not be involved in. Our involvement interferes with the proper functions of the States and creates friction between the Commonwealth and the States. That is very debilitating for government in this country.

The DEPUTY CHAIRMAN. Order! The honourable member's time has expired.

Clauses agreed to.

Clause 90 (Trading hours).

Mrs KELLY (Canberra) (9.l9)-The Opposition is giving the Government the opportunity of assisting small business. Tonight, Government members can come across to join us to vote against the decision to deregulate shopping hours in the Australian Capital Territory. The Opposition is voting against that for the benefit of small business in Canberra, and we would welcome anyone from the other side of the chamber, especially the Minister for the Capital Territory (Mr Hodgman), who would like to join us in this vote. The decision to deregulate shopping hours was taken by the Lynch razor gang without consultation with the Canberra small business community, without consultation with the Canberra consumers and without consultation with the Australian Capital Territory House of Assembly; and on the recommendation only of-who knows? Maybe this decision was taken just at the whim of the razor gang. Recently the National Capital Development Commission conducted a very detailed survey of retailing. That survey gave the clear indication that there was no consumer demand for deregulation of shopping hours. On 27 February the Australian Association of Independent Businesses Ltd wrote to the Minister for the Capital Territory opposing any move to deregulate shopping hours. Since that time. I have had representation from very many small shopkeepers in Canberra opposing deregulation, especially from small family traders who are in

the big shopping complexes run mainly by Lend Lease Corporation Ltd. Those small shops and their family owners will not have a choice about opening times because of the leases under which they operate. The opening times for those shopping complexes are set by the managers Lend Lease. That does not give small family businesses a fair go.
The DEPUTY CHAIRMAN (Dr Jenkins)- Order! The Committee will come to order. The level of conversation is so high that it is almost impossible to hear the honourable member for Canberra. I suggest that it would assist the Committee if honourable members were quiet.
Mrs KELLY-I think the problem tonight is that Government members are not prepared to listen to sense. They are not prepared really to put up a fight and support the small business people of Australia. I am speaking on behalf of them tpnight. Many honourable members have already been lobbied by the Australian Association of Independent Businesses, which has outlined its clear objections. So this decision is taken without any concern for the Canberra consumers, without any concern for small business and without any consultation at a time when there is already an oversupply of retail space and there is low consumer confidence in the Australian Capital Territory. This decision will not help business in Canberra and it will work to the detriment of the Australian Capital Territory economy.

The second thing 1 want to say is that if they want to get a fair assessment of the reaction of the people of Canberra to the recommendations in the Lynch committee report they should go out and talk to them. The fact of the matter is that the small traders of Belconnen Mall, as my colleague the honourable member for O'Connor (Mr Tuckey) will confirm, are determined to exercise their rights, if they can to become the owners and not the tenants. C1~uses 91 to 106-by leave-taken together.

Mr SCHOLES (Corio) (9.38)-IIi line with the Opposition's belief that the functions described in these clauses are State and local government functions which should be dealt with by the people of Canberra and the elected representatives of those peoples the Opposition maintain its opposition to the clauses but will not divide on them because of the pressure of time.

Clauses agreed to.

Clauses 107 to 113-by leave-taken together. Mr CHAPMAN (Kingston) (9.40)-1 have pleasure in supporting these clauses which relate to the Australian Postal Commission. They give effect to a very important part of the report of the Review of Commonwealth Functions which was presented to this chamber recently. These clauses abolish the Australia Post Courier Service. The abolition of the Australia Post Courier Service is an important reaffirmation of the basic philosophy of the Liberal Party. It is based on the precept that the people ought to be free to choose how they live, work, vote and spend their disposable income. Obviously, a result of this belief is the fact that the Liberal Party must be consistent in supporting and aiding as much as possible an economy where freedom of choice is the norm. That receives application in these clauses. The reductions in and rationalisation of Government spending given effect by this legislation positively demonstrate that small, less interventionist government is the foundation stone of the Liberal-National Country parties. The Federal Government will continue to implement policies that acknowledge that philosophy. 1 welcome the assurance that the Committee under the Minister for Industry and Commerce (Sir Philip Lynch) will continue its review of Commonwealth functions. I hope that the current initiatives are just the beginning. Much more remains to be done to achieve a more market oriented economy and a freer society in Australia. It is important that the philosophical basis of these decisions should be fully understood not only by Liberals but also by those in the community whom the Liberals represent and on whose behalf the elected government bases its decisions and policies. Suffice to say that a single thread must run through any successful government-a preeminent idea or concept which unites, but does not totally bind, that party and unites it with the people it has been elected to govern. For the Liberal Party that thread, of course, is its philosophy of liberalism. That is the concept which unites the party. But the party is not irrevocably bound by a doctrine which forces it to make decisions which are not in accord with the welfare of the people it is governing. It is a basic thread and fl basic philosophy upon which all the decisions of the Government are based. It is on that basis that this particular decision to abolish the Australia Post Courier Service is based.
It is not appropriate that a government should fulfil all the demands and wants of a community. People should have those demands fulfilled in the market-place, either by combining voluntarily as

3190 REPRESENTATIVES 4 June 1981 Commonwealth Functions (Statutes Review) Bill

a group or working on an individual basis. Over and above that it is the role of government to fulfil those needs which people are unable to fulfil for themselves. The Australia Post Courier Service does not come within the ambit of that proposition. Therefore, I support these clauses which will abolish the Service's activities.
Clauses agreed to.
Clauses 114 to 121 (Part IV)-by leavetaken together.
Mr KERIN (Werriwa) (9,43)-The Opposition is opposed to Part IV of the Bill comprising clauses 114 to 121. Part IV relates to the Government's decision with respect to the change of status of the Australian Wool Testing Authority and the necessary changes to the Wool Industry Act. The Opposition is opposed to that part of the omnibus Bill because it has become clear that the Government has backtracked so much on its original proposition that there is no need to continue with the cosmetic farce that it is now engaged in. It has adopted more changes of position in this matter than appear in the Kamasutra. This Part of the Bill is nothing more than a contrivance to save face. The only upshot of the move will be an increase in wool test charges to the industry as the Hill Samuel independent report has clearly said. The Chairman of the Australian Wool Corporation has said so, as has every objective independent observer.
If the Government believed all along that the wool industry was getting something on the cheap and that it was this and only this that offended its ideology it should have said so honestly to the industry, instead of engaging in the farce it has and inflicting confusion on the industry. The best thing the Government could do, if this Bill is passed, would be not to proclaim it. When I last spoke on this matter in the House on 14 May I said that it was abundantly clear that the Government had bungled and that it did notunderstand the role and functions of the AWTA because that body was an independent, official, impartial and highly efficient body and that its sale to the private sector was not only wrong but also dangerous to the wool industry. I pointed out the way in which the AWTA had saved the industry money, how low its costs were and that all that the Government could or would do would be to raise costs and throw the wool industry into chaos.
It has since been reported to me by a wool broker of impeccable reputation that since I made the speech the Minister for Primary Industry (Mr Nixon) instituted a witch hunt in his Department to find out who I had been talking to. In case some poor person is going to be erroneously subjected

to the wrath of the Minister and victimised I wish to state before the House that before that speech I spoke to no one in the Department of Primary Industry, the Bureau of Agricultural Economics, the Commonwealth Scientific and Industrial Research Organisation, any wool broker, anyone from the Australian Wool Corporation or any person in the commercial wool trade. I spoke with two people in the AWTA after a statement had been issued on their behalf, and I was told exactly what any member of the public would be told. I insisted that that was all I was to be told. Of course, it is ridiculous for the Minister to institute a witch hunt.
In 1971-72 I worked in the Bureau of Agricultural Economics in the wool marketing section when it was principally engaged in the Australian objective measurement project. I know what has happened in the Australian wool industry since 1971. Of course, there was no need for me to have known anything about the wool industry at all. All I would have had to do would be to read the cascade of material opposing the Government's move, which material has come from absolutely every organisation within the wool industry.
Even the wool broker mates of the Minister for Industry and Commerce (Sir Phillip Lynch) who put him up to the crazy idea to sell AWTA were not supported by the National Council of Wool Selling Brokers. There is no need for me to ring David Asimus or Ian McLachlan or any wool industry leader. Their attitude is on the public record. The latest example is a telegram to all New South Wales and Australian Capital Territory senators from the Livestock and Grain Producers Association of New South Wales, which reads:
The Livestock and Grain Producers Association of NSW totally oppose the Federal Government's proposal that the Australian Wool Testing Authority (AWTA) be divorced from its relationship with the Australian Wool Corporation (AWC).
The Governments justification for the 'razor gang' cuts appear to be based on the premise that the user must pay. In the case of AWTA this premise is not valid as the user does pay.
In fact, the Government does not provide one cent in direct cash handouts to AWTA. At the same time, AWTA has been able to make profits over the last five years of about $1million per annum and hold its charges at about the 1970 level.
The reason for disposing of AWTA had to change and Mr Nixon has said that 'there is no longer a requirement for the Authority to have a link with the Government'.
This 'link' provides the very basis of the wool industry's marketing system. All sectors of the industry have very strongly opposed the Government's present proposal. These objections are best summarised by the International Wool Textile Organisation:

Commonwealth Functions (Statutes Review) Bill 4 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 3191

'Members of IWTO are strongly of the view that in its unique function between sellers and buyers, AWTA must be, and be seen to be, an official, independent and impartial body.'
Mr Nixon has further stated 'that the fundamental aim is to ensure that AWTA continues in precisely the same way as it does already.' 'We will establish a foundation' says Mr Nixon 'which will be designed by the industry itself.'
One thinks that the industry has already designed its own 'foundation' and that it already exists-namely, AWTA.
No real argument has been put up by the Government as to why it is necessary to dispose of AWTA. This is particularly disturbing when there is the slightest possibility of doing damage to Australia's wool marketing system.
As wool growers, we are extremely concerned with the Government's proposal to dispose of AWTA: This concern has been conveyed to the Prime Minister by a large number of our members, apparently to no avail.
The only course of action left is to seek your support to have the 'razor gang' legislation either amended-to remove references to AWTA, or defeated.'
The word 'link' is of prime importance because it is now quite clear the link between AWTA and the AWC and the Government will not be severed. If anything, the AWC will be closer to the AWTA. We still do not know what status or structure the new organisation will have, but it is quite apparent from all the confusing answers to questions in this House that the Government does not intend carrying out its original announced purpose. We are not even sure who is being consulted in the industry other than the AWC. I will read once again the Prime Minister's bald announcement of 30 April 1981 in the Review of Commonwealth Functions under the heading 'Transfer of Functions to Private Sector'. He stated:
We propose to offer for sale as a going concern . . . the Wool Testing Laboratory-
There is nothing ambiguous in that. It is a clear statement leaving no room for misinterpretation. We now have before us a Bill which is very short on detail and does not definitely state that the AWTA is to be sold to Mr Fraser's holier than holy private sector. Just about everything is subject to the consent of the Minister. Even the sale of the wool stores is subject to the consent of the Minister. In other words, in an exercise which is entirely about saving face the Minister is going to go behind closed doors with the AWC and others, first to be educated by the wool industry about the stupidity of the Government's action and then to reach a compromise which is not too ridiculous, which does not cost the industry too much and which does not destroy the effectiveness of the AWTA too much. All that will be done is that the Wool Testing Authority will be given a new name with a new board which mayor may not have voting rights. We have not been told. There is little ever to vote on. The Government has discovered that there is a part of the Department of

Primary Industry which can pass judgment on the standards. It has always been there. I better tell the Minister that it is called the Australian Wool Measurement Standards Authority. I guess the CSIRO representative will be taken off. We have not been told. Perhaps I should inform the Minister the reasons for CSIRO being on the Wool Testing Authority. It is there because of its active professional involvement in wool technology, technological innovation and measurement standards.
This Bill creates a legal device whereby the Australian Wool Corporation, which is a statutory corporation, may first set up a body corporate, which can be a statutory corporation, to acquire the testing facilities operated by the AWTA, which is a statutory authority but which is at present legally owned by the AWe. There are provisions in the Bill which permit this company to be sold to a second company which will in future undertake the operations which are at present performed by the AWTA. Apart from referring to the abundant ridiculousness of this there are two things to ask: Firstly, if the AWC clearly owns the AWTA why is there a need to set up a legal intermediate body to buy the assets of AWTA? Secondly, why is the intermediate body corporate suddenly called a company and the possible final owner also to be called a company? It is just ridiculous. If it is to be called a company and to meet some of the descriptions given by the Minister and Acting Minister it will have to be a company limited by guarantee. So far we have heard of a private company, then a foundation, then a statutory corporation and now the body is to be perhaps a company limited by guarantee.
If the AWTA is to be taken over by such a company it may not have to pay tax. But who will own the company and where will the proceeds go? The Wool Corporation will sell the assets to, say, a company or a body corporate which will last for an instant if the second company comes along. The Wool Corporation will get the proceeds. But then someone has to pay for the second company-if it comes along. If the Wool Corporation is to have a share-the Minister virtually said that it will-it then has to buy the thing it has just sold. If other people are expected to be cobuyers-let us say the wool council, wool brokers or fellmongers-where will they get the money from and why would they put the money in if they will not get a return on their money? We do not even know whether they are being consulted. The whole thing is ridiculous. The Government cannot have it both ways.

The DEPUTY CHAIRMAN (Hon. Charles Jones)-Order! The honourable member's time has expired.
Mr BUNGEY (Canning) (9.53)-I rise to support the views of the honourble member for Werriwa (Mr Kerin). I am pleased that he is supporting my views. 1 was proposing to oppose the clauses mentioned. I circulated my notice of this much earlier in the day than did the honourable member. I oppose this part of the Commonwealth Functions (Statutes Review) Bill for several reasons. It has been put to me on several occasions that the 350 decisions of the Review of Commonwealth Functions are one parcel. I do not agree with that. I do not agree that if one buys 350 eggs and one of them is bad one has to consume the whole 350 eggs. This is one bad decision amongst those 350 decisions. Let me emphasise in speaking against this decision that I support the rest of the package. But I maintain that this is a bad decision in that package of 350 decisions.
I disagree with Part IV on several basic grounds. It has always been a policy of the coalition parties as far as I am concerned that in dealings with industry, particularly primary industry, there is consultation before decision. There was not adequate consultation before decision or before the announcement of the decision to sell the Australian Wool Testing Authority or the Australian wool stores. We have always maintained this policy. Generally, within the platform of the Liberal Party, there is a provision that we have to meet two basic criteria when looking at rural marketing and statutory and orderly marketing organisations. Firstly, we should look to see that the interests of consumers and producers are protected and, secondly, we have to see that we have full consultation and co-operation with the industry involved. In both ways this decision abrogates those basic criteria that are written into our platform. I understand that the platform of our partners in the coalition has similar provisions with regard to the consultation, discussion and agreement with industry before a decision is made. I think we have only to look at some of the provisions of this legislation to realise the way in which consultation was effected. At least three times in the clauses that we are now opposing it is stated 'and shall, if so directed by the Minister'. That is far from consultation in any way with the industry.
I can assure honourable members that there was no consultation-I have checked on this very closely-with the Australian Wool Council, which has followed on from the Australian Wool Industry Conference. There was very little, if any, discussion with the Australian Wool Corporation.

There may have been some perfunctory discussion with the chairman of that Corporation but certainly not with the Corporation itself. Any discussion that has taken place with these two bodies has been in the light of the decision-not by way of reaching the decision. For that reason I cannot condone the way in which this procedure has been conducted.
An amendment has changed the way in which the decision has been promulgated. Firstly we had a decision to sell to private enterprise; now it is to be transferred in some way or another to some foundation or the like. The matter is still very unclear indeed. There is very great opposition from a number of sectors in the industry. In a previous speech I referred to opposition from the International Wool Textile Organisation. I pointed to objections from the Government in India. I pointed to the objection from the Australian Wool Testing Authority. That Authority represents all sections of the industry at the moment. Those bodies genuinely agreed that the move was not desirable or necessary. I refer to a recent Press release issued by the Australian Wool Corporation on 22 May. I quote one section of the statement. It reads:
'At this stage of the examination I can say that all alternatives would result in an increase in costs to the industry with no discernable benefit to it,' . . .
That statement was made by the Chairman of the Corporation, Mr David Asimus. I seek leave of the Committee to incorporate in Hansard the Press release of 22 May of the Australian Wool Corporation.
Leave granted.
The document read as follows-
Press statement 22 May 1981
Wool Corporation opposes decision to sell AWTA.
At its first meeting since the announcement of the Government's decision to sell the Australian Wool Testing Authority, the Australian Wool Corporation today declared its opposition to the Government proposal.
The Chairman of the Corporation, Mr David Asimus, said after the meeting that, as a result of the Government decision, Corporation management and consultants had been working since early this month on alternatives concerning the sale of AWTA and establishment of a successor.
'At this stage of the examination I can say that all alternatives would result in an increase in costs to the industry with no discernible benefit to it,' Mr Asimus said.
'Even a structure similar to that of the existing AWTA, with similar industry representation, could well involve significant additional cost.
'For a new organisation to fund such extra costs as well as instalments of asset transfer payments to the corporation, wool testing charges would have to be increased annually to a much greater extent than would otherwise be the case.'

Mr BUNGEY-I thank the Committee. In a number of questions put before the House and in telegrams to the Minister for Primary Industry and the Acting Minister for Primary Industry I have been attempting to find out what costs will result to the wool industry and wool users from this decision and what benefits will accrue. In the replies that I have received-where I have received replies-I have found nothing to convince me that there will be savings in any way to wool growers or to the Government. In fact, it is quite clear-it was referred to by the previous speaker, the honourable member for Werriwathat the Hill Samuel independent report commissioned by the Australian Wool Corporation indicated that the costs of the operations of the Testing Authority will increase by at least 15 per cent by virtue of this change. That is undesirable. We can compare it with the fact that charges for services rendered by the Australian Wool Testing Authority, which have been its sole method of financing, have not increased by more than 3 per cent over the past 10 years. Admittedly, I believe that some increase, divorced from this decision, brought about by cost and wage increases is in train at the moment.

Let me emphasise clearly that there is no cost to the Government in any way, and there will be no saving on the current operation of the Authority. There will be no saving in capital or operating costs in any way by effecting a sale of the Australian Wool Testing Authority. No public servants are involved and there is no cost to the Commonwealth in capital or operating costs. As I see it, this is an unnecessary dictation to the wool growers, who have taken a very realistic and proper attitude to many other aspects of their industry. For instance, they contribute quitesignificantly to promotion of the industry, both within Australia and overseas. They contribute to research within their industry and for their industry, both at the producer level and in the textile industry. They also make a significant contribution towards the market support policies and practices within the wool industry. The wool growers have already been called upon and have met the challenge to service their industry without recourse to Government aid. It seems to me an unnecessary dictation to require the sale of the functions which have been provided, serviced and paid for by the wool growers. I can in no way support the sale of the Australian Wool Testing Authority or a direction to the Australian Wool Corporation to dispose of certain assets in the way of wool stores.

I can imagine what will happen if all this deregulation is applied in the primary industry area. Deregulation in this area will result in a complete destruction of the orderly marketing arrangements for primary industry goods in this country.

It is an attempt to prove in some ridiculous way to a small group of Liberal back benchers how tough the Government is going to be. CAMPBELLTOWN That this Council, whilst supporting in general terms the thrust of the Lynch Committee towards smaller government, views with the utmost concern the proposal of that Committee to sell the Australian Wool Testing Authority to the private sector as that proposal:

From: K. D. Von Bibra, Chairman, Wool Council of TFGA
The Wool Commodity Group of the Tasmanian Farmers and Graziers Association, held its annual conference in Campbelltown on 7th May 1981. While supporting the principle of reducing public expenditure for inflation control, the Wool Commodity Group are adamant that the Australian Wool Testing Authority be retained in its present form.

(I) That this annual conference of the Wool Council of TFGA completely rejects any suggestion that the Australian Wool Testing Authority be sold. I would have hoped that the statement made on 15 May by the Minister for Primary Industry and the responses of the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) and the Acting Minister for Primary Industry (Mr Sinclair) would also have indicated the complete bona fides of the Government so far as the Australian Wool Testing Authority is concerned.
We believe that the establishment of a special foundation to continue the present functions of the Australian Wool Testing Authority will prove to be the most appropriate way in which to deal with this issue. There can be no question of in any way diminishing or impugning the reliability of the certificates that have been issued by the Authority. I repeat the assurance given on 15 May by my colleague the Minister for Primary Industry that there will be total consultation and that a final decision will be made by the Wool Corporation in consultation with the Minister.

Mr KERIN (Werriwa) (10.9)-What the
Treasurer (Mr Howard) has said is right in a way. The legislation is wide; it provides no details. Vir-
tually everything is left to the Minister. This mat-
ter will be resolved behind closed doors. That might be called consultation but, as it is, and as it
has been for the last month or so, nobody knows exactly what the Government is doing. Essen-
tially, the legislation is a contrivance to save face.

Question so resolved in the affirmative.

Clauses 122 to 147-by leave-taken together.

Mr JOHN BROWN (Parramatta) (10.18) -Part V of the Bill deals with bankruptcy. I want to make a few brief comments putting the Opposition's case. We are very much opposed to this
Part because we feel the Government is introduc-
ing some retrograde steps by encouraging the private sector to take over this area of investigation.
A member of the accounting firm of Hungerford,
Hancock and Offner has been appointed a member of a joint management review team to re-
view the operations of the Official Receiver sec-
tion of the Corporate Affairs Division of the Department of Business and Consumer Affairs. This
Part of the Bill implements the findings of that re-
view team in as much as it facilitates the appointment of ,public accountants to do insolvency
work. The Opposition is aware-I presume the
Government is, although it has not made it very public knowledge-that the proposals which the
Government is now suggesting are almost identical to those outlined in a Green Paper introduced
by the United Kingdom Government in June 1980. This United Kingdom Green Paper invited
comments from the legal, accounting and com-
mercial interests to the proposition that what the Government is doing here should also be done in
England. I can tell honourable members that that
paper attracted trenchant criticism from all those professional areas-from the accounting pro-
fession and the legal profession in Britain. They
saw the pitfalls that that would have introduced in Britain. This is the same type of legislation the
Government is trying to introduce here.

3196 REPRESENTATIVES 4 June 1981 Commonwealth Functions (Statutes Review) Bill

This Government introduced these amendments without any reference to the community interests at all. There was no reference of the matter to the accountancy profession or the legal profession to seek opinions of their members as to what they would do. The only information that these sections of interest might have had would have been in the form of two Press releases, one on 31 December 1980 which made some reference to outside trustees and the other on 30 April 1981 which concerned the razor gang statement. Tbat Press release made a reference to the Government's intention to contract out the Government's bankruptcy work. Neither of those could be described as giving the legal profession, the accountancy profession or the business community full information or an opportunity to comment on the Government's proposals.
I will not take up any more of the time of the House. I merely tell the Government that we on this side of the House do not accept these proposals. We will be opposing this Part of the Bill. This Bill when introduced into the House included recommendations by the Committee, one of the members of which was a member of the accounting firm of Hungerford, Hancock and Offner. The object of the proposals was to get the Government out of bankruptcy work and to get private enterprise into bankruptcy work. On the very day the Bill was introduced, 10 and behold, an advertisement appeared in the Sydney Morning Herald. It was an advertisement on behalf of Hungerford, Hancock and Offner seeking a person to join that company's Sydney insolvency section. It stated that the applicant should ideally have had one to three years' experience in a professional office. Insolvency experience was not a firm prerequisite. It continued that the main areas of activity would include receiverships, liquidations, schemes of arrangements and financial investigations, et cetera. It seems to me an incredible coincidence that the company which recommended these changes that the Government should be contracting this work out to private enterprise should, on the day the Bill was introduced, be advertising for more staff. I do not think I am particularly suspicious, but I point out what a rare coincidence that is. Can we be blamed for thinking that that firm might have had some inside information on what the Government. proposed to do?
This legislation is a retrograde step. This Government has a responsibility to the business community and to help the people who have suffered desperately as a result of bankruptcies. I think it is fair to say that the only industry in Australia that has grown under this Government

has been the bankruptcy industry. The number of bankruptcies has increased unbelievably. It seems to be a very retrograde step for the Government to be abrogating its responsibility to protect the business community and those people who have suffered desperately because of bankruptcies by handing this sector over to private enterprise. We firmly oppose these amendments. To facilitate the passage of this legislation we will not seek to divide the Committee.
Clauses agreed to.
Clauses 148 to ISS-by leave-taken together, and agreed to.
Proposed new clause ISSA.
Mr LIONEL BOWEN (Kingsford-Smith) (10.2S)-by leave-I move:
(1) Page 42, after clause 155, insert the following new clause:
"155A. Section 19 of the Administrative Appeals Tribunal Act 1975 is amended by inserting after paragraph (2) (c) the fol1owing paragraph:
'lea) Compensation (Commonwealth Government Employees) Division; and'.".
(2) Schedule 2, page 72, proposed amendments to the Compensation (Commonwealth Government Employees) Act 1971, after
"Paragraph 51 (3) (b) . . . Omit 'Compensation' " insert:
". . . After section 54 insert the following new section:
'54A. The Commissioner shall determine a claim for compensation as soon as practicable but in any case within 120 days oflodgement of the claim.' ".
(3) Schedule 2, page 74, proposed amendments to the Compensation (Commonwealth Government Employees) Act 1971, after proposed section 65, insert:
Retention of Appeals to Prescribed Courts " '66. Notwithstanding the amendment of this Act by
the Commonwealth Functions (Statutes Review) Aet 1981, where no application is made to the Administrative Appeals Tribunal for a review of a determination in accordance with this Part, an application may be made to a prescribed court for a review of the determination in accordance with this Part as if this Act had not been amended by that Act.' ".
Part VI of the Bill deals with the Commonwealth Employees (Compensation) Act. The Bill seeks to amend over 24 Acts. It is no wonder that the Committee is in confusion and there is delay in relation to this legislation. There are more clauses of the Bill to be considered. The amendments moved by the Opposition are intended to improve the legislation. Reports have been given to the Attorney-General (Senator Durack) which clearly indicate that in the area of workers compensation there are inordinate delays in dealing with people who are injured at work. Honourable members will appreciate that those people are entitled to receive speedy compensation. I am advised that death claims at the present time take from 18 months to two years to be dealt with.

Commonwealth Functions (Statutes Review) Bill 4 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 3197

Rather simple claims, such as a loss of hearing, can take from six months to 12 months to be dealt with. The report that was prepared by the Administrative Review Council states that 50 per cent of cases are taking in excess of five months to be dealt with and some are taking up to two years. My second amendment states:
The Commissioner shall determine a claim for compensation as soon as practicable but in any case within 120 days of lodgement of the claim.
Another amendment to the present Act will allow for appeals to be made to what are called prescribed courts as an alternative to what is now proposed, which is that appeals will have to be heard by the Administrative Appeals Tribunal. Those practising in the profession and those who are well aware of the difficulties are anxious to retain the present position whereby an appeal can be made to a prescribed court. In New Soulh Wales, for example, appeals can be heard by the Workers' Compensation Commission of New South Wales. In support of that proposal I point out that one can see that the litigants will have much more confidence if they know their case is to be heard by a judge who has the experience of dealing with matters of that type. I see no reason why-it would be regressive to do so-we should remove the right of Commonwealth employees to have their appeals heard in this manner. In a nutshell those are the reasons why we think it is important to retain existing rights, and amendments have been moved accordingly. The amendments that have been moved are also designed to speed up the procedure and to make it mandatory that the decisions be given by the Commissioner within a period of 120 days.
Mr HOWARD (Bennelong-Treasurer) (IO.28)-Perhaps I can briefly indicate to the Deputy Leader of the Opposition (Mr Lionel Bowen) why the Government feels unable to support the amendments that he has moved. I appreciate that they have been moved by the Opposition out of a concern to maintain certain principles. The first amendment would have the effect, as I understand it, of setting up separate divisions of the Administrative Appeals Tribunal. The Administrative Review Council report which was tabled on 2 June opposed that approach. I gather that the main reason for that opposition was that it would, if implemented, limit the flexibility of the Administrative Appeals Tribunal in assigning judges between panels. The whole purpose of a number of these amendments is to increase the level of flexibility.

determining claims is attractive-I can understand why the Deputy Leader of the Opposition suggests that I point out that medical reports often take a good deal of time to be obtained. It is often in the interests of an appellant, quite apart from any question of administrative delay, that those reports are available. They will often strengthen his or her case. I am not immediately aware that such an amendment is necessarily in the interests of social security and compensation of appellants. Incidently, the transfer of appeals to the AAT does bring it under the Ombudsman who can investigate delays. I think that provides for a more flexible approach.
The third amendment, which is to allow appeals to the courts as well as to the Administrative Appeals Tribunal, goes against the direction of the Bill, which is to have one avenue of appeal. Once again, this is not just something that was divined by the Government. It follows the recommendation of the Administrative Review Council. We think it is desirable and in the interests of the achievement of a speedy resolution of the appeals, and the avoiding of the confusion of conflicting judgments and jurisdictions, to adopt this approach. As I have said, whilst I understand completely the motives of the Deputy Leader of the Opposition in moving these amendments and respect the views he has put, for the reasons I have outlined the Government is not able to accede to them.
Amendments negatived.
Remainder of the Bill-by leave-taken as a whole.

Income Tax (Assessment and Rates) Bill

of the Commonwealth Functions (Statutes Review) Bill I wish to indicate that I have an objection to clause 212, which states:
The Legislative Drafting Institute Act 1974 is repealed.
The Opposition opposes that repeal. If ever we needed something we need parliamentary counsel and the ability to train them. Honourable members can see the mess in which this Government is at present, apart from other difficulties, in putting through legislation in time. The Institute was a training ground for people to become qualified parliamentary draftsmen or parliamentary counsel. Doing away with this body is not helping this Parliament or the training of draftsmen. Are we to understand that in future these people will be trained somewhere else and that we will try to hire them after they are trained? Does this Government think that it will save money by doing that? Is it not more effective to have the people who eventually will be senior counsel in the drafting area trained in the sphere appropriate for this Parliament?
It is important that we place on record our dismay that legislation which we were able to introduce in 1974 is now being repealed on the basis that it will be cost saving. Like everything else this Government has done in this area it will find that because of its hiving off of its trained personnel or its ability to train them it will have to recruit such people from somewhere else at double the cost. It is a very short-sighted measure which affects the careers of people who relied on having a future in this area. The Opposition vigorously opposes clause 212.

Mr WILLIS (Gellibrand) (10.36)-1 move:
That all words after 'That' be omitted with a view to substituting the following words:
'whilst not opposing the second reading of this Bill, the House notes that:
(a) because a tax indexation factor of only 3.8 per cent has been applied, all taxpayers whose incomes in 1981-82 will be more than 3.8 per cent above their 1980-81 incomes and whose circumstances do not otherwise alter, will be paying a higher proportion of that income in income tax in 1981-82 than in 1980-81;
(b) such an increase in the burden of income tax continues the pattern of an increasing income tax burden during the Fraser Government's term of office, and
(c) such developments are in stark contrast to the Government's continual attempts to portray itself as a low tax party,
and accordingly deplores the Government's action in abolishing tax indexation altogether for future years, which indicates its clear intention to make full use of the secret tax of inflation'.

In Committee
Clauses 1 to 27-by leave-taken together, and agreed to.
Clause 28 (Indexation).
Mr WILLIS (Gellibrand) (10.38)-Clause 28 deals with the abolition of indexation from the tax system. Because of the time factor I do not wish to repeat the arguments that I made in my speech in the second reading debate. The indexation which is being abolished by clause 28 relates to dependants allowances and allowances for a sale parent and for a housekeeper. Indexation for these allowances is, of course, part of the overall process of indexation. It is important not only that the Schedule be indexed but also that the allowances be indexed. I rise simply to register our protest at the abolition of indexation in respect of dependants allowances and the other allowances dealt with in this clause.
Mr HOWARD (Bennelong-Treasurer) (lO.39)-In the context of the consideration of this clause I would'like to emphasise two things. Firstly, the election promise of the Prime Minister

Income Tax (Assessment and Rates) Bill

4 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 3199

(Mr Malcolm Fraser) in his last policy speech has been implemented in full by this Government so far as half indexation of tax is concerned. It is important when we are talking about commitments to indexation to state the very obvious proposition that if the Government is elected on a particular program and a particular commitment in this area it is relevant to accept that when the commitments made in the policy speech that have led up to that election have been discharged claims of broken promises and broken undertakings in the general area to which those commitments relate are without foundation. The commitment on which this Government was fe-elected, so far as half indexation is concerned, was to have a $500m tax deduction on 1 July. The very Bill we are debating-the Income Tax (Assessment and Rates) Amendment Bill-gives effect to that. I make it quite clear that any suggestions that commitments in the last policy speech on this subject have been breached are without any foundation.
Secondly, there are arguments for and against tax indexation which I, and the Government, acknowledge. There are advantages in tax indexation, but the Government has come to the conclusion, after balancing the arguments for and against, that a system of discretionary tax reductions is preferable. Obviously the honourable member for Gellibrand is right in saying we can have half or full indexation and also discretionary tax cuts but I think he would agree that if we have a system of automatic indexation the capacity for discretionary tax cuts is reduced. I think that is elementary. It does not follow that through the abolition of automatic tax indexation, or half indexation, the quantum of tax cuts will be the less. Indeed, some people have argued that the abolition of tax indexation implies that in the future governments of either pursuasion will be under greater pressure than they have been in the past to give a greater degree of discretionary tax reductions.
Mr CUNNINGHAM (McMillan) (10.41)-1 would like to make a few comments on the abolition of tax indexation. The Treasurer (Mr Howard) has sought to justify the Government's decision on the ground that because the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) made a promise the Government will continue with indexation this year and it will then be abolished. In 1975, the Prime minister promised:
We will reduce the tax burden, we will put an end to Labor's tax rip off.
We will fully index personal income tax for inflation over three years . . . It will make Governmen t more honest with your money. They will no longer be able to rely on the secret tax of inflation.

However, here we are in 1981 debating the Income Tax (Assessment and Rates) Amendment Bill, which is designed to eliminate tax indexation. The Treasurer did say that the Prime Minister had promised full indexation and had honoured that promise to the extent that half indexation had been reached. After six years he nearly got there but now we are heading fast back to where we started. All that huff and puff we have had for six years has amounted to nothing.
Mr Ruddock-It was relevant to reduce inflation.
Mr CUNNINGHAM-I think it can be clearly indicated that inflation is on the rise again. There is no doubt about that. Under the old 1977 policies we would be going to full tax indexation to arrest the situation, but we are going the opposite way. So much for the Prime Minister's promise. It is just like the one we finished with a while ago. Today's Sydney Morning Herald had an interesting table headed 'Poor Man's Guide to Higher Taxes'. Mr David Collins, a lecturer in economics at Macquarie University, clearly indicated in that table where tax indexation has been going under the Fraser Government. The $1.50 a week tax cut proposed in this Bill will be more like a 95c tax cut for most people. The table shows that the single taxpayer who begins the new financial year on the average earnings of $15,000 will actually pay $480 more than in the previous year. The proportion of his income lost in tax will rise from 23.3 per cent to 23.9 per cent. So, the Government'~ promise of half tax indexation, about which the Treasurer spoke before, has become effectively only 35 per cent indexation. The promise of half indexation actually turns out to be a promise of 35 per cent indexation, which turns out to be a promise that it will last for 12 months, which in turn turns out to be a promise that the Government did not intend to keep indexation going anyway. So much for this Government's approach to taxation and tax concessions.
Under the Bill, in the next 12 months, those people in the higher brackets of income rio doubt will be better off than those on lower incomes. The figures for assessable incomes show that between 1976-77 and 1981-82 the average tax for those on the lower incomes increased. For those on $9,000 a year it increased by 1.2 per cent but for those in the higher brackets of $30,000 a year and upwards it declined by from 1.6 per cent to 3 per cent. Everything this Government seems to do in taxation is designed to shift the wealth from the poor to the very wealthy. Of course, that is very much in tune with what the Fraser Government has been about for the last five or six years. This

3200 REPRESENTATIVES 4 June 1981

Income Tax (Assessment and Rates) Bill

Bill, along with many others, provides another op-
portunity for the Fraser Government to shift the wealth of the country from those who can least
afford to pay to those who can afford to pay greater taxation. The excuse for that is that if
people are given the opportunity to spend their own money then it will be spent more wisely than if the Government collects the just tax that these
people should be paying. The Opposition disagrees with that philosophy. This Government has backed off from tax indexation. It has been promising lower taxes and lower Government
spending, but we all know that this year Government revenue is up 16.2 per cent on last year. All the spending cuts appear to be in rhetoric more than anything else, yet this Government is only too efficient at giving tax concessions to those who need them least. From the evidence of this week, some of the promises which the Prime Minister has made and which the Treasurer has defended have been honoured and some people are very lucky in that they will get rather large tax concessions whilst other people will miss out; they

will get 95c a week, under 35 per cent tax indexation. Eventually, at the end of the 12 months, the promises will disappear altogether and people can expect to be paying increased taxes. What were the words used? The Prime Minister said that, after 12 months, indexation would make this Government more honest with the people's money and the people would be able to rely on the Government having no secret tax increases because of inflation.

So we have had a continuation of the deceit which has been practised by the Prime Minister in connection with so many of the undertakings that have been given. The Prime Minister, of course, was quite blunt about the reason why tax indexation was going to be abandoned. The Prime Minister is on record as having said that the reason why tax indexation was going to be abandoned was that it was not obvious to the electorate, that the taxpayers. did not understand it, that a government got no credit or kudos for it. It is quite obvious that as far as this Government is concerned taxation policy will not be determined on the basis of rational economic policy and management. As far as the Prime Minister and this Government are concerned, taxation policy, as all the other areas of economic policy, will be used for cheap politicking, for meeting electoral bribes and will not be used to achieve the type of purposes that we should expect economic policy and sane and rational economic management to achieve.

PETROLEUM PRODUCTS PRICING BILL 1981
Second Reading Debate resumed from 27 May, on motion by MrMoore:
The the Bill be now read a second time.
Mr HURFORD (Adelaide) (11.1)-1 think it is ridiculous that at this late hour-after 11 o'clock-we are commencing debate on a most important Bill. It is just another example of how the business of this House is being mismanaged and how inadequate attention is being given to important legislation. I repeat that debate on the Petroleum Products Pricing Bill is commencing after 11 o'clock with the expectation that we will be here until at least 1 0 'clock in the morning. We were here until 2 o'clock this morning and 3 o'clock the morning before. This Bill now before the House bears all the characteristics of the Government-ad hoc decision making, inconsistent attitudes and the projection of a facade of

concern, while proceeding unashamedly to abdicate its responsibility to govern.
The purpose of the Bill is to establish an authority to monitor petroleum products prices but the authority will have virtually no independence, low status and limited powers and resources. As long as this Government remains in office, and unless the amendment I shall move, is passed, the authority will be subject to interference by a Minister who has no commitment to its existence or fundamental purpose. The authority is to replace the functions in respect of the petroleum products industry previously conferred on the Prices Justification Tribunal. This Government has abolished the Tribunal. The Opposition is totally opposed to the abolition of the PJT. We regard the Bill before the House as a lame attempt to placate legitimate public concern about petroleum prices and concern among service station proprietors in Australia regarding the pricing practices of the major oil companies. We in the Labor Opposition believe, having been convinced by the recommendations of the Collins royal commission into the petroleum industry, that the authority should go further than is envisaged in this Bill. The amendment to the second reading which I shall move on behalf of the Opposition sets out our attitude succinctly. I move:
That all words after 'That' be omitted with a veiw to substituting the following words:
'the House is of the opinion that(1) the Bill should be withdrawn and redrafted to
extend the role of the Petroleum Products Pricing Authority to that of an independent regulatory agency for the petroleum and petroleum products industries as recommended by the 4th Report-of the Royal Commission on Petroleum (the Collins Report), with functions to include:
(a) restructuring, monitoring and regulating the pricing system;
(b) the rationalisation, where necessary, ofretail outlets;
(c) the regulation of dealer-company relationships, and
(d) the development of a conservation program, and
(2) the Government should be condemned for its decision to abolish the Prices Justification Tribunal which was carrying out the functions of the proposed Petroleum Products Pricing Authority and calls upon it to retain a reformed Tribunal for such purposes as the provision of effective powers to monitor and inquire into the justification of prices in all oil products and other goods exempted by this Bill'.
The concept of prices justification was introduced in Australia by the Labor Government in 1973. It involved the establishment of an independent body which could inquire into prices. and report whether they were justified. There was no compulsion on the companies to accept the findings of the Tribunal. We did not have those powers under

Dr Theophanous-I second the amendment and reserve my right to speak.
Mr O'KEEFE (Paterson) (11.21)-The Petroleum Products Pricing Bill is very important. It provides for a body to take the place of the Prices Justification Tribunal. It provides for the establishment of a Petroleum Products Pricing Authoity with the power to inquire into prices and price increases for petroleum products. Of course, the abolition of the PJT is provided for in the Commonwealth Functions (Statutes Review) Bill 1981, which was introduced on 27 May last.

Almost every country in the world has some agency, or DcOne function of the PJT which will remain is the

partment of State designed specifically to deal with this industry in the areas of distribution, marketing and pricing.

power of surveillance over prices charged by the petroleum products industry. These powers will

It recommended that such an agency be established for Australia. That was stated by the Collins Royal Commission which examined aspects of the petroleum industry for many months. The need for an agency remains. The Government brought in this legislation last year to regulate franchises and the number of company-owned retail sites. As the Opposition pointed out, this legislation has not resolved all of

be given to the Petroleum Products Pricing Authority established under this Bill. The pricing of petroleum products has been a very controversial issue over the past few years. It covers a very wide field of activity. Wholesale and retail outlets, bulk distributors, primary producers and transport operators all have to be covered under a price structure. This Authority has indeed a very wide range in which to examine prices.

the industry. We regard that as an abdication of of petrol, distillate, heating oil and associated

3206 REPRESENTATIVES 4 June 1981

Petroleum Products Pricing Bill

products because they have an everyday usage. Liquid fuels are the basis for much of the national way of life and standard of living. We are fortunate in Australia to produce 65 per cent of our petroleum requirements. We import 35 per cent of requirements from overseas, mainly from the Persian Gulf and particularly from Saudi Arabia. With a mixture of local product and imports it can easily be ascertained that pricing is a complicated matter and requires close surveillance. It means much to the economy of Australia. It is sad to say that we have great problems with delays with tankers coming from overseas and with tankers coming from Bass Strait to the refineries on the eastern seaboard. Every time there is a hold-up caused by a strike in Bass Strait it adds considerably to the cost of petroleum products being delivered to the eastern seaboard or to other parts of Australia.
This Government, by its world parity pricing policy, has made it attractive for overseas companies with the necessary know-how and expertise to provide capital for petroleum exploration in Australia, both on-shore and off-shore. The policy has also attracted locally established companies to explore and, indeed, to develop pockets of known petroleum that hitherto were not economic. We have an instance of this in Bass Strait, where Esso-BHP, owing to our pricing policy, found it economic to tap a lake of fuel in the Kingfisher field. Possibly this field will be in production by 1982 to help us to maintain a stable pricing policy. To do so we must keep up our oil exploration policy. This is extremely important. It has been good to read recently of possible petroleum finds in other parts of Australia. Energy pricing, in particular the pricing of petroleum products, is of fundamental importance. Realistic pricing will influence consumption of petroleum products and, as I stated previously, will encourage exploration for and development of additional petroleum resources.
Under this legislation the Authority will conduct inquiries and determine whether prices for the supply of petroleum products or other services are justified. As mentioned previously, what a complicated setup it is with all these fields of petroleum demand. The surveillance of the prices will extend only to defined petroleum products and to services related to the production or supply of such products. Companies will be required to notify the Authority of any proposed increases in the prices at which they supply petroleum products or associated services in a particular locality. Of course, provision exists for the Authority to withhold increases or to allow interim price increases pending the outcome of an inquiry. This is,

indeed, a very fair part of the legislation which we are debating here tonight. The Minister has power to direct the Authority on matters to which special consideration is to be given. Under the legislation provision is made for strict confidentiality of certain material. This is an important provision and appears in clauses 26 and 37 of the Bill.
It was interesting to hear from the honourable member for Adelaide (Mr Hurford) that Caltex Oil (Australia) Pty Ltd has made a share offering to the Australian public to take part in the activities of that company in Australia. This is good news. The costs of the distribution of petroleum by companies are related to their refineries, to the location of their seaboard terminals and to their overall costs. Bulk installations and methods of distribution playa very important part in the costing of petroleum products. Those companies that have come into the petroleum market in this country in recent years are at a disadvantage compared with those that have been set up here for many years. With the increase in costs they cannot be expected to compete with major companies that have been established for many years. This must give them great problems. It must also give the Authority problems in establishing a fair price setup.
I will be as brief as possible, but it might be just as well to refer to some of the main provisions of the Bill. Clause 5 establishes the Petroleum Products Pricing Authority, which will be headed by the Commissioner for Petroleum Products Pricing which is a new position. This person is to be appointed by the Governor-General and may be the person who holds the office of full time member of the Temporary Assistance Authority. The appointment will be for a period of five years. Clause 11 allows the Minister to appoint one or more associate commissioners for particular inquiries. It must be understood that such appointments are necessary because a single commissioners would need help. Provision is made for the disclosure of financial interests, and for the resignation or termination of appointments of the Commissioner and associate commissioners. The Commissioner is empowered to convene meetings of the Authority at such times and places as he thinks necessary. Certain other provisions relate to the holding of meetings.
Clause 17, which is another important clause, empowers the Minister to require the Authority to conduct an inquiry, specifying the product and/or company to be investigated. In other than a prices justification inquiry, the Minister may also give directions for the conduct of an inquiry. He may specify a time limit for inquiries and he

Petroleum Products Pricing Bill

4 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 3207

may also direct the Authority to give special consideration to a specific matter or matters. I believe this is an adequate Bill. The honourable member for Adelaide (Mr Hurford) who led for the Opposition, was of the opinion that the Bill was not adequate. I think it covers every facet of the industry and 'in our opinion' it is an adequate Bill and has our full support.
