Mr HURFORD (Adelaide) (11.33)-by leave-I move:
Clause 3, page 2, lines 33-35, omit paragraph (c) of the definition of 'petroleum product'.
Clause 4, page 3, line 4, at the end of sub-clause (1) add or by.'a trading entity in which a company has a beneficial financial interest'.
Clause 12, page 6, lines 9 and 10, omit 'being an interest that could conflict with the proper performance of his functions in relation to that inquiry,'.
Clause 12, page 6, lines 14 and 15, omit 'unless his interest is disclosed in any report in which he participates', substitute 'being an interest that could conflict with the proper performance of his functions in relation to this Act'.
Clause 16, page 8, line 12, omit " with the approval of the Minister,'.
Clause 16, page 8, lines 17-21, omit sub-clause (5), substitute the following sub-clauses:
'(5) In performance of its functions, the Authority shall have regard not only to the need for the company or companies concerned to achieve a level of profitability that is sufficient to enable the company or companies to maintain an adequate level of investment and employment but also to the desire of the Government of the Commonwealth, in pursuing the general objectives of national economic and social policy, to implement an equitable antiinflation policy, and in particular, the desire of the Government of the Commonwealth to subject prices to public scrutiny so as to ensure-
(a) that companies are in a position of public accountability which parallels that of unions' requirements to justify their members' wage levels before the Australian Conciliation and Arbitration Commission; and
(b) that unreasonably high profits are not being made at the expense of consumers and that unnecessarily

high costs, either as the result of inefficiency or other unjustified reasons, are not being passed into prices. (6) In sub-section (5)"unnecessarily high costs" includes such items as costs arising from(a) excessive transfer prices between related
companies; (b) excessive royalty payments and the like to
overseas parent companies; (c) exceptional wages and salary increases; (d) unnecessarily high interest payments and rents; (e) unnecessary advertising and packaging
expenses; (f) inefficient operations, and (g) the refusal to make proper allowances for pro-
ductivity improvements; and 'unreasonably high profits' includes levels of profitability, resulting from the abuse of a strong market position, that are more than sufficient to enable the company or companies to maintain an adequate level of investment and employment. '.
Clause 20, page 14, line 19, omit "$10,000", substitute '$100,000' .
I thank those who gave me leave, but I would have been surprised if they had not done so. In fact, I am doing the Government a good turn by moving the amendments in this way rather than moving each of the eight amendments separately. I wish that we had here the Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs (Mr Moore), who is responsible for the Bill. I made the eight amendments available to his office some hours ago and I hoped that in some cases he could have accepted one or two of them. That would have meant that we would have had to move them separately. However, it means that they are given some attention, and the next time a similar Bill is before the chamber I hope it will be amended in the way I have suggested.
Before dealing with the amendments, I point out that generaly they have two features. One is to make the Petroleum Products Pricing Authority independent of the Minister. There are so many cases in this Bill where the Minister's discretion is involved, and without independence, the Authority will have no power but will be merely a cipher of the Government. The Minister, of course, is a sycophant of the oil industry, and the people's interests would be neglected. The first amendment relates to clause 3. The power provided in this clause for the Minister to exempt any product for the purposes of the Act is one of the many discretionary powers conferred on the Minister. That would be removed by this amendment. The second amendment relates to clause 4. It is important that no scope is available for the purposes of the Act to be hindered by the use of noncorporate entities such as trusts or partnerships. Our amendment would mean that such noncorporate entities, where they were subsidiary to a company, would have the same effect as if they

Of course, the Government needs to monitor to a degree that decision making if it is not in the interests of the motorists of Australia. We are dealing with a very precious commodity.
Dr THEOPHANOUS (Burke) (11.42)~I commend the amendments which have been moved by the Opposition to the Petroleum Products Pricing Bill. I commend the Bill to honourable members opposite, the Committee, and especially those who live in country areas. Unless these amendments are carried the Petroleum Products Pricing Authority, which is proposed to be established in place of the Prices Justification Tribunal to exercise control over the price of petrol and other oil products, will not be able to exercise proper control. One can see this if one looks at the main and central clause of the Bill-clause 18-which reads:
The Minister may, by notice in writing under his hand delivered to the Commissioner, direct the Authority to give special consideration, in exercising its functions and powers under this Act, to the matter or matters specified in the notice and the Authority shall comply with any such direction.
This clause is so broad that it means that all the other factors which prices justification tribunals are normally required to take into account need not be taken into account if the Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs so directs. We have a situation where under this clause, for example, if the Authority makes a judgment that in the opinion of the Authority the profitability of the oil companies has been taken into account but in the opinion of the Minister the Authority should have made a determination for much higher profitability for those oil companies, then the Minister is able to direct the oil companies to accept his view and take notice of his opinion to such a degree that the Authority would have to overturn its original view. Under those circumstances, what kind of protection will be given to the motorists of Australia? It gives the total discretion, the total power, to the Minister. If the Authority does not take notice of the Minister's message it will be contravening the Act itself.
In what way then will we have an independent tribunal? In what way will we have a tribunal that is supposed to take into account the various factors which are involved in the complex task of determining the price of petrol? These parts of the Bill are complex. It is not just a matter of determining what is in the interests of the profitability of oil companies. It is also a matter of determining what is in the public interest and, in particular, of determining whether, for example,

an analysis has been made as to whether those kinds of prices are unnecessarily burdening the community in this day and age. The Authority needs also to take into account other factors such as the distribution of petrol and petrol pricing. The honourable member for Franklin (Mr Good luck) mentioned this point in his remarks. 1 notice, however, that he did not vote for the Opposition's overall amendment which was moved to the second reading and which would have guaranteed and protected the point which he made earlier.
We recommend to honourable members opposite the amendments we have moved. They are not radical or outrageous. They are there to protect the consumer. We call upon honourable members opposite to support them if they are genuinely concerned to ensure that petrol pricing is maintained at a reasonable level. We are aware of the reasons that this Bill has been introduced. It has been introduced because of the fact that the Government wanted to wipe out price control in virtually every other major area of the economy. The Government is aware that there are certain members of the oil industry, especially those who belong to the National Country Party, who would be very upset if a decontrolling of the price of petrol were introduced because this would affect people living in remote country areas.
Therefore, the Government has agreed to replace the Prices Justification Tribunal with an authority concerned with a very limited task regarding the price of petrol. This suggests that somehow oil companies are the only bodies that need to be controlled in the matter of pricing and in the matter of protecting the consumer against conspiracy. 1 am referring to the conspiracy of the oil companies and other companies in presenting their cases to such an authority. People who have worked on the PJT have told me about the fact that companies do engage in conspiracy in terms of their representations to the Tribunal. There is no protection against this sort of thing in the present law. We commend the Opposition's amendments. They will protect the consumer and we ask that all honourable members support them.
Mr WILSON (Sturt--Minister for Home Affairs and Environment) (11.48)-1 wish to take a few minutes to respond to the points made by the honourable member for Adelaide (Mr Hurford). I realise that it is late but 1 think when amendments are put forward it is appropriate that there be a response, albeit brief. With regard to the first amendment, petroleum products cover a very wide range of refmery goods and it is not possible to delineate them in the Schedule to the

3210 REPRESENTATIVES 4 June 1981

PetroleulII Products Pricing Bill

Petroleum Products Pricing Bill. In the normal course of events the Petroleum Products Pricing Authority will deal with those products of significance to the community and the economy as, in fact, the Prices Justification Tribunal does now. The existing provision allows a desirable flexibility in the event that for particular reasons it becomes necessary to specify additional products outside the jurisdiction of the Authority.
With regard to the second amendment proposed by the honourable member for Adelaide, I inform the Committee that it is not acceptable to the Government. The additional words are not necessary. I draw attention to the fact that provision exists in clause 4 (3) for the Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs to declare any other companies which might become involved in the refining or in the production of petroleum products. In the meantime, the relevant companies will be covered by the Act. As the honourable member for Adelaide would know the Commonwealth has limited powers over the non-corporate sector. This Bill, therefore, focuses its attention on the corporate sector.
The third amendment, like the first two, is not acceptable to the Government. It goes further than the self-discipline concept of the Bowen report and would put an unreasonable stricture upon an associate commissioner who, by definition, would only be appointed from outside the Authority for particular inquiries. The existing words are consistent with other legislation. The fourth amendment is not acceptable as the existing words provide for proper disclosure of any relevant interest and they are consistent with other legislation. With regard to the fifth amendment, it is the policy and intention that the authority will conduct inquiries only with the approval of the Minister, as is the case with inquiries presently carried out by the PJT. The authority will in no other way be constrained in its normal price monitoring functions.
The sixth amendment is not acceptable to the Government as the Authority is not an appropriate body for reporting on broad economic and social policy issues. These functions rest with other bodies and with the Government directly. The Authority is concerned with the petroleum products only and will have the powers necessary to determine whether prices are justified on the basis of reasonable costs. In relation to the seventh amendment, the policy intention is that the Minister will have a power of direction to ensure that the Authority has regard to the particular matters where necessary in the carrying out of functions and powers.

Dr Theopillmous-Why should you?
Mr WILSON-The honourable member for Burke asks why he should. The justification is that a similar provision exists in the Industries Assistance Commission and the Prices Justification Tribunal legislation. In relation to the eighth amendment I point out that the existing penalty is consistent with penalties for similar breaches. There is 110 demonstrated need for a higher penalty. In conclusion I make reference to the points raised by the honourable member for Franklin (Mr Good luck). I am sure that he will watch this Authority carefully, as he said he would. If he is concerned, I am absolutely certain that we will hear from him in the House.
Amendments negatived.
Bill agreed to.
Bill reported without amendment; report adopted.

MI'SCHOLES (Corio) (1 1. 54)-The Opposition does not oppose this Bill. However we believe that the distribution of funds and functions which should properly be the responsibility of a local government body should be declared and, where it is practicable, inserted in the Bill. It is our intention to move an amendment to the motion for the second reading which indicates our attitude to the manner of decision making on the distribution of funds from these bodies to the community and welfare funds. It is also our intention to move an amendment in the Committee stage relating to the makeup of the Australian Capital Territory Gaming and Liquor Authority. In the first instance it has not been possible for us to draft amendments to the Bill which would have been practical in order to meet what we see as being a desirable end. Therefore, we believe that our objective can be salisfactorily achieved only by the Bill being redrafted. I move:

the Bill be withdrawn and re-drafted to provide that the dispersal of funds raised by the Authority. other than those committed to the racing industry, will be in accordance with determinations of the Australian Capital Territory

4 and 5 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 3211

House of Assembly but limited to purposes associated with sport, recreation, culture, welfare and. community related activities in the Australian Capital Territory.
The purpose of the amendment is to make it quite clear that the distribution of those funds which are paid to the community welfare fund-an amount of about $2m a year in the Australian Capital Territory-are allocated in accordance with decisions which are made by the people who are elected by the residents of the Territory to carry out those functions which would normally be associated with local government and, to a limited extent, a State government or a territorial government. At this stage, given the nature of legislation which controls the making of laws in the Australian Capital Territory, the amendment would have to be made by ordinance which we believe should be disallowed only by this Parliament and not subject to alteration by the Minister for the Capital Territory.
It is appropriate, I think, that funds in an area should be allocated by the people closest to the needs and concerns of that area-the elected representatives. They should make the decisions. They are the elected members and the people of the Territory have the opportunity to deal with them if they do not properly do the job that is expected of them. The people of the Australian Capital Territory have no opportunity of affecting the numbers in this place except on a fairly marginal basis-
Mr Ruddock-They are supposed to be getting self-government.
Mr SCHOLES-That matter has been up several times tonight. The honourable gentleman does himself no service by parroting that phrase. Self-government is an obligation in a democracy; it is not something one can opt out of. There is only one other place in Australia which is treated in the same way as the Australian Capital Territory is treated and that is the City of Melbourne where the council has been dismissed because it would not treat certain developers and large business organisations well in giving them building permits. The organisations are very substantial donors to a party with which the honourble member has some association. The council has now been replaced so that the centre of the city can be raped. Mr Hodgman-What do you mean by that? Mr SCHOLES-It is not necessary. The power already exists for any government that wishes to take such action to do anything it so desires outside the Parliamentary Triangle. It is an area where one person, the Minister is responsible

to a government charged with national responsibilities and not seriously concerned with these matters, makes decisions on behalf of, in a completely paternal manner, a substantive section of the Australian community. The Department of the Capital Territory is not elected for or answerable to the community.
The debate relating to this Bill is not appropriate for a discussion of the constitutional requirements of the Australian Capital Territory. The Government has chosen that the Australian Capital Territory will not have self-government on the basis of a referendum. We will not discuss the merits or otherwise of that result. The Minister for the Capital Territory (Mr Hodgman) said earlier that he is prepared to go some way towards self-government provided a formula can be arrived at. If the functions within the Minister's responsibility were taken away from the responsible governments in any of our electorates or States, there would be an uproar from honourable members who sit opposite. It would interfere with the rights of those people who govern those areas. Those rights are denied in the Australian Capital Territory. By moving this amendment we seek to give those rights in respect of the distribution of funds which come from taxes levied on gambling and racing in this area to the people of this Territory. It is a right to which they are entitled, and it is an obligation that they are entitled to be expected to undertake on their own behalf.

Friday, 5 June 1981 Mr FRY (Fraser) (12.1 a.m.)-I support the amendment moved by the honourable member for Corio (Mr Scholes). The Australian Capital Territory Gaming and Liquor Authority Bill is acceptable. It is one of the few things in the razor gang recommendations for Canberra with which I found I could agree. It amalgamates a number of authorities which is a sensible thing to do. It should work out in theory and I hope it works out in practice. The way in which this Bill has been drawn up provides a need for the amendment which the honourable member for Corio has moved. The need for that amendment reflects the general contempt which the Government has for the views of the locally elected representatives of the people in Canberra.
The Minister for the Capital Territory (Mr Hodgman) said, in his second reading speech, that he proposes that the Authority should include a member of the Australian Capital Territory House of Assembly and a legal practitioner. I do not think that is good enough. I think provisions

should be written into the Bill in the terms that have been suggested by the honourable member for Corio to ensure that there is adequate representation from locally elected people. Of course it is terribly important that the locally elected Assembly should make the decisions about where the money is disbursed. They are familiar with the needs of the community. They are the people who should make the judgments. They do it conscientiously. Last year they spent a lot of time considering how the money should be allotted. Then we had the disgusting situation of the Minister quite arbitrarily overruling the recommendations of a committee of locally elected people who know what are the needs of the community.
Last year the former Minister for the Capital Territory, Mr Ellicott, quite arbitrarily reduced the sums recommended by the Assembly and awarded significant amounts to other organisations which were not recommended by the Assembly. Recommendations were introduced for organisations such as the HandicaI?ped Citizens Association, the Canberra Commumty Legal Service, the Asthma Association, the Epilepsy Association and the Specific Learning Difficulties Association. These were organisations seen as having real needs in the community. The Mini.ster saw fit arbitrarily to reduce the recommendatIOns by the locally elected people and, in their place, he gave significant sums, for example, to the Duke of Edinburgh Awards Scheme and the Canberra Horticultural Society, which were not included in the recommendations. These are laudable organisations and I do not begrudge them getting some money, but I resent the Minister ma~ing arbitrary decisions and reducing recommendatIOns made by locally elected people.
The fact that we are here after midnight debating a Bill concerning domestic matters in the A~s tralian Capital Territory points up the absurdIty of the arrangement that this Government has for the administration of Canberra. People from all over Australia are sitting here after midnight considering legislation which sets out the way in which the people of Canberra may disburse funds raised within their own jurisdiction from poker machines, from the Totalisator Agency Board and from the administration of liquor licensing laws. that they have raised? We do not need hill-bi11ie~ from Hobart telling us how to disburse our funds. We can do it ourselves. This debate points up the absurdity of the arrangements in this city.

We do not need other people to tell us. We need to accept that responsibility and, if we are given the opportunity, we will.
I hope the Minister will consider these representations. I hope he will see that the locally elected people are represented on these organisations and that their voices are listened to. We do not want just token representation; we want real representation by people who are elected in Canberra, who have the right of recall if they do not perform as well as we think they should. The people of Canberra do not have this representation now. The Minister can do what he likes and make all sorts of arbitrary decisions and the people of Canberra can do nothing about it. I repeat: How would the people of Hobart feel if we made those sorts of decisions for them?

Mr HODGMAN (Denison-Minister for the Capital Territory) (12.15 a.m.)-I thank the honourable member for Corio (Mr Scholes) for the spirit in which he has moved his amendment, but it is not acceptable. The first matter of concern to me and the Government is that if the honourable member thought about it he would realise there are very strong grounds for objecting to an authority constituting an even number of members. To create an authority of six members is to invite the situation of a 3-3 vote, with all the problems that that places upon the chairman of a small body. The Government concluded that five was the appropriate number. Whilst I recognise the spirit of the honourable member's amendment to give increased representation to the House of Assembly, without any disrespect to the House of Assembly, I draw to the honourable member's attention that we are amalgamating into one authority the Totalisator Agency Board, the Poker Machine Licensing Board and the Liquor Licensing Board. We have had representations from groups in the Australian Capital Territory wanting to be included on the Australian Capital Territory Gaming and Liquor Authority. I mention some of them: Galloping, trotting and greyhound interests, licensed clubs, retailers of alcohol, wholesalers, poker machine licensees, consumers and a miscellany of government officials who also

want to be seated on the Authority. The Government took the view-I think it is a proper viewthat it should maintain the one person representation from the House of Assembly which currently exists on the three boards. I do not say that the House of Assembly does not want more representation, but I do say it has not asked for it. I appreciate the spirit of the honourable member's amendment, but I am not prepared to acceptnor is the Government-that of a board of five, two should be members of the House of Assembly.
In response to the first point raised by the honourable member for Corio in respect of his earlier amendment, which is in some way related to the amendment before the Committeeindeed, the honourable member for Fraser (Mr Fry) spoke to this amendment when we were dealing with the first amendment-I suggest that one could look through the Bil1150 times without finding anything about the disbursal of funds. It is not a question of 10 pages of amendments; it just could not be done.
Last but not least, in relation to the question of self-government I am sorry to have to inform the honourable member, because apparently he is not aware ofit, that I met with the House of Assembly within one week of my appointment as Minister. I told it-it was publicly reported-that I welcomed its suggestions about moving towards self government. I would be delighted if the people of Canberra would take up some of the responsibilities which flow over my desk every day and accept for themselves the responsibility to do things which I am convinced the most highly educated, articulate and intelligent community in Australia is quite capable of doing for itself. The House of Assembly has less legislative or governmental power than the National Aboriginal Conference. I simply draw to the attention of all honourable members that I welcome thoughtful and wellprepared submissions on self-government. Unfortunately, I have heard nothing of substance except from a citizen's group being led by Mrs Craven, and certainly nothing from the House of Assembly.
Amendment negatived.

The amendment to clause 5 will ensure that persons who, because of their knowledge of and expertise in the area of the Australian Capital Territory liquor trade, the value of whose contributions to a body such as the Australian Capital Territory Gaming and Liquor Authority cannot be underestimated, are not excluded from being eligible for appointment to the Authority. However, the amendment to clause 11 will ensure that such a person, if appointed, is required to disclose any direct or indirect pecuniary interests he or she might have in a matter being considered by the Authority and to absent himself or herself from any deliberations on that matter by the Authority. The amendment to clause 35 will omit those provisions which purported to give the Australian Capital Territory Registrar of Titles power to impose obligations and liabilities on the Totalisator Agency Board and the Poker Machine Licensing Board when in fact those boards cease to exist once the Act is proclaimed. The amendments are drafting and formal ones. I have discussed them with the Opposition. I understand there is no objection. Accordingly, I commend the amendments to the Committee.

AUSTRALIAN NATIONAL UNIVERSITY AMENDMENT BILL 1981

Second Reading

Debate resumed from 28 May, on motion by MrFife:

That the Bill be now read a second time.

Mr DAWKINS (Fremantle) (12.24 a.m.)-It is almost exactly 12 hours since I last addressed the House and in almost exactly another 12 hours I will be addressing it again. I am beginning to feel a little bit like a chiming clock. Because the hour is late I do not intend to delay the House. However, there are a few things which one should say on the Australian National University Amendment Bill. The purpose of this Bill is to amend the Australian National University Act as it relates to the activities of student organisations on campus. This is the second attempt that the Government has made to stifle student organisation at the Australian National University. This attempt demonstrates two very endearing qualities about this Government. Firstly, this Government is not only silly but it is persistently silly; and, secondly, it has an almost infinite capacity to bungle its enactments. In 1979 the Government introduced a Bill to amend the Australian National University Act, which was described by the Minister at the time in a passage of Hansard which I seek leave to incorporate in Hansard. Leave granted.

of the provision of amenities or services that are not of an academic nature, that are of direct benefit to the University, and that have been declared in university statutes to be amenities or services in respect of that organisation; compulsory fees moneys are not to be paid to a national organisation which represents students but they may be paid to a national student organisation whose principal function has to do with sporting or recreational activities, or with a particular education, social or cultural field, or with the interests of post-graduate students; financial statements are to be prepared and audited each year showing details of the manner in which compulsory fees have been expended.
Mr DAWKINS-The main target of the provision was the ANU Students Association and that explanation, which I will not read, was a long winded way of saying that membership of the Association was to be voluntary and that funds collected via the compulsory services and amenities fee were not to be used for political activities and most importantly these funds were not to be expended on the affiliation to the Australian Union of Students. Regrettably for the Government, that law did not work. The main problem was that the ANU Students Association had other forms of income which the Australian National University Council, following legal advice, decided was not subject to the constraints imposed by the 1979 amendment.
Following the Council's May 1981 meeting, the Association transmitted to AUS its affiliation fee from funds it had derived from sources other than the compulsory fee. Just before the May meeting the Minister for Education (Mr Fife) wrote to the Council advising it to decide otherwise and threatened that this legislation would follow if it did not. Retribution was swift and that is why we are now debating this legislation, the purpose of which is to attempt to ensure that all the income that the Association receives, however derived, will be subject to the same constraints as the income from fees. The honourable member for Dundas (Mr Ruddock) has alerted me to one point and that is, however the income is derived by application of the income from the fees, the income will be subject to the same restrictions as those imposed by the 1979 amendment.
This Bill is necessary only because the Government bungled its earlier attempts, and one \Yonders whether this attempt will be any more successful. As I said before, this amendment is also silly. It reveals that this Government is still iinprisoned by the Australian Student Liberal Federation. The members of this Government are hostages of members of the kindergarten Liberal Party who, like mollycoddled children, have forced their big brothers and sisters in the Government to achieve the purposes which they have so patently failed to achieve through the

democratic processes of the ANU Students Association. The ANU Students Association is a very democratic association and makes its decisions by way of regular general meetings. The Association has decided that it wants to remain affiliated to the Australian Union of Students and continues to reaffirm that decision. The kindly Liberals have never been able to get a majority of students to agree with them that the Association should leave AUS. One would think that' this Government and this horse-tiding, fast-cardriving Prime Minister would have more important things to do than be brow beaten by a pack of spoiled brats. But then, as Senator Chipp has said, he is really only a kid at heart and cannot resist playing little games like dropping ice cubes into people's pockets and then squealing with delight at the reaction of his victims.
But there is a much more serious side to this matter. Firstly, the Association should be able to make its own decisions about how it spends its funds. After all, it is not compulsory for students to belong, even though it is compulsory for students to pay the services and amenities fee. The Council of the University decides what proportion of income from that fee will go to the Association. In my very firm view it should be up to the democratic processes of the Association to decide how that fee income is to be applied. In support of that argument I want to quote briefly from a letter dated 30 April 1979 from Sir John Crawford, the Chancellor of the Australian National University. He says:
The Council has long believed that in a modern university it is very desirable that, so far as it is at all possible, students themselves should manage their own student organisations. Whether the ANU Students' Association should, in the ordinary manner, be affiliated to the AUS or not is, in the Council's view, for the ANU's students themselves to decide. Referenda on this question have lately been held on other campuses.
At ANU simple procedures exist to determine the question.
I could not agree more' with Sir John Crawford on that matter. It should be up to the democratic processes of the ANU Students Association to work out the way in Which the funds which they receive are applied. It is not up to governments. It is not even up to university councils to use draconian methods to determine or constrain the way in which those funds will be applied.
It is a fact that the statutes which set up all the universities throughout Australia provide for students' associations in some form or another. They exist to organise the student body and to represent it to the university, the Government and the community. All these associations obtain funds from fees which are paid by students. In most cases they are part of compulsory fees. It is

Australian National University Bill

4 and 5 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 3217

my strong opinion that these separate associations need to have a national organisation to encourage debate on educational and other issues and, importantly, to represent the collective student view to the Government. After all, the Commonwealth is now responsible for the almost total funding of tertiary education. The students need to have a say before government as government makes decisions about important matters. The Commonwealth also makes decisions about the level of assistance which is provided to students going through universities and colleges. Again, it is important that there be a national organisation to represent the views of students on these important matters.
It is perfectly legitimate for student bodies on all the campuses to affiliate with AUS and to pay fees to it in order to support its activities. It is not only important it is also vital that they do so. A well run and well representative AUS is not only good for students in Australia but it is also good for Australia. The contribution of AUS to the education debate in the late 1960s and early 1970s was invaluable. It led to a number of changes of policy which came about at the time of the election of a Labor government. The AUS or its predecessor has been responsible for a number of important initiatives. I mention just one, the Aboriginal scholarship scheme, which AUS introduced at a time when there was little or no support for Aboriginal students. Consequently, very few Aboriginal students were involved even in secondary education, let alone tertiary education. It has to be remembered that the Abschol scheme became redundant only when an alternative scheme was instituted by the then Minister, W. C. Wentworth. As a result the need for Abschol was virtually removed. The AUS has done great work. It is now emerging once more as a powerful force in Australia, especially in the education debate.
It is not surprising that this Government does not like the AUS. It is not surprising that the Government's juvenile foot soldiers in the Liberal Students Federation do not like it either. As long as this Government and the Liberal Party are wedded to an education policy which is based on privilege the AUS will always criticise them and their policy. The Government must realise that it is always inconvenient to have critics but in a country which is supposed to be a democracy criticism is an essential part of the democratic process. There is an even more obnoxious aspect to this legislation and the way in which the Government has pursued its mindless obsession

with the AUS. It concerns the way the Government has stood over the University in a most grotesque way. The Government refused to discuss the amendments to the Australian National University Act in 1979. It has refused to discuss these amendments in 1981. It is true that there has been some communication between the University and the Government but it has always been on the basis that unless the University accepts the Government's view the Government wi11legislate to ensure that it does not have any choice. It is intriguing in this context to point out a reference by Sir John Crawford in his letter of April 1979. He said:
May I add that, as you will know, it has nowhere proved easy to legislate satisfactorily on these issues. Council has accordingly stated that it will review within two years the operation of its Statute. It believes that this is the better way to proceed at this time rather than by amendment of the principal Act by way of an addition to section 29.
Sir John Crawford was indeed prophetic when he made that remark. Here we are less than two years later amending the bungled legislation which the Government introduced against the wishes of the University in 1979. The Government was simply concerned to stand over the University. This constitutes a very grave interference in its affairs. It goes further than that. The Australian National University Act provides that the Prime Minister and the Leader of the Opposition should each nominate two members of the Parliament to the Council. They are not there as representatives of the Government and the Opposition but as representatives of the Australian community to act as they see fit as representatives of the Australian community on the governing body of Australia's national university. One of the Prime Minister's representatives is the honourable member for Dundas. He has behaved in a totally unprincipled way in his membership of the Council. He has used his position simply to be a toady for the Government. He has threatened the Council that unless it follows the Government's directives or the intentions of its legislation dire consequences will follow. At the May meeting he said that by not agreeing with the Government the University had earned itself a bad reputation.
Mr Ruddock-Where did .you get that information?
Mr DAWKINSIt is contained in a forthcoming article in Woroni which I am sure the honourable member will be pleased to read.
Mr Ruddock-l would be interested to know who the author is.

Mr SPEAKER-The honourable member for Fremantle should continue. The honourable member for Dundas should remain silent.
Mr DAWKINS-He will have his opportunity to speak. So much for his views on academic freedom and the autonomy of universities. He was not content with that and went further. He said that serious consequences would follow if the University did not comply with the Government's directives. He pointed out that the University was seeking co-operation in respect of, amongst other things, superannuation supplementation. This was an outrageous abuse of the honourable member's position on the Council. It led the Chancellor of the University to describe his threats as subtle blackmail. It is quite unprinCipled for the honourable member for Dund~s to behave in that way as a representative of the Parliament and the Australian people on the Council of the Australian National University. It typifies the arrogant approach which this Government has taken in trying to control student organisations at the ANU and, for that matter, at the Canberra College of Advanced Education. It is for that reason that the Opposition strenuously opposes this legislation. It should not proceed; it should be defeated.

We became aware when reading the accounts of the students' association that certain moneys were being paid to subsidise certain legitimate student activities. But it was claimed that revenue earned from those activities was being set aside by the students as though it was revenue of their own that they had earned by legitimate activities. I think that it is fair to the House that I, as one of the Parliament's representatives on the Council of the Australian National University, should indicate the nature of the deception involved. Deception is exactly what it was. I had no hesitation in telling my colleagues on the Council that that is what I though it to be. I have no shame at all in having done that. I have no shame in responsibly reporting to that body my view of the determination of my colleagues to ensure that the intention of the Government, expressed in statements in this place and expressed in an Act of Parliament, be carried into effect. I certainly told the Council of that determination. That view was already before the Council because it had the letter from the Minister for Education (Mr Fife) before it. I think that I have a responsibility not only to tell the Parliament of views of the University, as I do from time to time, and my colleagues in our party councils, from which I was appointed and the views the people within the University hold, but also to tell those on the Council of the attitudes of the Government. Council members are free to vote as they see fit, as they did on that occasion.

Mr RUDDOCK (Dundas) (12.38 a.m.)-The Australian National University Amendment Bill is to put beyond doubt certain questions that have been raised in relation to the operation of the Australian National University Act as it applies to the funding of student organisations at the University. When the legislation to amend the ANU Act was introduced, I and numbers of my colleagues were certainly of the view that the funds made available to the student organisations were to be properly applied to the activities that the amendments to the Act envisaged the students' assoCiation might carryon. A question was raised at that time as to whether there was any possibility of funds made available for a particular purpose being laundered by the students' association in such a way as to relieve them of their speCial character as funds that could not be remitted to the Australian Union of Students and for that body to obtain them by subterfuge. The fear that some of us had at that time but which we were assured that the Act would deal with was realised when the University Council and the students' assoCiation sought legal advice.

I will explain the nature of the deception involved in this exerCise, A place called Childers Street Hall received a subsidy of some $1,031, yet revenue of $635 was claimed to be earned from that hall. In other words, the subsidy was greatly in excess of the revenue. Lennox House, for its operations, received $6,539 in subsidies, and the revenue derived was $1,905. In Orientation Week there was an element of revenue earned from activities as well as a subsidy. A Rank Xerox photostating machine was subsidised by some $2,198 and students were charged $963 for using the machine for copying. Telephone accounts came to $5,710 but $710 was collected from the students using the phone. The magazine was subsidised to the extent of $14,802. It received advertisements to the value of $2,162. Very little money was not the subject of a direct subsidy from the general services fee. In my view all of the revenue had that character. The University in looking at thatwhether it was legally right or not I regard as irrelevant-morally was involved in a deception in approving, as it did by resolution, of students taking that subsidy and claiming that they had

University from engaging in political activities reAs mentioned previously, the Government has

3220 REPRESENTATIVES 4 and 5 June 1981

Australian National University Bill

action because it is not prepared to tolerate student political action. It is part of the democratic process that groups in the community . should be able to organise and make represenlations to the Government. The plain fact is that the student unions have, over the years, become autonomous bodies which exist to provide services, representation and resources for the benefit of the student body. In carrying out such functions they are of necessity involved in political activities. In this sense they are analagous to trade unions. It is this similarity which draws unto them - the hate of Government members.
The Federal Government, by restricting the means by which the student body and the Australian National University can gain essential funding for such organising purposes, is denying a basic democratic right of our society to the students. The truth of the matter is that the Federal Government fears the powers of the student body to bring home to the Australian people the danger of their living standards which the present educational policy of the Government represents. Restrictions in the Tertiary Education Assistance System allowance, the introduction of fees for second and post-graduate degrees, the forced amalgamation and closures of colleges of advanced education and the cut in the facilities of the Australian Schools Commission are all reductions in educational services which will greatly restrict the ,opportunities of Australians to improve their educational skills for the benefit of Australians as a whole. It is not too late for the Federal Government to reverse this trend.
Mr Bourchier-Fair go.
Mr MILTON-I have nearly finished. Be patient. It is not too late for the Federal Government to move away from its confrontationist attitude to students in tertiary educational institutions. I. say just one thing before I close my remarks. Listening to the honourable member for Dundas (Mr Ruddock) confirmed the impression which I gained when I was a member of the Council of the University of Melbourne that the nominees of Liberal governments on university councils are there just to act as stooges for the Government. They have no true understanding of university traditions and no sympathy for the students. I believe this Government should withdraw this iniquitous Bill and accept that the fundamental right of the student bodies is to engage in political activities and that it is a normal, necessary ingredient of tertiary education.
Mr BEAZLEY (Swan) (12.52 a.m.)-Student unions have over the years played a constructive role in Australian political debate. The Australian

Union of Students is included among them. In the major political issues in which Australia and its predecessors have engaged in recent years-the question of the Vietnam war and conscription and the needs of education-their views have been justified by experience. The political positions adopted by those organisations were at variance with the views of honourable members opposite. Quite unimpeachably the student unions and Australian sponsored campaigns were supported by the bulk of their members, as they continue to be supported. Those campaigns were in the direct interests of and of direct concern to those members. It never pays one to be right in politics; it only pays to win. If one is right and loses one is doubly damned. One's opponents' efforts to exculpate their own moral inferiority will make them doubly vicious. Behind the pompous asininities of honourable members opposite about compulsory unionism and the importance of prohibiting the use of service funds for political purposes of direct interest to students lies a steely determination to render the organisations ineffective.
Like the Australian Broadcasting Commission cuts, like the cuts in expenditure in the area of Aboriginal affairs, in this issue there is a liberal dosage of naked and unloved revenge. It is not surprising that the Government has trouble makeing its legislation stick. When one gets down to it, it is impossible to implement unless one puts an officer of the Auditor-General's Department in every student union office. One gets to the obvious situation where there are buildings provided by amenity funds in which political meetings for AUS purposes or other purposes are held. Are the students to be charged every time they walk into those buildings for an AUS meeting? There are typing services available in the student union organisations and printing machines. They are useful for handing out notices that are related to amenities. Will an officer of the Auditor General's Department determine when it is an amenity-related notice or an AUS-related notice. It is nonsense. This piece of legislation, like the previous piece of legislation, will not resolve that problem. The Liberal Party will keep poking a stick at it. The great Liberal Government will offend every liberal principle on academic freedom when that suits its purpose, but this ought not to cause us to be surprised as there is no other liberal principle that the Liberal Party is not prepared to jettison when it so suits it.
This Government in its various metamorphoses over the last couple of decades has been extraordinarily hard on the younger generation. It has rendered them jobless, restricted their access to educational possibilities and marched them off to

Australian National University Bill

4 and 5 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 3221

fight in disastrous wars. This Bill is merely a minor part of a general trend of youth bashing by a government whose leading members were born mummified. It is not surprising, therefore, that about 66 per cent of the young population in the last election voted against this Government. They will continue to do so. At the next election they will defeat the Government. The people will be convinced that they ought to defeat the Government because of the stupid sorts of actions that the Government has undertaken.
Bill agreed to. Bill reported without amendment; report adopted.

Third Reading Bill (on motion by Mr Fife)-by leave-read a third time.
